THE RED PAGE.
"In Such a Night!”
—Merchant of Venice, v., 1.
Thou hast tiik most unsavoury similes. 1 Hen •
IF., i., 2.
IT has been lovely mosquito weather lately; and
when the dog who lives next door to aHE
i Bulletin office has lifted up his head and
howled he has stimulated the dog opposite,
and;the dog in the back-lane, and the dog in the
fish-jshop round the corner; and the moon has
occasionally had to hide her face as she did m
Mr. Moore’s verses, whan the lord of the valley
and Eveleen were commemorating vows regarded
by the local Y.W.C.A as highly improper. For
the purposes of the metaphor, a recent complainant
may be considered the next-door dog, who doesn t
look as if he got his tucker regular. Presumably
this is the voice of the fish-shop dog, with plates
to clean, replying cheerfully
I did not need to read Lawson’s suicidal autobiogiaphy
to learn that the writer was specially singled out for
punishment some years before the advent of the First
Germ Cell.
Unluckily—for many reasons—Lawson does not under-
stand why. He goes a Journey by Night in the Dark
Forest, and presently finds his leg between the jaws of a
Trap—and howls. It docs not occur to him to ask who
put the Trap there, and what for. It is a very old Tiap,
and i.t has sharp teeth, and hurts. Anjone can see how
it hurts by reading-Lawson's description of the Journey.
Lawson-on-himself is interesting from the psychological
aspect, but open to criticism when he proceeds as an
authority to deduce from a purely personal experience
axioms for the working out of all future Australian liteiary
problems. All writers understand from private experi-
ence how important the Literary Ego is to itself—but
some learn to believe that this importance is nothing
whatever in comparison with the importance of Litera-
ture. A man’s sense of his own injuries may be keen ,
but he will bear the inflictions more bravely when he
traces them to their correct pathogenic source ; lie may
even get to bear them with as much fortitude as if they
were those of his friends.
• * * *
The Higher Intelligence is nowhere evenly diffused :
there is a limited number of bodk-buyers and readers in
any population ; the population of this country is small
in comparison to that of England (Lawson s Literal y
Paradise) -consequently there is a smaller sale for locally-
published books ; result, a poorer remuneration for local
writers. This is a Fact —one of the Teeth in the Trap.
Other cold, automatio faots indicate that a great deal
not only of Literature, but of Art and Ethics— and Pro-
gress.—is the indirect product of a sense of personal in-
justice—the everlasting grievance against things in
general; but that really does not affect the-quality of the
result, from the general or critical point of view. This
appeal of writers to the personal sympathies of readers
is one of the most reprehensible things in literature. As
if the feelings of the reader are not of far greater bulk
and account than those of the writer!
It would be indecent on the part of the player who,
after suffering much mental and phj'sical injury, has been
cruelly killed off on the stage in the last act, to oome
before the curtain and beg the pity of the audienoe. The
audience went there purposely to see him suffer and die.
The more life-like the suffering and death, the better they
relish it. To paraphrase Lessing— “ Everything stoical is
untheatrioal ; the interest is always commensurate with
the amount of suffering displayed by the object of inter-
est.” And if the Tragedian does not like Tragedy, let
him take to Light Comedy ; his audiences can laugh as
well as weep—it is questionable if they will not draw a
greater amount of pleasure from laughter than from tears.
Tears have a certain consolation of their own; but it is of
a religious character, allied to the feelings of the mariied
man who comes back to a chaste wife after a trip to
Japan ; and, like all these religious joys, it is respectable
but not thoroughly satisfying.
* * * *
This outcry against Circumstances—which ante-date
Poetry—this irritation by Facts whioh are independent
of all Mankind and all Metres, this childlike sobbing for
special consideration, for undivided Love, is interesting
in poets. I have been affected by it myself earlier in life ;
and I know women of forty-particularly single women—
who suffer from a kindred feeling. It is classed by later
medical writers under the heading of hysteria. It is allied
to epilepsy and neurosis. The doctors prescribe bromides,
and sometimes chloral.
Of the Yelp we have had many peculiar examples.
Two instances occur. Poe’s last words —“ It’s all over !
Write, Eddie is no more.” How his voice must have
broken at the “ Eddie! ” Poe wa3 a man infinitely sorry
for himself (witness, “ The Dream within a Dream ’’), and
“ Eddie ” was his pet name! And Keats’ tearful request
to have scribed over him the familiar epitaph : “ Here
lies one whose name was writ in water.” It was an
appeal to the sympathies of the audience. These men
pursued literature, and found it hard—and neither of
them took sufficient physical exercise.
Lawson tells us in his auto-biog. how “ hard graft ”
agreed with him. After four or five months of it he was
“ too healthy to read or write or bother about it.” He
was so healthy that the importance of literary recogni-
tion was as nothing to the importance of frequent “ plum-
duff.” His grievance was transformed from the editor to
the cook. But he was still the Man with a Grievance.
Lawson.thinks he mtisf be unhealthy to write. I hold
that the literary output of unhealthy minds and bodies
is likely itself to be unhealthy; and I have a thousand
arguments to support the view. Lawson s best work will
probably be done when he is a whole man. I will admit
that the Pursuit of Literature in these provinces is hard.
It is perhaps harder in England—the literary Aidenn of
the tired Australian writer ; but it is no harder than the
pursuit of bricklaying, or stoking, or stevedoring, or the
pursuit of a mercantile clerkship at 30s. a week of 84
working hours; and the Pursuer in any of these pro-
fessions receives no press notices whatever.
By the fact that I am not already a millionaire I con-
clude that prices paid for contributions by Australian
journals are not princely ; but this is the fault of acci-
dent ; elsewhere newspapers are run by rajahs for the
sole purpose of enriching the contributors.
I may be considered old-fashioned and Pharisaical in
my ideas, but I contend that the first duty which every
man,'sound in mind and limb, owes to his community is
to make a living; and this to me is the only modern
heroism worth mentioning. And I am not speaking as
one absolutely unacquainted with the difficulties which
Lawson enumerates in his experiences when I assert that
it is easier to live on the continent of Australia, and
to live freely and well, than on any other civilised section
of this planet. <qp>
* * * *
The impecuniosity, er deception, of Labor newspapers
and Workers’ Unions appears to have galled Lawson as
much as anything. I understand that. I edited and
wrote the Australian Workman for some months at an
indifferent salary. My board of directors was composed
of a cabman, a stonemason, a compositor, a general
laborer, and a cabinetmaker; and the manager was a
ship’s steward.
Only good health and a hereditary sense of hungt?
saved me from chronic misanthropy. And this same sefcy
showed me ultimately that the enthusiast was a fellow
who fell in love with a girl in a book when he was young,
and would not have anything to say to the girls down the
street. But when he grew older he saw that he had the
earth, and wanted the moon; that a pink reality was
worth all the impossibilities; and he forsook his “ white
ideal” on the spot.
Lawson grieves that his enthusiasm, and the accidents
which kindled it, are extinguished ; but that has nothing
to do with Australian literature. Lawson no longer seeks
for an earthly Eldorado of Truth and Justice ; but that
has nothing to do with Australian Literature either.
These things are merely incidents in the evolution of an
individual ; and Lawson has no more right to blame the
Country for the imperfection of human affairs, or to ask
the world’s condolence in a quite juvenile bereavement —
the loss of his ideals—than to blame the inclination of
the planetary axis of rotation for the absence of perennial
summer. When ideals are dead they should be decently
buried. They make excellent fertilisers.
The Anglo-Australian literature—yet in swaddling-
clothes—will, like the literature of England, be the re-
sult of hard pursuit and laborious self-denial —and Time.
It will probably be, on the whole, a very cheerful litera-
ture, because Australia is struggling hard to be a cheer-
ful country, in spite of The Bulletin. Carlyle liked this
country, because here a man might have meat for his
labor. Shakespeare would have liked it, only for the in-
different quality of the local beer. Obviously the hale
singer of Stratford wrote on good ale, and his work is
highly impersonal . . . and he left a little less money
than Jay Gould to be divided among his heirs. Yet it is
not recorded that he complained that literature was hard.
Privateering paid belter, as his contemporaries, Francis
Drake and others, proved ; but Shakespeare stuck to
letters. No doubt he growled sometimes ; but he didn’t
let his grievance get into his work. He did not even
let it get so far as to reward virtue before the curtain at
the expense of art. He had a keen appreciation of the
difference between drama and melodrama, and he over-
came the temptation in the fifth act to spare Othello—
who was his personal friend, and kill Tago—who was a
publisher. It is melodrama that Australian literature has
chiefly to avoid.
* « » •
Lawson has suffered a great deal. I have reasons for
knowing that he has not over-stated his facts; and
admirers of his undoubted genius cannot fail to be abso-
lutely sorry for the picture he paints of his experiences in
the pursuit of literature. But when, drawing a moral
from his own misfortunes, he advises the young Austra-
lian writer to flee his native country as a land accursed —
“to seek London, Yankee-land. orTimbuctoo, rather than
stay in Australia till his genius turns to gall or beer, or,
failing this, to shoot himself carefully with the aid of a
looking-glass”—l am forced to rise as one young Austra-
lian writer, and announce that I am not taking any.
This our native country is good enough for some of us
still. There was a beautiful sunrise this morning—ltaly
never saw a fairer; and the vineyards of Central Cumber-
land are full of grape-blood this month —the mother of
good red hermitage and claret and sherry ; and the thorn
flowers in January.
The “ gaunt selector” next to me knocked off at four
o’clock this afternoon to go shooting, and the “gaunt
selector ” next to him went to a horse-race earlier in the
day.
We have r.o objection to invading London, some of us,
for purely commercial purposes—for the loot of Fleet-
street ; but we will not “ go steerage ” because it is dis-
agreeable ; and we will not “ stow away” because of the
penalties ; and we will not “ swim” because it is too wet;
and we prefer to go rather as conquerors than exiles, and
with some coherent claim for admission to the literary
company of the workers and thinkers of our language.
* • * *
The price of achievement in all things is effort. If
Australia is to have a distinot, characteristic literature, it
can only be given her by men who will be prepared to
labor patiently and consistently in a remote province
with a remote possibility of personal reward. Those
men who do not feel strong enough for the struggle are
at liberty to stand dcwn. Spinoza earned enough bread
to keep body and soul together by polishing lenses, and
thought and wrote. If a man has anything worth saying
he will ultimately be heard. Spinoza was heard. Colum-
bus knew the world was round : but he had to wait a
long time for the opportunity to prove it: when the thing
was proved people listened. Sometimes they only listen
after the man is dead. But this again does not affect the
quality of the result. Heine on his mattress-grave was
cheerful. He had a harder time than some Australian
writers, and I am afraid he wrote better than others.
There is no necessity to multiply examples.
My advice to young Australian writers is simply
“ Keep your bowels open and trust to the Lord.” No-
body starves in this country; but nearly everybody has
a chance of drawing a Cup winner. Lawson’s confessions
are candid; but they must be accepted in the light of a
personal example—the example of a man of unquestioned
ability who has worked so far under a considerable handi-
cap ; not the least of which is a generous, confiding
temperament.
This pessimistic Australian literature, this unlucky
melancholy of Kendall, and Gordon, and Clarke, will
later on be viewed in the light of these writers’ tempera-
ments and misfortunes, and judged accordingly ; and it
will be found then that the men, and not the country, are
responsible for the gloom and misery of the song. It will
be also admitted, of course, that there were great excuses
for the men. But the work will no longer be regarded
as typical or illustrative or descriptive of the country
in which these men lived and lamented. It will be
understood that they viewed Australia as eick people,
from a hospital window. — E. J. Brady.
* * * *

. . . The story of early
struggles against numerous
difficulties, as told by Henry
Lawson in The Bulletin, is
an instructive lesson, and
there will be few of the
many readers thereof who
will not sympathise with

Not one of whom could be

and more regularly than so
far has fallen to his lot!

. . . Why are you as
poor to-day, Henry
Lawson, as far as worldly
possessions are concerned,
as you were when you
started as a battler? An-
swer : because you roamed
too much. Do you remem-
ber how you made the brass
fly in ’B3-’B9—when you
wrote ballads for the T. and
C. ? I do, for I saw you.
As a battler no one expected
you to be an angel. But
you have yourself to blame.
“ For’ard ” says, “ We are
driven to New Zealand to be
shot out over there.” Not
necessarily. You may have
been driven from Sydney ;
not from the country. Every
man can get work if he will
look for it. You can shear.
Isn’t it a fact that a good
battler of a shearer can get
three sheds a year if he
works methodically ? Can’t
a machine shearer make his
£lOO in the one season—say
three months—if he gets a
chance ? He can live for the
rest of the year then and do
nothing if he likes. You
relate how you were broke
here, there and everywhere.
It was your own fault.
Listen ! lam twenty, you
are thirty; lam your double
in all, bar the deafness. The
drought hunted you from
Guntawang in the BJ's. It
hunted us from our selec-
tion, too, on the Q. border.
I studied ever since I could
tell one end of a bullock
from the other. I schooled
till 14 ; I haberdashed till
15; I chucked the white
collars and snagged it till
16—deliberately. I wanted
material to write up after-
wards. Did you chuck all
comforts to follow the swag-
men at 16 ? No, you didn’t.
You did at 22, when you
were forced. I clerked and
did half a hundred jobs
till 17; I taught school till
wonder he is poor, whilst
thousands read his books
and admire the author.
Just think of the hundreds
fattening in good Govern-
ment billets that never
knew a day’s hardship,
many of whom are not fit to
unbuckle the shoe of a man
like Henry Lawson, yet
when this hard-up Austra-
lian-born man with brains
and a wife and child to sup-
port applied to a paternal
Government for temporary
work “ he was kept hanging
about for weeks.” At last
in despair he applied for a
railway pass to enable him
to get work in the country,
&c. He says : il I did not
receive a reply.” Yes,
Henry Lawson, your story
is a sad one, but it is. an
admirable and a brcrre ; it is
the crying aloud of the voice
of knowledge that refused,
despite its poverty, to be
stifled. The grit was in you
and you were bound to con-
quer, and with a little more
wisdom as to the value of
your work you will go on
conquering ; then those who
have been hardest upon you
will greet you loudest. . . .
Harry Stockdalr.
. . . After an unsatisfac-
tory experience of Austra-
lian journalism I turned to
play-writing, and so far have
been told by some who claim
to know that I have done
better than any playwright
resident in the country. I
am not going to be as frank
as Henry Lawson and state
my exact income for, say,
the last five years, as such
a course might tend to pro-
mote an undesirable com-
petition in a field which, at
present, is practically my
own. At least I have not
encountered my rivals, if
there be any, at the mana-
ger’s office on Treasury
day. But if the fact be of
any interest I will go so
far as to say that I have
conclusively proved, per-
sonally, that plays are to be
sold in Australia. I never
work except on commission,
with a retaining-fee paid at
the start, and I have re-
ceived six such commissions
during the past 2J4 years.
I have sold outright nine
plays while resident in
Sydney, and my account-
book shows that the price
paid for one of them ran
into three figures. A one-
act piece (recently produced
in Sydney), involving, two
weeks’ work, and playing
only lialf-an-hour, sold for
20 guineas, and one at pre-
sent going in M.L. was pur-
chased for considerably over
that amount. And that is
better than even writing
stories for The Bulletin !
Bernard Espinasse.
nearly 18 ; shore, fenced, did all kinds of bush work
till almost 19 ; edited a successful country paper for 12
months, till nearly 20—been swagging ever since. I
tramped nearly 2000 miles as a swagman all totted up.
I’ve slept in the Domain in the bitterest winter,
and starved penniless, and felt like a criminal.
Did you do all these things deliberately ? No, you were
forced to. Then who should be the more bitter, you or I
—for that matter, I should; because I had every com-
fort. I am never bitter except when I have money;
when I haven’t, I laugh. I worked on pioneer lines, and
earned enough to take me to London and back. I did ;
and had no friends, and am much shyer than ever you
were. What did Ido these things for? That I might
make a name, not knowing whether I had the ability or
not. I didn’t start with an implicit faith in human
nature. I have none at all. And how much encourage-
ment had I? A few paragraphs in The Paper—this
Paper, none of which were more than a few inches
in size. I have had no story or verse published yet.
I claim a better knowledge of your whole work than
every other Australian. But now, as it were, having
your literary autobiography before me, that work can
never have the same fasoination for me as of yore. You
seem to have diminished its value. You have made me
uneasy. To be brief, you have disappointed me. Yet,
in your dream, “ The Writer’s (own) Dream," you
say—
“ By the dumb heart-hunger and dreams of youth, by
the hungry tracks I’ve trod—
I’ll fight as a man for the sake of truth, nor pose as a
martyred God;
By the heart of Bill and the heart of Jim, and the men
their hearts deem white,
By the handgrips fierce, and the hard eyes dim—with
forbidden tears—l’ll write," . , . I—7 x 7.
• * * *
Mercy on us ! Mercy on the moon ! , . . From
the back lane a solo ascends—
I once wrote, concerning Henry Lawson, that his verse
impressed me the less in that its prevailing pessimism ap-
peared more to be pumped-up for the sake of his song
than to embody his natural turn of thought, impera-
tively finding outlet in the song. But The Bulletin—
perhaps remembering that, as regards our Henry, “genus
irritabile vatum ” was certainly not written in vain—did
not print me to the effect aforesaid. Which was well ;
because—reading Lawson’s recent epitome of his career—
I see reason to modify my opinion. I now believe that
not for merely lyrical purposes is Lawson iconoclastic,
and revolutionary, and Uniyerse-impeachative generally.
Some veneer of rhetorical sound and fury there doubtless
is, but Lawson’s pessimism is, after all, in grain. He (at
present) “ sees yellow,” and will, belike, keep hammering
away for years to come at that “painful riddle of the
world ’’ to which—as less querulous (and, if Lawson will,
more ignoble) philosophers, like myself, are content to
believe—there is no answer.
The gravamen of Lawson’s indictment of Things in
General lies in his belief that his talent—or, if he prefer,
genius—has, in the land of his birth, been inadequately
appreciated, and his mere labour insufficiently paid-for.
On which account he adjures “any young Australian
whose talents have been recognised ” to hie him incon-
tinently to “ London, Yankeeland, or Timbuctoo, sooner
than stay in Australia till,” &o. (The full passage
will likely become a classic of Australian discontent—
and world-ignorance.) “The Talented Young Aus-
tralian” might just as well “turn his genius”—
it is genius, then 1—“ to gall—or beer,” in Sydney, as to
wormwood —or gin, in London. London, which having
picked what suits her from the horde of “talented
young’’—Australians and others—pressing ever to her
feet—calmly tramples the others into the mud of an
oblivion which need not necessarily have been theirs had
they held on at home for the mental widening and
deepening—the intellectual reinforcement and corrobor-
ation—without which (with very rare exceptions) no
enduring literary mark has ever been made. The trouble
with too many of our aspirants, in a word, is that they
are agog to strike before the iron is hot; they want to
engage all along the line before their ammunition-wag-
gons have come up.
Though, in labouring with his hands, Lawson has
quitted him right manfully, he appears to me to have
developed, or to be now developing, that dry-rot of the
Iner manhood—self-pity. Pity for others—irrespective
its moral or humanitarian aspects—is an actual mental
.nic ; but, when a man takes seriously to commiserating
mself, he throws open his flank to Fate. That she
lesn't always charge home is his luck, merely.
Just to get the perspective of things as regards Law-
in's not-ineloquent plaint, I might say something of my.
If. Lawson's experiences with publishers, in the first
ace, have been positively princely as compared with
line of mine. Lawson complains that for his two books
lostly of matter which had already been published and
lid-for) he received but £2OO in all. Well, for all rights
my “Sword of a Sin” —a novel, good or'bad—l re-
lived exactly £l2los. Of course I wanted more, and have
nee tried to get more; but for my failure therein 1
lither pity myself nor revile the publishers. I simply,
hen the recollection of the “ deal ” intrudes upon me,
ill the attention of one Alex. Montgomery to the fact
lat he is in some respects a really fine specimen of fool
id has, as such, been for the most part rightly served.
As to up-and-downs of fortune, also, I have had crowded
to my half-century of existence a variety of vicissitude
r the side of which Lawson’s record would read like
mimonplace’s self. I have been wrecked, starved,
ibbed, hocussed, clubbed, swindled, shot at, maligned,
id, as the last straw, unjustly disinherited. But the
Linel’s (or, is it the ass’s?) back has obstinately refused
i break. I’ve never howled ; and I’m not howling now
only illustrating.
Wherefore, O Henry, be of better cheer ! Haul not
>wn thy colours thus untimely, and regard not the
iblisher-man generically as thy natural enemy. In-
vidually, I grant thee, too many of him are ready to
•ind thy bones, and mine, to make him bread ; but if I,
; twice thy .years, have not lost hope of some day strik-
ig against one in whom the bowels, by some strange
lance, have not been omitted, surely it is full soon for
lee to go and sit amongst the potsherds.— Alex.

The clouds scurry and scatter; the rift of purple
idens to a sea, with one round sail of silver.
How the moon triumphs through the endless
ight! ” There is a great silence.
PIPES AND BEER.
Before I was famous I used to sit
In a dull old underground room I knew, §
And sip cheap beer, and be glad for it, 'A
With a wild Bohemian friend or two. -£*
And oh, it was joy to loiter thus,
At peace in the.heart of the city’s stir, M
Entombed, while life hurried over us,
In our lazy bacchanal sepulchre.
There was artist George, with the blonde Greek head,
And the startling creeds, and the loose cravat;
There was splenetic journalistic Fred,
Of the sharp retort and the shabby hat;
There was dreamy Frank, of the lounging gait,
Who lived on nothing a year, or less,
And always meant to be something great, !
But only meant, and smoked to excess. |- i
And last, myself, whom their funny sneers
Annoyed no whit as they laughed and said
I listened to all their grand ideas
And wrote them out for my daily bread ! '« -J ..
The Teuton beer-bibbers came and went,
Night after night, and stared, good folk, '
At our table, noisy with argument,
And our chronic aureoles of smoke.
And oh, my life ! but we all loved well
The talk,—free, fearless, keen, profound,—
The rockets of wit that flashed and fell
In that dull old tavern underground !
But there came a change in my days at last, /•
And fortune forgot to starve and stint,
And the people chose to admire aghast
The book I had eaten dirt to print.
And new friends gathered about me then, rM
New voices summoned me there and here ;
The world went down in my dingy den,
And drew' me forth from the pipes and beer.
I took the stamp of my altered lot,
As the sands of the certain seasons ran,
And slowly, whether I would or not,
I felt myself growing a gentleman.
But now and then I would break the thrall, '
I would yield to a pang of dumb regret,
And steal to join them, and find them all,
With the amber wassail near them yet— .y
Find and join them and try to seem
A fourth of the old queer merry three, >
With my fame as much of a yearning dream
As my morrow’s dinner was wont to be.
But the wit would lag and the mirth would lack,
And the god of jollity hear no call;
And the prosperous broadcloth on my back
Hung over their spirits like a pall 1
It was not that they failed, each one, to try
Their warmth of welcome to speak and show ;
I should just have risen and said good-bye
With a haughty look had they served me so.
It was rather that each would seem instead, -•
With not one vestige of spleen or pride,
Across a chasm of change to spread
His greeting hands to the further side. -
And our gladdest words rang strange and cold,
Like the echoes of other long-lost words ;
And the nights were no more the nights of old
Than spring would be spring without the birds !
So they waned and waned, these visits of mine,
Till I married the heiress, ending here.
For if caste approves the cigar and wine,
She must frown perforce upon pipes and beer.
And now 'tis years since I saw these men,
Years since I knew them living yet;
And of this alone I am sure since then—
I That none has gained what he toiled to get.
For I keep strict watch on the world of art,
And George, with his wide, rich-dowered brain !
His fervent fancy, his ardent heart,
Though he greatly toiled, has toiled in vain.
And Fred, for all he may sparkle bright
In caustic column, in clever quip,
Of a truth must still be hiding his light
Beneath the bushel of journalship.
And dreamy Frank must be dreaming still,
Lounging through life, if yet alive,
Smoking his vast preposterous fill.
Lounging, smoking, striving to strive.
And I, the fourth of that queer old throng,
Fourth and least, as my soul avows,—
I alone have been counted strong,
I alone have the laurelled brows !
Well, and what has it all been worth ?
May not my soul to my soul confess,
That “ succeeding,” here upon earth,
Does not always assume success ?
I would cast, and gladly, from this gray head
Its crown, to regain one sweet lost year
With artist George, with splenetic Fred,
With dreamy Frank, with the pipes and beer 1
Edgar Fawcett.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
• “6 Y H ” on certain aspects of the
Northern colored-labor question
On the Johnstone (N.Q.) you may see hundreds of
turban-topped, out-throat Hindoos working for the white
men. The farmers say they are better than the Kanaka-
more kindly, and working longer hours (with less growl)
than the latter, who is fast becoming “done-up” with
the poisons in which the Chow does wholesale “ biz ”
with Tommy Tanna. Of course, there are still many
Kanakas employed, but lots of them do prohibited tasks
—cooking, horse-driving, milking and house-work. I
know one bachelor farmer whose Kanaka cook makes up
the boss’s bunk, etc.—pending the arrival of a Hindoo
married couple, and kids —man to plough and wife to
cook and do housework. The wage paid these scum
ranges from 15s. to 20s. per week.
Re the fossilised N.S.W. Marine Board.
In S.M. Herald of Oct., 1880, is recorded the
4oss of the Susannah Godeffroy. Captain, mate
and six seamen drowned. The old tub had been
lying- for three years at Snail’s Bay, was reckoned
rotten, and only fit for a coal hulk. Was patched
up and sold, and passed by a marine surveyor of
the ) eriod. She took in 400 tons of coal, for
stiffening, at Sydney and went on to Newcastle,
where she gathered in 1100 tons more, leaking
earnestly all the time. Sailed for Yokohama 19th
Sept., 1880, and nine days later was piled up on
North Beach, eight miles above Newcastle, for
safety’s sake. She went to pieces in ten minutes,
evidence disclosing that she was rotten as an old
coal-basket. Much public indignation was ex-
pressed at the ship (uninsured —no one would take
risk) being allowed to go to sea. Jury on deaths
of captain, etc., were most indignant, and sug-
gested that Govt, should do something. Govt,
declared it was going to do something. It is still
“ going to do something.” And that was nineteen
long years ago.
The Gold-Fever.
We joined the rush on Forest Creek; from ship
and shore we came—
The men that held Eureka in the days of golden
fame.
From Bendigo and Ballarat, to last new Gipps-
land rush,
We worked on all, and starved on some, between
the times of flush.
We digged and raised and cradled when our
Turon claims we chose ;
We sluiced it at Temora —felt the cold Kiandra
snows;
Picked and puddled from New England back to
where Mt. Brown a-west
Makes trio with the White Cliffs and the Barrier’s
i silver crest.
If you get the fever badly
You must chance it, luck or lack;
For there isn’t any curing
Till you make for fields out baok.
Oh, it’s when the gold is calling,
And the windlass it is hauling—
It’s ounces to a color you’ll be off along the track.
We dug among the Maoris, when the Greenstone
hills were tried,
And helped to cut the head-race out on bleak
Otago side ;
We watched the snow-swelled freshets down the
Lainmerlaw in spring,
And dodged the falling pay-dirt that the sluicing
nozzles bring.
We tramped it in from Cooktown, through Hell's
Gates to Palmer field,
And Oakey Creek ran yellow as we panned that
first grand yield;
Above the line of Capricorn we worked in tropic
rain
Until the yellow man came there and drove us
south again.
When you see the new rush starting
Your nerves are all a-cock,
The town-delights won’t hold you
If you think to peg a block.
Oh, it’s when the gold is calling
And your mates they are a-bawling—
You’ll roll your kit and join them where you
know the cradles rock.
Echunga saw us toiling, and Teetulpa’s buried
i streams;
And south ward from Port Darwin did we drive
our sweating teams.
We walked the rocking corduroy that led across
the isle
By Beaconsfield and Bischoff, and the fields we
found at Lisle.
We specked the wind-worn nuggets when we fol-
lowed Bayley through,
Our shakeis swung and riddled as we prospected
at Cue.
From Kimberley to Norseman, from Pilbarra to
the ’Cross,
The red earth shows our wanderings—the scat-
tered bones our loss !
But you’ll always want to follow
Where the wash is raised and tried,
For the fever keeps you moving,
And the fields are far and wide.
Oh, it’s when the gold is calling
And the chains of town are galling—
If you’re loth to leave a certainty to try your luck
outside—
Break away !
Out again on Fortune’s highway !
Every other road’s a byway!
And while yet the gold is calling
Let us leave the townfolk crawling—
Let us damn the dull, dead certainties, and chance
our luck again !

round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel 00,

SYDNEY G.P.O. SUNDAY ARRANGEMENTS.
The Postmaster-General has arranged that letters for the
north, south, and western mails, leaving Sydney by train on
Sunday evening, may be posted, at the
General Post-office up to 5 p.m., and
postage stamps may be purchased at the
Stamp Sales Office between 4 and 5 p.m.
The council of the Sydney Zoo has recently purchased a 17-
foot alligator for £s* -Which is dirt cheap, as alligators go.
Those used at The Bulletin' editorial rooms usually cost us 7s.
10id. wholesale. But that is a mere detail. The new Zoo alli-
gator has so far refused raw dog as l»eing too sudden a change
| from nigger.

London, Saturday. —Mr Joseph Chamberlain, Secretary
of State for the Colonies, who has been suffering from an
attack of gout, has recovered from his indisposition.
Joe’s gout must have been pretty bad when he gave
that advice about repaying the New Guinea land-burglars
[Jobbers (?)] Gout, sometimes affects the reason. And
men have, in their agony, seen pigs flying through the air.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 11, 1899.
The Blot and the Compromise and Other
Matters.
Tiie Federal compromise arrived suddenly
as most good things do, and last Friday it
was announced that all the six Premiers
were agreed, and were prepared to fight for
the New Bill to the hitter end. Even
George Reid, of New South Wales, is at last,
by his own report, an enthusiastic advocate
of the Federal Constitution, and has promised
to make it a vital part of the Government
policy. Also Oily James Dickson, of
Queensland, is agreeable, and Braddon is
pleased, for his ignorantly-abused Blot lias
been vindicated after all, and all the boss
politicians are satisfied for the moment, and
the unanimity of everybody is wonderfully
unanimous indeed.
• • • •
The alterations made don’t amount to so
very much after all. The amendments de-
manded by the Reid Ministry included the
removal of the much-slandered Braddon
Blot, and the good old Blot still survives.
For it proved that Premier Reid hadn’t a
shadow of an idea what to put in its place
and after nearly nine months hitter denunci-
ation of the Blot he was fain to admit that
it was the best available Blot afier all.
Which is a great triumph for Braddon and
his Blot. By way of making the surrender
look a little better, however, it is provided
that the Blot is to be reconsidered after ten
years—though, of course, it was always liable
to reconsideration by the Federal Parliament
and the people at any time. The amend-
ment. of the law relating to river-control, on
which the Reid Government was tremend-
ously strong, was dropped, and that matter
remains where it was. The amendments
regarding Money Bills and judicial appeals
were also dropped. The demand for a new
financial scheme was dropped likewise, lie-
cause Premier Reid had no better financial
scheme to offer— neither had anyone else.
The capital is not to be fixed at Sydney, nor
is it to be New South Wales property,
but it is agreed that it shall be in
New South Wales (where it would prob-
ably have been in any case), on a piece
of Federal territory at least 100 square
miles in area, and that it shall be located
—like a noxious-trade or a boiling-down
establishment at least 100 miles from
Sydney. The demand that the number of
senators for each State be increased from
six to eight came to nothing. But it was
decided that when the two Federal Houses
sit and vote together to settle a deadlock, the
measure under discussion may be carried if a
majority of the entire number of members
vote for it, whereas the old idea was that to
carry it would require a vote of two-thirds of
the members actually present. The two-
thirds idea was originally invented by New
South Wales Minister Carruthebs, and
was inserted largely at the instance of Car-
Ruthers and Reid, so the latter has only got
what he probably would have got last year if
he hadn’t fought against it.
• • • •
The mass Referendum re alterations of the
Constitution demanded by the New South
Wales Assembly dropped, like most other
tilings ; it placed the four small States so
absolutely under the domination of the two
large ones, and gave the two large States
such entire power to take back everything
granted by the contract of union to their
smaller neighbors, that it never had a show.
T“ alter the bargain made between the
States and embodied in the Constitution, it
is still necessary to secure the assent of a
majority of the States and a majority of the
people, but the arrangement has been greatly
liberalised by providing that the assent of
both Houses of the Federal Parliament is
not necessary ; if one House passes the Re-
form Bill twice it may send it to a Referen-
dum whether the other House assents or not,
Mid if the popular majority and the majority
of States accept it, it will become law. Also, it
has been agreed that no State shall have its
boundaries altered without its consent. This
last is the only amendment demanded by
New South Wales which was carried in its
entirety.
• • • •
The new Bill is not likely to suit the
bitterly provincial clan in New South Wales
much better than the old one did. The most
remarkable feature about it is the vindica-
tion of the poor old harmless Blot. There
was never anything wrong with that memor-
able smudge though it was the butt of
all the ignorant provincialist grumblers in
Cornstidk Land. It sets forth that three-
of the customs and excise revenue,
whether the same he small, large, or medium,
and always providing that there is any cus-
toms and excise revenue at all, shall be
handed h ick to the States so that they may
pay the interest on their public debts with
it, if the debts remain under provincial con-
trol. And if the debts are taken over by the
Commonwealth, as they certainly will he
sooner or later, three-quarters of the customs
and excise are to he set apart to pay interest
with. Considering that it takes, not three-
quarters, hut the whole of the customs and
excise revenue to pay interest now, and that,
when interprovincial customs are abolished,
it will probably take a lot more than the
whole of this revenue to do it, the dreadful
Bkaddon scheme is about as harmless as a
drink of milk in any case. It doesn’t com-
mit the Commonwealth to any particular
tariff or to any tariff at all, and it leaves
things exactly as they are. Nevertheless,
the provincialist party in New South Wales
will doubtless start its ridiculous campaign
against the Braddon clause all over again,
and tell all its bygone lies afresh, just as if
they were a perfectly new set of Ananias.
• • • •
A good many other arguments of the paro-
chial multitude will also do duty over again.
Among them was Jack Want’s tremendous
blither about the Victorian duty on New
South Wales coal—the duty that never
existed ; and the Telegraph’s shriek about
tlie inevitable increase of New South Wales
postage-rates, and the ruin of the New South
Wales coal export industry (presumably
because, under Federation, Queensland and
Tasmania would have to take off their pre-
sent duties and let coal in free), and about
the necessity for a tariff revenue of £8,700,000
or something like that. Also there was
Coghlan’s tale about a 27 per cent, tariff or
something to that effect ; and Brunker’s
clumsy lamentation that a 27 per cent, tariff
meant that every man with £lOO income would
have to pay £27 in taxes ; and the Star’s
statement that “ all the burden of taxation
would fall on New South Wales ” ; and
Pumpkin Smith’s terrifying apparition,
adopted later on by both Star and Tele-
graph, to the effect that the Federal Govern-
ment would have to pay the bounties granted
by Victoria in aid of its own local industries.
Even Premier Reid hadn’t the front to ask
the other provinces to remove these evils
out of the Federal Constitution, for the suffi-
cient reason that he knew none of them
were ever in it, and the originators of this
flock of wild apparitions knew it also. So,
with one exception, all the rocks which the
New South Wales parochial party aimed at
the old Bill will do just as well to fire at the
new one, and a great, soul-filling fight is in
prospect.
• * • t
And, as for Premier Reid, that n.an of
strange experiences and unexpected de-
partures, whose Yes is No and his No Yes,
and his Yes-no something for the gods to
shriek at his situation is a curious
one. The Retd party cursed Barton be-
cause he proposed to negotiate witli tho
other provinces instead of demanding
concessions, and it has also negotiated;
and instead of demanding concessions
it asked for them in a mild voice, and didn’t
get the most of them. And now Reid is
pledged to stake the existence of his Ministry
on defending the old river agreement, and
the old system of judicial appeals, and the
old financial arrangement, and the old Blot,
and to fight to the bitter end for many
things which he and Want previously de-
nounced. Too much Yes-No has brought
biin back to be the advocate and fond parent
of a Bill which is largely the Bame old
Bill over again—the one he voted for and
spoke against last year. Last time, the Bill
was defeated because a private member de-
manded the iniquitous 80,000 minimum, and
the Government said it objected yet con-
sented, the reason being, as Minister Young
explained, that it couldn’t rally up its
party against the proposed breach of
faith without grossly infringing the rights
of private members. This time, the
Reid Government is pledged to have
no minimum at all, whether it has to
infringe the rights of private members or
not, and thereby it undertakes to give the lie
to its colleague Young and to show that it
could have prevented that memorable breach
of faith—only it didn’t want to. And, un-
less Reid apostatises again, he and his
faithful Telegraph must part company at
last—always provided the Telegraph be-
lieves all the horrors it swore to a year ago.
• * • •
The new Bill, of course, is not perfect,
any more than its brother, the old one,
was. It is better by reason of the proviso
for a bare majority at the joint sitting. It
is a little worse because of the foolish and
utterly Tory provision that no portion of any
of the present unwieldy States can get
separation, however much it may suffer from
the tyranny of the metropolitan majority,
without that majority’s consent. And both
Bills would have been vastly improved if they
had provided for placing the debts and
railways under Federal control. But it
is apparently the best scheme that pro-
vincial prejudice will at present allow of.
It is a vast amount better than no scheme at
all—and if it is rejected the name of Fede-
ration will probably have become such a
weariness that the alternative for many
years to come will be no scheme at all. It
provides one of the most democratic forms
of government in the world, and it contains
abundant possibilities of amendment later
on. Therefore, if Premier Reid goes straight
for the first time in his life—if he stakes the
fate of his Ministry on the measure, and
creates a sufficient number of democratic
nominees to bear down the opposition of
the Tory Upper House, if that House
proves hostile, and suppresses Want, and
makes cow-merchant Brunker lie low,
and refuses to wobble when his customary
boss, the Daily Telegraph, bids him
wobble—if he does these things The Bul-
letin will have the unwonted satisfaction of
being Reid’s friend and brother for once and
in this one instance. But it is useless to
disguise the fact that this time there
may be serious trouble ahead in more than
one province. Tho huge vote of 3rd
June, 1898, is hardly likely to Vie repeated,
for a year of waiting and wobbling and inde-
cision has cooled the popular enthusiasm,
and tho popular impression that the great
Yes-No must go wrong somehow at the last
moment will give an unreal feeling to the
wholo proceedings. If all the provinces sub-
mit the new Bill fairly, and don’t load the
dice this time in favor of the bad cause with
any minimum proviso, the measure will pro-
bably be carried everywhere, just as it was
last year. But otherwise anything may
happen.
“ Saving His Face.”
The N.S.W. Statistical Register begins to
look, not so much a cold, dispassionate mass of
figures regarding Cornstalk affairs, as a publi-
cation issued with a view to whitewashing the
financial methods of the Reid Ministry. Some
curious things have happened to the N.S.W.
official statistics of late. As an instance:
for a year or two the annual estimates
issued by the Statistical Department set forth
that in 1895—the first complete year in which
the Reid Government held office—the popula-
tion of Sydney decreased 15,000, and that in
the ensuing year, though there was an increase,
it was a very small one—only 1500. It was
awkward that such a thing should happen, and
people said unpleasant things about the pro-
sperity which was to arise as soon as the boast-
ful G. Reid got fairly started, and how it
somehow didn’t arise. Rut in the Statistical
Register for 1897 a perfectly new set of figures
was presented which set the trouble right and
put Reid on a much pleasanter basis. The
alleged metropolitan population at the end of
the Protectionist period was reduced by 26,000,
and the decline of 15,000 in 1895 was turned
into an increase of 6060; and instead of an
increase of 1500 people in ’96 there was shown
an increase of 7000. These alterations naturally
made the Foreign-trade party in office feel
much better. Instead of showing a large in-
crease of city population under Protection and
a decrease under Foreign-trade, as the old
figures did, the new set showed a small increase
under Protection and a large one under Premier
Reid’s policy of shoddy and cheap imports.
No reason was given for this amazing alteration
of the figures, but the party in power found it
very comforting all the same.
* • * •
Then the factory statistics of N.S.W. under-
went an opportune, though less unaccountable,
transformation. Until the end of 1895 no place
of work in which machinery Was not tised liras
included in the statistics unless it employed at
least fivo hands* and milliners, dressmakers,
and tailors were not reckoned atnong factory
operatives at all. But in the year when the
new Foreign-trade tariff came into force, a huge
alteration was made ; the millinery, dressmak-
ing, and tailoring Classes were reckoned as fac-
tory operatives for the first time, and every
place not Using machinery where four hands
were employed was reckoned a factory, and
the people engaged therein included in
the enumeration. This had the advan-
tage of securing a certain measure of uniformity
with Victoria. It also had the advantage of
increasing the apparent number of workers by
4618, and enabling Foreign-trade politicians to
point out that this tremendous increase arose as
soon as the blessing of Shoddy descended on
the land. And again the party in power in
New South Wales felt better—a great deal
better
• * • •
And now even the poor old Deficit isn’t
allowed to rest, and a perfectly new set of
official statistics regarding it has been pub-
lished. The old lot—the one which the Statis-
tical Register swore to up till 30th June,
1897, used to set forth that the overdraft was
as follows :
Year. Ministry. Deficit.
1886 ... Jennings £1,286,581
1867 .. Parses 2,159,295
1888 ... „ 1,581,843
1889 ... „ 1,700,074
1890 ... „ 1,615,551
1891 ... „ 2,026,846
1892 ... Dibbs 1,945,054
1893 ... „ 2,294,935
1894 ... „ 2,217,599
1895 ... Reid 1,919,334
1896 ... „ 2,301,054
1897 ... „ 2,363,139
These figures were officially published as
authentic, and they stood on record till less
than a month ago. They were a beastly un-
pleasant set of numerals which wound up by
showing that the deficit reached its highest
proportions under the Reid Ministry ; and
rude people drew attention to the fact and
made remarks about it. But within the last
month a perfectly new set of official figures,
showing that the deficit didn’t reach its largest
proportions under Reid at all, but that that
financial genius reduced it enormously, was
issued from the N.S.W. Statistical Department.
And for the third time the Foreign-trade party
in power feels a great deal better.
• • * •
The new' deficit is made up by adding (in the
cases of Jennings, Farkes and Dibbs) to the
actual shortage at the end of each financial
year the amount of all liabilities incurred but
not actually paid. And it pans out thus
Jennings.
Visible deficit. .Liabilities Incurred. Total deficit.
1886 ... £1,286,581 £1,198,485 ... £2,485,066
Parkbs
1887 ... 2,159,295 ... 446,057 ... 2,605,352
1888 ... 1,581,045 ... 808,785 ... 2,390,430
1889 ... 1,907,100 ... 768,444 ... 2,474,523
1890 ... 2,352,884 ... 793,830 ... 2,409,386
1891 ... 2,202,884 ... 768,258 ... 2,795,108
Dibbs.
1892 ... 2,052,884 ... 1,055,945 ... 3,001,003
1893 ... 2,276,181 ... 676,700 ... 2,951,881
1894 ... 2,200,487 ... 926,497 ... 3,125,984
Reid.
1896 ... 1,902,224 ... 781,117 ... 2,683,341
1896 ... ... ... 2,596,990
1807 ... ... ... 2,443.596
1898 ... ... ... 2,293,596
From 1889 onwards there isn’t one of these
figures which bears even the faintest resem-
blance to the old set. Even the visible deficit
(apart from the outstanding liabilities which
are a new element imported into the business)
doesn’t in the least resemble the one which
Coghlan’s department has been swearing to so
long. So the natural inference is that either
the old lot was quite unreliable, or that the
now lot is ''quite unreliable, or that both lots
are perfectly unreliable together.
• • • *
Anyhow, the perfectly new information about
the deficit comes along just in time to rescue
Premier Reid out of an awkward hole. The
old information set forth that that boastful
political sham had, in bis period of alleged
economy, made the shortage bigger than it ever
was before, and nasty things were being said
about it. So one of his departments lias
promptly come to bis assistance by making the
four-year-old shortage of that useful beast of
burden, Dibbs (Hea\en only knows what the
gang in office would do without Dibbs),
£900,000 more than it used to be, and Reid’s
organ, the Telegraph, is already pointing with
pride to its burlesque fat man as the person who
paid off £BOO,OOO of the burden left behind by
his incompetent successor.
* • • •
And—except on paper—it is all a hollow
fraud and a mockery and a delusion. The
visible deficit of Reid, in 1898, is, even according
to the new whitewashing system, £93,000 more
than the visible deficit of Dibbs in 1894. The
difference lies in the fact that in addition to
Dibbs’s visible deficit at the end of 1894 lie had
unpaid liabilities to the amount of £925,497
while Reid has no unpaid liabilities. And the
re-ison of this is, not that Reid lias paid up any
more punctually than the other man did, but
that bis artful Audit Act Amendment Act of
1895 decrees that all of what used to be called
live unpaid liabilities of the year shall not be
that year’s unpaid liabilities at all, but
shall be part of the liabilities of next
year. He simply passed a law that last
year’s arrears should no longer be last
year’s arrears but should be amounts not
due till this year, or some future year. If
Premier Reid had applied the same principle
to the cleaning of Sydney he would have made
it, with no expense and merely by the stroke of
the pen, the one city in the world in which
there was absolutely no dirt whatever. It
would only have been necessary to pass a little
law saying that, on and after the first day of
July, 1895, dirt shall not he dirt but shali he
called (say) gold, or ox-tail soup, or anything
else that seemed suitable. Or lie could have
abolished typhoid and all other diseases, and
made Sydney so healthy that people wouldn’t
die at all, merely by enacting that “illness
and death are abolished after ibis dale and
will bo hereafter described as good health and
a profound admiration of the Reid Govern-
ment.”


The working of the idea was beautifully
simple. In the old days, if .£lOO,OOO was voted
in (say) 1892 for some public work, and only
£lO,OOO of the money was actually paid away
in that year, still the whole £lOO,OOO was
charged to 1892, the odd £90,000 being in-
cluded in the “sums owing but unpaid.”
Under the new system, if £lOO,OOO is voted in
1899 for a public work, and only £lO,OOO is spent
in 1899, that year is charged with the £lO,OOO
only, and the £90,000 is not reckoned as an un-
paid liability at all. Reid has unpaid liabili-
ties at the end of the year just like his prede-
cessors, but, being one of the artful dodger
tribe, he has wisely enacted that they won’t be
unpaid liabilities, and that they won’t appear
in the accounts. Dibbs and Parkes and Jen-
nings are charged, at the end of each year, not
only with the money they had spent, but w'ith
the full cost of the works authorised, even
though these works were not much more
than begun and the money might not be
actually due for years, and even as late
as 1896 Reid was still spending money
which he included in Dibbs’s shortage.
Reid only charges himself with the money
actually spent ; he might, if his accounts were
presented in the same form as the other fel-
low’s, have a million or more of unpaid liabili-
ties—only he has passed a law which prohibits
the painful matter from being officially men-
tioned.
• • * •
The moral of all which is that the State
Statistics should not even look as if they were
faked to glorify the bosses in office and help
them to keep in office—largely by maligning
the party in opposition. They should be abso-
lutely honest and above suspicion. When
Reid’s deficit at the end of the year is only the
amount actually spent in excess of revenue,
then Jennings and Parkes and Dibbs’s deficits
should also be the amount they actually spent
in excess of revenue also—not the amount they
actually spent, and, in addition, the large
sums which they didn’t spend, but which
became due in after years on works
which they only started. The old method of
showing the figures was fair and honest ; the
new one is disgracefully unfair. Premier Reid
did not reduce the deficit by £BOO,OOO odd as
the new set of figures pretends to allege ; he
simply devised a w’ily scheme for “ saving his
face,’ as the Chinese expression goes. His
whole career has been one of shams and pre-
tences, and his whole object and purpose is to
“save his face.’ If Dibbs had passed Reid’s
amended Audit Act in ’92 the alleged deficit of
£3,001,003 would have dropped to £2,052,884
on the spot; the province wouldn’t have been a
penny richer, but it would simply have been
enacted that the £1,055,945 unpaid liabilities
would not be called unpaid liabilities any
longer. And if he had gone a step further and
abolished the rest of the deficit by enacting
that in future it shouldn’t be called a deficit
but a rhododendron it would only have been a
case of carrying the same artful idea a little
further towards its logical conclusion.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Compensation for the Burglar.
Thf. great Papuan land-swindle scheme fell
through last week, the British Government,
under the pressure of a rain of angry cable-
grams from Victoria and N.S.YV., having
decided that the utterly illegal steal shall not
come off. But kindly Joseph Chamberlain
recommends that, Victoria, N'.S. VV., and Queens-
land should repay to the chief land-jobbers
(Lowi.es and Somers Vine, apparently) the
money they spent trying to diddle these pro-
vinces, it being a fair thing, in Chamberlain’s
opinion, that any man whose house is broken
into should pay the burglar for his time and
the wear and tear of his jemmy. The proposi-
tion shows up Mr. J. Chamberlain as a most
unexpected ass, or a very wild humorist—pro-
bably the latter. In the agreement for the
control of Papua it was distinctly provided
that Queensland, N.S. W., and Victoria
should put up a certain annual contribu-
tion for the support of the Papuan Govern-
ment, and that the Governments of the
three provinces must be consulted on all
important Papuan matters. Lowi.es, M.P.,
knew the nature of the agreement as well as
any one. Also, he knew perfectly well that the
stupendous grab he was aiming at would never
receive the assent of N.S. W. and Victoria at
all events, so he carefully avoided all mention
of it on his visit to these provinces. He passed
himself off as a friendly visitor seeking general
information in the interests of the “Empiah,”
and he was well received in that capacity.
Later on he began to appear in the light of a
mean and shabby spy playing a confidence game
on his hosts. He persuaded the very remark-
able Governor M'Gregor, of Papua, to make
an agreement, handing over to Lowles’s syndi-
cate, at a ridiculously low price, all the lands
and minerals of Papua that are likely to be
available for the next 40 years, to the absolute
exclusion of Australian miners or land-buyers,
who could only get in on the syndicate’s terms.
He apparently persuaded M ‘Gregor to ille-
gally keep this transaction dark from the
Governments of Victoria and N.S. YV., and from
all except one or two members of the Queensland
Ministry—for the sufficient reason that if N.S.
Wales and Victoria had been consulted as pro-
vided by law the mighty grab hadn’t a ghost
of a chance to get through. Premiers Reid and
Turner just woke up in time to see their
honored and trusted guest getting out of the
window with the valuables, and for the last
five or six months they have been hanging
desperately on to his leg and calling for the
police; And now that he has been made to
give up these valuables he wanes £15,000 from
Reid and Turner as compensation for the time
he spent in annexing the loot, and the trouble
he had to get out of the window, and the
mental worry he went through while Turner
and Reid were hanging on to his leg. Turner
lifts up his voice in Melbourne to the effect
that he will see his late treacherous guest
hanged first, and some remarks from Reid on
the same subject are anxiously expected.
More Papuan Syndicate.
The Papuan land-swindle has ceased to interest
the Australian public, and, in view of the
cable news that Chamberlain has disallowed
the Vine syndicate, the whole case will
probably be shelved, as far as the Australias
are concerned. Meanwhile, would it not be
advisable to enquire a little more diligently
into the secret reasons of the assertive Lowles’
sudden subsidence ? While all eyes were cen-
tred on that {(articular crowd of grabbers, other
enterprisingindividuals havesneaked into Papua
and are beginning to exploit it to their heart’s
content. One Sydneyite is managing a syndi-
cate, British and Australian, for the better
preservation of the monopolist; and several
other crowds are unostentatiously going for the
boodle. It is more than probable that Vine
and co., while making their stand and attract-
ing attention to one focus, quietly crept in
under another name; and, when the first
attempt has been abandoned with a show of
reluctance, they will be found peaceably estab-
lished under another title in Papua. The fact
is that the contributing provinces have squashed
one syndicate with such all-engrossing zeal that
half-a-dozen others, just like their prototype,
found opportunity to undisturbedly settle on
the island. If Australia really intends to keep
British Papua, her nearest oversea neighbor,
clean of slavery and extorting monopolism, a
permanent agent will have to be appointed for
the Possession, or the administration handed
over to the contributing provinces. The pre-
sent arrangement means simply the establish-
ment of an elaborate bonus-system for the
benefit of land-grabbing syndicates.
Suffield and the Other Man.
An obscure line or two in the Sydney papers
has just informed the public that the sentence
on Rofe, the ex-solicitor who recently got a
new hearing, is to stand, after all. But the
huge discrepancy between the cases of Rofe
and Suffield is rather aggravated than
diminished by this decision. Just consider the
full beauty and remarkableness of that dis-
crepancy :
Suffield, the uninfluential engine-hand, was convicted
on the evidence of one girl, who contradicted herself on
every point, and despite the positive evidence in his
favor of a number of respectable witnesses. He got ten
years, and had to serve out his sentence.
Rofe, the influential solicitor, was convicted on con-
vincing evidence, and his own counsel practically threw
up the sponge and pleaded, not that he was innocent, but
that he. was “ led away.” He got a nominal sentence of
five years, and was immediately released. He didn’t
serve a day of his sentence, though the other persons con-
victed in the same case served theirs as usual.
There always was a widespread doubt about the guilt of
the workman, yet he can’t get the semblance of an
inquiry into his case, though the law provides that an
inquiry may be granted where “ any doubt or question ”
arises as to the guilt of a prisoner.
There never was any widespread doubt about the
solicitor’s guilt, yet he got his inquiry.
The engine-driver was told he couldn’t gee an inquiry
unless he produced new evidence—a thing which isn't
even hinted at in the law.
The solicitor got his inquiry, so far as is known, with-
out producing a scrap of new evidence.
The case of the workman had to be advertised all
over the country, in the press and in Parliament, before
his demand w-as even seriously considered.
The solicitor got his inquiry quite calmly and peace-
fully, without the need for press, or public, or Parlia-
mentary agitation. He got it so silently that even the
process by which it was obtained is unknown, and the
evidence was suppressed by the court’s order.
The inquiry also confirmed the conviction of the in-
fluential solicitor’s guilt, which is solid proof that he got
his silent, unobtrusive inquiry upon very little cause.
The uninfluential working man can show good cause,
yet he can’t get an inquiry.
When an inquiry is demanded on behalf of the work-
man, the invariable answer is that the jury’s verdict is
final according to law, and that if every man whose
friends think he is unjustly convicted gets a new hearing
there will be no finality in criminal proceedings. There
is practically no finality in civil proceedings, but it is
always assumed that that is all right, while the absence
of finality in criminal proceedings is all wrong. And it is
a convenient fiction that all the thousands of people who
regard Suffield as being unjustly convicted are his per-
ional friends ami are biassed accordingly. Apparently
that obscure workman had an amazing number of personal
friends in the most remarkable places.
In the case of the well-connected solicitor nothin# was
said about the jury’s verdict liein# final, and the evil of
disturbing the beautiful finality of criminal proceedings
didn’t matter in the least.
Another stereotyped reason why the Suffield case
shouldn’t be du# up again is that Dean got an inquiry
and was released, though he was really guilty—therefore,
interference with a jury’s verdict is unwise and inquiries
are unsatisfactory. But though Dean is a constant
reason why StJFFiKLD shouldn’t get an inquiry, he was,
apparently, no reason why the well-connected other man
shouldn’t have one.
How does all this look as a case of one law—
alleviated by polite and considerate grovel on
the part of people in authority—for the rich,
and ten years without the option for the poor?

The Cornstalk’s Wrongs.
Truly the N.S.W. parochial gang is a hard
crowd to satisfy. The new Federal agreement
provides that the capital of the Commonwealth
shall be in New South Wales anywhere over
100 miles from Sydney ; so N.S.W. has got the
capital to a dead certainty to commence with.
Also, even if the Federal city is situated 200
miles from Sydney it will be so much nearer to
that port than to Melbourne or Adelaide or
Brisbane that the Cornstalk city will be able to
secure all the trade. N.S.W. will control the
railways running through the new city. The
other provinces made no stipulation that the
railway rates from the Victorian or S.A. or
Queensland border to the Federal city should be
at the same rate as those from Sydney to the
Federal city, so that N.S.W. has further large
possibilities of securing the whole trade
of the new capital by making the freights
between it and the other provinces
practically prohibitive. None of these matters
perhaps amount to very much, still N.S.W. has
got a distinct start cf its partners in the Com-
monwealth. But it is agreed that the first
Federal Parliament shall meet in Melbourne—
it has to meet somewhere to decide where-
abouts in N.S.W. the capital shall be located.
And already the parochial Jeremiahs are
writing in the Telegraph about the same old
conspiracy on the part of somebody to swindle
N.S. W. out of something. That widely-known
fossil. E. W. Knox, desires to point out that
the Federal Parliament may go on meeting
in Melbourne till a Dew Federal capital
is built (nothing has been said, so far,
about building a perfectly new capital),
and the painfully local Knox evidently thinks
that the horrors of a Federal Parliament hold-
ing its first meetings in Melbourne are so self-
evident as to require no explanation. And he
wants to know what will happen if the Federal
Parliament never decides on a capital at all and
goes on meeting temporarily in Melbourne all
its life, or if it fixes the capital at Albury or
Moama, or some place in N.S.W. which is so
far from Sydney that Federation won’t im-
prove the value of city property. Of
course, no one can answer a conundrum like
that. There is nothing in the Federal Consti-
tution to prevent the Federal capital being
fixed at Albury (N.S.W 7 .), or to prevent the
Federal Parliament passing a law that Knox
shall have his hair dyed bright red and be
compelled to marry six wives ; and there is
not even anything in the N.S. W. Constitution
to prevent the Sydney Parliament shifting the
N.S.W. capital out to Hungerford or Cobar, or
to prevent it making cat-soup a compulsory
article of diet. In fact, the man who starts by
asking what is to prevent somebody doing this
or that horror if we federate, or if we don’t
federate, or if we do sometliing else, or if
we do anything or nothing, or if it is suddenly
found out that we don’t exist at all owing
to nobody having been born, has such a
wide field of foolish hypothesis before him that
he is quite unanswerable. Even if he asked
what is to- prevent the N.S.W. Parliament,
supposing it doesn’t federate, from enacting
that the railways be torn up and that the pro-
vince should take to the bullock-dray again,
the only possible answer is, “ Nothing at all.”
More Boot-Trunk.
Dear Bulletin. — Re a brilliant display of
police mismanagement in the Boot-Trunk case.
In the first place, you published a letter from
“ Cockney,” commenting on the business friend-
ship that exists between detectives and the
newspaper reporters who seek information from
them. “Cockney” made it clear that the
South Yarra police could easily have unravelled
the Boot-Trunk mystery had they been put in
possession of certain facts known to two rival
cliques of detectives and to the Superintendent.
Then you published a letter from newspaper-
scribe “M‘G.” in defence of the friendly
D’s. “ M.G.,” the individual who contradicts
everything and mixes everything up, said:
“The detectives had nothing even to con-
nect South Yarra with the tragedy—not a
scrap of any information of any value reached
the detectives from first to last.” Surely the
value of information depends upon the intelli-
gence of the persons to whom it is imparted.
The detectives had information which sug-
gested a connection between the boot-trunk,
the girl Dubberke, and a certain house in
South Yarra. They made no good use of
their knowledge. It was valueless—to them.
“ M‘G.” by this time has probably felt
sorry that he offered an apology for bis
detective friends. At the inquest last week
witness Gabriel, the South Yarra youth who
sold drugs and instruments to Tod, and knew
they were to be applied to Mabel Ambrose,
stated : “ Nearly every day after the body was
discovered I used to say to him, ‘ That’s Mabel
in the boot-box, all right, Tod.’ ... I knew
about half-a-dozen other people who chaffed
him . . . it was a general thing.” The detec-
tive who took Tod into custody stated that
Tod, after his arrest, said, “It was very hard
on him the way people chaffed him at South
Yarra, and he had to put up with a lot owing
to the chaff.” In fact—as one of the cross-
examining lawyers remarkedeverybody
knew about it but the detectives,” and
“M’G.’s” letter merely served to justify
“Cockney’s” comments on the contemptible
worshipping of ’tecs by weak-minded news-
paper people.— More Cockney.
When the Bill authoring the construction of
the Byrock-Brewarrina, Rock to Green’s Gun-
yah, and Koorawatha-Grenfell' railways was
before N.S. W. Leg. Council, that body inserted
a proviso that the owners of adjacent lands
should be specially taxed to make good any loss
on the working of the three lines. Wherefore
there is boiling rage in Brewarrina, and large,
wild, hairy men use reprehensible vocabulary
in Green’s Gunyah. They point out that the
N.S. W. railway system has only been one year
without a deficit, and they have helped for
many years to bear other people’s railway-de-
ficits while having no railway of their own, and
now they can only get a line by paying their
own deficit single-handed. Also, considering
that N.S. W. has a very long list of non-paying
lines already, they want to know if it is pro-
posed to make the landowners along these pay
up their shortages also, and if it isn’t, then why
isn’t it ? There is a certain amount of logic
about that last question.

To all appearance, the Queensland police have
about given up hope of fastening the Gatton
murder on to Burgess. Witness the following
dejected item which lately arrived from the
North with its tail between its legs
The police are not confident of winning: their case, when
things come to a head, though they profess to have
evidence which will vindicate the moral guilt of some-
body, even if he cannot be legally convicted. It is said
that, with the evidence they have accumulated, the police
will be able to vindicate their action before the public,
who, as they know, are not at one with them in the
course they are adopting.
It is now beginning to be remembered, too, that
the witnesses described the mysterious man at
the sliprails as wearing a beard, and not only
was Bcthgess a clean-shaved man when arrested,
but he was clean-shaved several days before
the crime—when he went out prospecting with
Mattingley, the Killarney schoolmaster. The
fact that people who reported getting a glimpse
of a bearded man in the dark promptly recog-
nised the clean-shaved Burgess as the bearded
man goes for nothing. The average identifier,
who deserves ten years’ hard labor, would have
sworn that Burgess was the bullock he saw
leaning its head on the fence if that would have
helped the sensation along. Anyhow, if the
case against Burgess falls through—and Bur-
gess has now upwards of a dozen independent
witnesses who prove his alibi from Gatton on
Dec. 25, 26, 27 —what compensation is it pro-
posed to pay him for his wrongful imprison-
ment ? He got two months, nominally for
vagrancy, but really because a police-officer
gave it as his opinion that there were “ other
circumstances ” which warranted his retention.
If there were no other circumstances after all,
how does this matter stand ?
Two Mudgee papers, the Western Post and
Guardian, complain that Judge Docker, when
presiding at the District Court there lately,
kept the business going from 10 a.m. one day
until nearly 1 o’clock next morning, and from
10 a.m. on that latter day till 9 p.m. The
community has left many points of pro-
cedure to the discretion of Judges, trusting
that Judges would in such points behave
with prudence and wisdom ; but members of
N.S. Wales District and Supreme Court
bench have of late years so frequently out-
raged public feeling and prejudiced the
principle of fair trial that it is no longer
safe to trust implicitly to discretion alone.
Parliamentary bridle and rein are wanted. The
evils of protracted court sittings are obvious to
all except the Judges who indulge in them;
and have been so often stamped with public
condemnation that even these, in the interests
of justice, should curb their predilections and
keep the fame of legal administration pure. As
they don’t do it, Parliament should compel
them.
“ Lex ” to The Bulletin ;
Melb. Tocsix says that the Vic. Govt, has cabled to the
Slonial Office for a royal pardon for detective Dckobt
/ore trial. Why such a request should be made is
hardly clear, seeing that Via Govt, seems to have under
the amended “Governor’s instructions ’’ similar powers to
the Crown in such matters, and that pardon even by the
?ueen before trial would hardly be constitutional.
he alleged cable may be imaginary, but there are
rumors about Melbourne that three different requests
to the same effect have been sent to England, alleging
the probable boom in abortions which a conviction would
generate, as a reason for the pardon ; and that a reply
has been received to one of them, stating that nothing
can he done until the Supreme Court has done its share
of the work. Whether the statement is correct or
not, it has been publicly made; and it would
seem to indicate either very little public spirit
or very great jealousy of a very small rival,
that none of the Mefh. newspapers have informed the
public of the truth or otherwise of the statement. It is
hardly in the public interest, or in accordance with Vic,
traditions of Responsible Government, that (as would be
the case if such a cable has been sent) Colonial Office in-
fluence should be invoked by Vic. Cabinet to block the
regular channel of Vic. criminal justice. In fact., it would
be, on a small scale, comparable with Duput b Bill to
shelve the criminal section of the Court of Cassation.

The J6550-reward question was dragged into
the verdict of the Coroner’s jury in the Melb.
boot-trunk case. With splendid audacity the
foreman begged the Coroner to “ bring under
the notice of the Chief Commissioner of Police
the work done by the three detective officers,
This, That, and T’other, and by Senior-Con-
stable Thingummy, whose shrewd forethought
in preserving the head of the deceased for
identification had been the means of bringing to
light a most foul crime.” The jury added this
precious rider with full knowledge of how
the crime had been discovered by the con-
fession of Thkkla Dua BERKK, and hadn’t been
revealed by the pickled head at all. They had
heard counsel engaged in the case make satirical
comments on the detectives’ ignorance of the
jocular suspicions which had been cast at Tod
by his South Yarra friends, and the only con-
ceivable explanation of the foreman’s little
speech is to be found in the foreman’s nation-
ality. His name, Patrick Allen, suggests
Irish origin. The four policemen singled out
for admiration were more or less Irish, like-
wise. All policemen are more or less Irish.
The coroner, whose name is Morrison, in-
formed the jury in cold, Scotch accents that
their uncalled-for postscript to the verdict
would not be officially recorded.
The Color Line.
Come ont from among them and touch not the unclean
thing, and be ye separate, and I will receive you.—
t Corinthians VI., 17.
To your arms ! The countless hordes of Asia
Are a-swarm, a demon world released !
From the camps of Zhengis Khan and Juhal,
Filthy scourings of the putrid East,
Turkoman and Cingalese and Tartar
Gather Southward for the vultures’ feast.
From the pestilent bazaars of Bosra,
And the plague-struck jungles of Lahore,
From the slums and opium-dens of Canton
And the quays of venal Singapore,
From the mountain passes of Malacca
Sets the turbid current towards our shore.
Here the greasy Jew of Vladivostok,
Or a painted houri from Japan ;
There the planter’s slave from Sourabaya,
And the mongrel-bred Manila man ;
Dirty Arab hawkers from Damascus,
Leprous coolies from old Hindostan 1
One by one the army of invasion
Trickles in and spreads abroad and grows.
Stowaways and crew, they land and vanish
Stealthily amid the crowd that flows
Up and down the street, and that, purblinded,
Knows it not —and cares not if it knows.
And the traitors walk among you, smiling,
White of skin, but nigger-black at heart;
Preaching glibly tolerance and pity,
Quoting laws of commerce, rules of mart;
With the callousness of greedy slavers
Listing even God to take their part.
Will you hearken to the canting prattle
Of the lying, conscience-barren band ?
Will you dally till the fetters rattle
On your wrists —or will you fight and stand ?
With the brave Jehovah goes to battle !
To the strong the Lord shall give the land !
Men and Brothers ! Will you let the stranger
Soil Australia’s homes and rob her farms ?
Will you let him kiss her lips and change her
To a harlot flaunting alien charms ?•
White Man’s Land, you cowards, is in danger !
White Man’s Land is calling you to arms !
N. Queensland.
