Boucicault’s fine old drama, “Flying
Scud,” revived at Sydney Lyceum on Satur-
day in the presence of a considerable audi-
ence, is a play surrounded by many recollection*.
The brightest memory attached to it in the time
of the present writer dates hack to the palmy
days of the old, battered Gaiety, that tneatre
which is now gradually jagging out in a neglected
old age, in the seedy back wilderness of Castle-
reagh-street- A lamentable scratch co. hung out
there in those days of auld lang beer—the kind of
co. that always wears its pant* too short, and has
low flapping shoes, and soiled white socks, and a
greenisli tail coat awav up in the small of ita back
to signify that it is a duke of great affluence. The
only exception was Captain Goodge, whose pants
were too long, and wrinkled up on his legs like a
pair of concertinas, and whose bell-topper looked
a* if he had fallen asleep at the table with his hat
in the gas-jet. And, even under these conditions,
the old “ Scud ’’ looked a healthy, vigorous—and
also rather ridiculous—drama Compared with
Frank Harvey’s cow-like plays that graze mildly
in the fields of recent literature, Boucieault’s work
is that of an artist—not of the very best order, but
an artist all the same. He was at least a man
who didn’t sleep in his write, which is a matter of
some importance.
41 41 * *
“ Flying Scud ” differs from most other
dramas in the absence of the fallen girl. Katie
Rideout lies under dark suspicions of having
fallen—in the stable, of all places; and the hero
deserts her, and drinks, and loses most of his pile
to Goodge and Mulligan and Moses Davis and
Chouser at cards ; but later on the dude who was
weeping in the loft because Julia wouldn’t marry
him is able to swear that Katie couldn’t have
fallen without him hearing the thud, so her char-
acter is cleared. The absence of the fallen girl is
a pleasing novelty, anyhow. The other hero also
loses all his money to Goodge and Mulligan and
Chouser and Mo. Davis—old Boucicault had the
gambling mania strongly in his dramas ; and
that ruined character is left to live happily
on his debts for ever afterwards. And, being
himself a thriftless Irishman, Boucicault leaves
Goodge and Mulligan and Chouser and Mo.
Davies without a bean, despite all their vast
robbery, and busy informing on each other
about a forged 1.0. U. None of Boucicault’s
characters are able to hang on to their money,
and the virtue of Thrift isn’t in them.
“ Flying Scud ” ends with the old moral that
the Lord jiastes the sins of the wicked in
His hat lor future reference, consequently
Mo Davies is left in custody with his mate,
Chouser, swearing affidavits against him ; and
Goodge and Mulligan rush across the stage on
their way to Germany with no assets but the
clothes they stand in. And the little cardboard
gee-gee that wins the Derby, after going down on
his head eight times or so, partly rehabilitates the
hero's fortunes, so that he can marry the girl who
was accused of having fallen in the 'stable.
• • • •
The horse that wins the alleged Derby is
a rather weedy animal to look at, but he is the
only seriously unsatisfactory member of the cast.
Miss Helene Burdette gathers another big laurel
as Katie Rideout, and that winsome gin could
hardly be done better than Miss Burdette does
her. Diver’s hero is good all round, barring
his invariable suggestion of nervousness, but it is
difficult to sympathise with the hero who caste off
Katie, and sconis her through the keyhole w hen
she tearfully asks to be let out so that she can
explain everything. Nunn, Hareourt, and Patey,
the most prominent of the four gentlemen who
have aces in their boots, and who live precari-
ously by stiffening horses and forging each other’s
1.0. U., are all satisfactory, with the exception
that the nose of Mo. Davis is shamefully overdone
—no human jxung would trust a man with a car-
buncle that size glowing oil his countenance. Bert
Bailey is always a pleasing individual to meet,
and when his honored white head and his two
shaky old legs brought Diver’s horse in a winner,
and a fear dropjied from his rheumy eye on to the
faithful animal, the popular enthusiasm nearly
scared “ Flying Scud ” out of his wits, and the
curtain went down with the whole company hang-
ing on to that noble ’oss to prevent him wrecking
the theatre. Also Miss Ivy Gorrick may be
classed as reasonably promising, in a somewhat
amateurish sort of way. She looks as if she
hadn’t become fully acquainted with the furni-
ture as yet. The “ Octoroon ” follows at some
date not yet specified.

A scheme is on foot to get up a large, in-
fluential show, or some other useful and saleable re-
cognition, to Messrs. J. and C. MacMahon on their
retirement from Sydney Lyceum a few weeks
hence. The two brief brothers of Bendigo have
kept that establishment oj>en for 20 months
through good luck and bad—mostly rather
bad; and in that time they have given more
dramas at lower prices than were ever given
at a leading Sydney theatre before. Some
of the dramas were bad. and some should have
got six months hard labor, but a great many were
very good. Also, the great bulk of them were
well-mounted and well-played by a very good
company. Incidentally, the smallest brother with
the long top-hat and the other brother have been
instrumental in bringing to the front some very
promising young Australian actors and actresses
who might otherwise have mouldered in ob-
scurity a while longer—the very promising
Miss Helene Burdette being a case in
point. There i* about as much show for a
young Australian mummer in Australia now as
there is for a kookaburra in church, so in this
respect the Mighty Atom and Co. have done
good work. And The Bulletin would like to
know if the influential committee which promises
to recognise the MacMahon Brothers’ services
couldn't get up two recognitions when it is about
it, and let the Lyceum eo. have the cash results
of the other one? It is prepared to take its Bible
oath, or any other drinkable, that the eo. wants
it badly, and deserves it quite as much as the
management.

The Sydney shows are sickbed o’er
this week with the pale tail-end of almost
everything. The [lantomime at Her Majesty’s is
busy dejjositing its last legs in the grave, and
ere tie's© few tasteful remarks are laid before
the public it will lie no more. The Bulletin
drops a tear the size of Fitzgeralds’ circus over
Peggy Pryde, and Gulliver, and the Ass, and
several other people, Miss Franees Adler among
them, and it is sorry to part with the bright blue
sky of Bagdad—about the only clean thing there-
abouts. But considering that the piece plainly
couldn’t hold up any longer, it is just as well
that it should tear itself away. Next week
the famous “Geisha” will receive a careful, in-
telligent, and discriminating notice, with a vein of
poetic fettling running through it, and adorned
with the evidences of a pure taste and an artistic
soul. The present writer is the owner of that soul.

The “English Rose” will be staged at
Sydney Royal on Saturday, “When London
Sleeps ” retiring with dignity after a triumphant

Lieutenant Travis, the greatest ventrilo-
quist on earth, appeared at the Tivoli as agreed
upon, and Sydney has decided it won’t fight about
tne question. If he isn’t the greatest ventriloquist
on earth he is probably near enough to it, and it
isn't worth anybody’s while to get his eye dis-
colored through calling the management a person
who wouldn’t know the truth if he saw it, even if
there is a small discrepancy somewhere. Travis
is certainly in the front rank of his rather dreary
profession, and he and the other professors may
argue the matter out among themselves. Miss
Peggy Pryde still casts her two well-filled stock-
ings and her two small shoes at the stars when the
spirit moves her, and the other people are well
and happy, or succeed in disguising their illness
and misery very well.

Biondi, the versatile gentleman of many
strange noses, has a matinee benefit at the Tivoli
on W ednesday next, when the full strength of the
Rickards co. will help to give him a send-off. The
surprising Italian has constructed a new sketch,
“ The Burglar,” in which, so this paper under-
stands, he breaks into his own house, finds himself
asleep in bed, decides not to disturb himself, then
wakes up and is enraged to see himself in the
room, chases himself, bolts in alarm with himself
in close pursuit, sees himself ahead dressed as a
policeman as well as behind racing in his night-
shirt, and is finally surrounded by himself and
captured with the assistance of a large crowd of
himself, which gathers round attracted by his own
objurgations. Biondi intends ojiening with this
sketch in London, but he will “try it on the
dorg”at the Tivoli on Wednesday. It is hoped
that the dorg will be numerous and appreciative.

Brough, late of the Bijou, seems to have
strengthened his claim upon Melbourne at large,
and the five-shilling section in particular, by
shifting his business to the Princess’s. “The
Adventure of Lady Ursula” was set before a full
house and a gleaming display of naked shoulders
on Saturday night—Lord Brassev’s presence being
thrown in gratis. Also, the public, by vehement
applause, gave the Brough co. to understand that
it had scored what the newspajiers call a
“ veritable triumph,” and which, in truth, was a
medi un sized success. Evidently the Princess’s is
the one and only Melb. theatre where a manage-
ment can expect to run a profitable enterprise on
“ the dollar line.”

“ The Adventure of Lady Ursula ” is a
short four-act comedy (by Anthony Hope), de-
pending a good deal upon its clothes—the gaudy
military uniforms nnd silken society splendors of
A. D. 1700 or thereabouts. Handsome costumes,
fine furniture and fittings, and an artistic assort-
ment of stage pictures make it an interesting
show: the dialogue is bright, and the story is a
slim, elegant fiction of the kind that cheers and
does not inebriate. Lady Ursula bets another girl
six pairs of stockings that she will contrive to
interview a certain Sir George, who has warned all
women off his premises for evermore. Her inten-
tions are strictly feminine, but somehow the
scheme for invading Sir George’s bachelor privacy
drags her brother into a quarrel, and then, with a
view to averting a fatal duel (for Sir George is a
dead sticker, like Julius Knight in “Under the
Red Robe ”), giddy Lady Ursula pretends to be a
boy in a blue court-suit; and Sir George takes her
at her word. He, in the ordinary way, is
a sceptical man of the world, and she is a
pal pallid fraud, with an embarrassed pair of
leffs, but he has to go on mistaking her
sex in the interests of the comedy. Then Sir
< ieorge discovers his awful stupidity by accident,
but the girl, not knowing this, challenges him to
a duel ‘on her own.” Also, she herself is chal-
lenged by one or two warriors who don’t know a
woman when they see one; and, finally, the
nickering little joke is snuffed out by George’s
offer of marriage, which Ursula duly jumps at.
1 lie play has an old-fashioned charm of manner,
*ad will no doubt improve on acquaintance, like
all damty work. There’s nothing in it—but what
is nice.

Brough, as a quaint old parson, Mario
Majeroni, in the lordly capacity of an angry
brother engaged to Miss Temple, together with a
genteel aunt and a few casual officers in the Foot
Guards, represent the necessary padding for the
story, which practically begins and ends at Lady
l rstila and her adventure with the bachelor man.
&he is less convincing than even Hhaks|jeare’s Rosa-
lind, and he commands very little more reverence
than the tenor and baritone heroes of “Dorothy,”
whose best girls are supposed to play the
she-male trick utxm them. An actress needs
to be specially born for the part of a Lady
l rsula, and being born to impersonate a man
she must know how to express the appearance
and feelings of a beautiful 19-year-old lady in
the act of wearing breeches for the first time.
Miss Essie Jenyns ten years ago might have
almost realised the character. Mrs. Brough acts
it fairly effectively. Her face is pretty enough,
she carries sufficient height and not too much
plumpness, and, setting one consideration against
another all through the piece, Mrs. Brough finishes
up with a gixxl balance to her credit. The writer
saw her walk through a burlesque-part in trousers
once upon a time, and can testify that she is much
happier in the knee-breeches of these latter days.
Joseph Carne’s husky monotones tend to make Sir
George seem a somewhat heavy person, but the
actor’s style is manly and pleasant.

The dearth of memory in Melbourne’s
dress-circles was very palpable at the Opera House
last Friday night, when that worn-out tenement of
old associations was about to close its record. One
looked for a hundred or two gray identities in-
dulging their gentle melancholy on the grand tier,
but found not half-a-dozen. The scene of Lister’s
ambitious opera ventures ; the place where “ Tam-
bour Major ” gave George Musgrove his start in
life, and where jealous Greer committed murder
and suicide; where Emilie Melville, George Rig-
nold, and ** Paul Jones ” flourished of yore, and
Jonel Sullivan got drunk, was allowed to pass
away in silence. With very few exceptions the
very few old people who attended at the death
of the historic o[>era House had seemingly come
there to see a variety show, without a thought for
the things that were and might have been in the
days of auld lang syne.

The Rickards co. had a great liotrse-
warming at the Bijou on Saturday, and the advan-
tages of the new pitch from the performers’ point
of view were generally recognised by the people in
front. Songs sound louder, and all sorts of turns
are better displayed to the far-off portions of the
audience than at the late Opera House. The
gallery gets fully 75 per cent, more value for its
six|>ence, anyway. A new background for the
“first part,” a small boy soprano, and a rapid
little pantomime by the Lorenzi-Phillips party,
gave an air of freshness to the Bijou show, which
was otherwise brought along from the other place.
Boy Daley is a taking item; the new setting is
the Cadden Woods scene from “ Little Minister,”
and the knockabout business of the matured
Brethren and Sistern (who were lately apjtearing
in Sydney) is quite as lively and almost as
humorous as the lunacies of the revered Poluskis
and Haytors.

At Fitzgerald Bros.’ Circus remarkable
Ajax is just now punching his lungs and liver
with his feet, to the delight of a more or less
crowded tent. The circus boom slackens a little
once in a way, but as a rule the multitude rolls up
with the same old roll. Bar Tigress Kitty, who
has been growling considerably at her mate, the
horse, this last week or two, the menageriejseems
happy; also it is understood that Kitty merely
shows her teeth for fun; she means no harm.
The steeplechase doesn’t mean any harm either,
but three times a night on an average a jockey
parts company with his bounding steed, and the
possibility of somebody getting hurt is a great
public attraction.

At the time of Deeming’s conviction, the owner of his
house in Andrew-street, Windsor, opened the place for
exhibition at 6d. a head, and raked in the shekels hand-
somely for a few days. Scenting a repetition of this, the
police blocked the owner of Mine. Radalyski's house in
South Yarra, pointing out that he would be liable to
prosecution for running an unlicensed theatre, or some-
thing of the sort; so the poor old fellow, who never had
any intention of exhibiting the place, was scared badly.
Anyhow, it would be only fair under such circumstances
to give the owner a chance of making a few pounds.
After a tragedy has occurred in a house it is long damned
for letting purposes.
Those five young lions in a cage at Fitzgtiald Bros.’
menagerie are sold to a Hamburg dealer, and will be
aed presently. The breeding of lions is a most pro-
e native industry, it seems, and if the Fitzses could
only do it on a large scale they wouldn’t trouble to run
their circus at all. Poor John Burns, the noble-looking
beast with the two blind eyes (one of which developes an
abscess and bursts at painful intervals), is not a success
as a sire. In fact he hasn't managed to achieve the joys
of paternity thusfar. As for the lion who squats on the
elephant’s back, he is doomed to live a monkish life un-
disturbed by female society. The fellow who begets the
cubs is a comparatively wild, untamed Lothario with a
particular fancy for having his offspring born in Maori-
land.
Doubtful whether Ada Crossley scored a point at Clara
Butt's expense when she took the place of her indisposed
rival on the first day of the Leeds Festival, and obtained
fine notices for her singing. Wily Clara seized the oppor-
tunity to present the Australian girl with a turquoise-
and-pearl bangle, which, when you come to think of it,
was a means of letting the public know, per medium of a
hundred gushful paragraphists, that Clara considers her-
self in a position to patronise her dear friend Ada.

butions. Publicity has not yet turned its ‘ bulla-eye' on

manager,” continues our correspondent, “ has been un-
able to recognise his signature on bills to the amount of

near relation is reported to be the absent-minded person
who forgot his own Christian name, and the conundrum
now is, ‘ Will this rising man remember he p ave these
bills himself, or must a jury decide that hts relative
should retire into seclusion out of reach of those too-
obliging money-lenders who are sometimes so criminally
careless of the signatures to such bills ? ’ ”
Walter Lacy, an actor of repute, who was an Australian
ere he took to the boards, died lately in England, aged
90 years. About the thirties he was a Govt, medical
officer in V.D.L. He was on board the Govt, brig
Cyprus, in 1829, when the vessel was seized by con-
victs. The crew, soldiers nnd firemen were landed on an
island where they remained some days until rescued by
the Georgiana, an Indiaman on a voyage to Bombay with
troops. Later, Lacy was wrecked off Swan River,
W.A., and after that mishap he concluded that Australia
did not want him. He threw physio to the dogs, went to
England and took to the stage, his first appearance being
as Count. Montallmn in the “Honeymoon,” at the old
Theatre Royal, Edinburgh, in 1834, and his last as Colonel
Damns at the Lyceum, in April, 79.

The Ballarat Woollen Mills lately called for
competitive designs and tenders from builders of steam
engines, that of the Austral Otis Engineering Company,
South Melbourne, being accepted. These engines are of
the compound surface condensing type, with cylinders
17" and 38" by 60” stroke, and will indicate 700 h.p. on a
consumption of lftlb. steam per hour. The design is a
handsome and massive one, and they are probably the
finest example of engine-building yet produced in the
colony.*

Adelaide, and Perth, the sole agent in Australia for J. and

ice-making machinery, is at present showing a large stock
of these machines, which have been got ready for the
summer season. The cost of refrigerators to' produce

N.S.W. doesn’t build railways now with anything like the
Mjror of the old days, and the Reid Government
didn’t inherit the vast mass of unfinished public works
which its predecessor did, and so wasn’t driven to a
stupendous outlay of loan-money in its early days. The fact
that Dibbs finished, in three years, 307 miles of railway
left on his hands by his predecessor, against Reid’s 190
miles m four years, accounts for a good deal of the for-
mer’s loan outlay. But the present N.S.W. Government
paid last year about £500,000 less of the public works
expenditure out of revenue than its predecessor’s averag’ei
£500,000 of small, unreproductive works which used to be
paid out of revenue are now charged to loans; and the
loan account is only kept down by building fewer produc-
tive works. This sort of thing is called economy by people
who only look at the totals and don’t inquire about
details—which is the reason why The Bulletin barracks
for a separate Public Works Fund and a separate state-
ment of ordinary expenditure.
• • « *
The new year opened gaily in the Australian
loan business. South Australia has already floated a 3
per cent. p.n. for £1,500,000, about £1,100,000 being to
redeem old loans, and the balance for new works. After
allow ing for expenses it gets about £94 for each £lOO, so
there is a dead loss of £90,000 on the transaction, and the
interest, allowing for loss at maturity of loan, is about 3i
per cent The only good points about the transaction
are that there is a saving of interest, the old loans being
at 4 per cent, and that the new loan is repayable only at
the option of the Government, so that it won’t have to
make another big loss on renewal. When S.A. borrowed
that £1,100,000 originally, some 24 years ago, it made a
loss of £9 on every £lOO, and as it makes another loss of
£6 per £lOO on the renewal it really owes £lOO where it
only received £B5. For every £9l ‘it originally received
it has paid back £ll3 in interest, and it has just
paid another £6 in discount, and it still owes £lOO. It
can’t repay the £lOO in any case till 1916, and by that
time it will have paid another £6l in interest. So it will
by 1916 have paid £l7O interest and discount for the use
of £9l which it probably spent in building a railway
that has been a dead loss ever since.
It is also announced from London that Maoriland
is floating a £1,000,000 3 per cent loan with a prospect of
getting about c 96. Apropos of which this suggestion is
respectfully offered to the Seddon Government. The
average bank-note circulation in Maoriland is about a
million—that is to say, there are about £1,000,000 of notes
for which the people don’t seem to want gold.
If the State chose to abolish this private note cir-
culation and replace it with £1,000,000 worth of State
notes which would be legal tender for all purposes,
including the payment of taxes to the Government, there
wouldn't be the slightest difficulty in keeping them at
par. The only trouble about a legal tender note-issue is
the tendency to force the country to take more notes
than it actually wants—to overdo the thing, in fact, just
as one may overdo borrowing, or railway-building, or
drink, or anything else. But if it isn’t overdone there is
no shadow- of objection to it, and so long as the Treasury
is willing to receive the notes in payment of taxes they
could hardly fall below- par even if the circulation was
£1,500,000. Canada keeps its State paper in circulation
by the simple process of making every bank hold 40 per
cent, of its cash reserve in Government notes, and the
scheme w-orks well enough. The legal tender notes of the
United States circulate freely and keep up to par simply
because they are received in payment of Government
taxes, and the Government pays them out again to
satisfy its obligations. As a cheap and perfectly harm-
less device for raising £1,000,000 w-ith which to construct
Maori land's three great unfinished railways a State note-
issue has no equal. Moreover, a note-issue is always leak-
ing awa.v through loss and accidental destruction of the
flimsy paper, so a good deal of it never turns up for
redemption, and the demand for paper currency would
increase as population increased, so the original million
wouldn't he the limit by any means. The only possible
objection is that the idea doesn’t look quite English, but
even in England the Bank of England note is legal tender
within certain limits. And floating loaus abroad isn’t in
the least degree English, either.

Apropos of a statement in the “ Wild-Cat ’’column
last week that the Mutual Life Insurance 00. of New
York, during 1898, increased the amount of its life assur-
ance in force by £7,203,000, the largest increase for one
company during the year, the great New York Life Insur-
ance 00. desires respectfully but firmly to put its oar in.
What the New York Life Insurance Co. desires to say is
that its life assurance in force at the end of 1898 was
£13,800,000 more than at the end of 1897, and that its
increase is the largest for the year by a long way. The
assertion looks quite probable, too, on the figures.

The Equity Trustees, Executors and Agency
00. (Melb.) shows signs of a sudden and amazing revival.
That institution, being apparently clothed and in its right
senses, states that it has, out of profits, written £240 off
the paper-value of its Victorian Government and Mel-
bourne Board of Works debentures, thereby bringing
them down to their present market value ; has provided
£l4 for depreciation of furniture ; and has a net profit of
£2770 left. It pays a 74 per cent, dividend (£938), adds
£5OO to reserve, making it £1250, and the remaining 1582,
with previous balance, leaves £2992 at credit of profit and
loss. Which is so unlike some of the former doings of the
Equity Trustees, Executors and Agency that its past re-
cord is worth looking up :
Net profit. Dividend.
June, 1892.. ..£287 10 per cent. =£1250
December, 1892 .. 2195 „ = 1250
June, 1893 .... 152 „ = 1250
December, 1893 .. 99 Nil.
June, 1894 .. .. 361 Nil.

June, 1895.. .. 556 „ = 625

December, 1898 .. 2770 7J „ = 938
_ . , £11,421 £9688
Therefore the last half-year’s profits are distinctly the
best that the Equity Trustees has shown since the boom-
days. It has recovered all the leeway it made by declar-
ing five dividends which were not earned, and is £1733
better off than it was before the sorrowful times of 1893.
All it wants now is careful management and a rigid re-
solve to live well within its means. In the good old
times the Equity Trustees paid massive dividends and
never even attempted to create a reserve—its first small
effort at building one up was made in 1893. If it resolves
now to amass a strong reserve, it has a fine opportunity
to start fair, for it has no dubious-looking assets to
write off. The bulk of its funds are in State and muni-
cipal securities, and it would do wisely to realise the £5988
out on mortgage and put it there likewise. Also, bv the
way, the habit of lumping together interest received‘from
the investment of the shareholders’ money and ordinary
business profits ought to be severely sat upon ; and at
least £2OOO of the profit balance should be transferred to
reserve lest the board be tempted to resume 10 per cent
dividends with it next half-year.
* * * *
The Assets Realisation and General Finance Co.
(Sydney) still works hard at realising its own assets with
moderate success. In 1896 the total loss was £15,192 •in
1897 it was £2162 ; in 1898 it was £lBl4. The largest
asset is now “ Bad Debts and Depreciation, £69,284,” and
the fourth largest asset is “ Unpaid Calls (of which about
£22,500 is bad), £25,296.” Between the admitted loss and
the calls which are irrecoverable, the total bad debts are
£91,784. The liabilities to the public are now reduced to
£40,136, so, on paper, there ought to be £53,000 for the
shareholders, or about 7s. in the £ of the capital which
they put into the luckless concern. But as the net earn-
ings don’t nearly cover working expenses, the prospects
of the shareholders are growing smaller every year, and
assets are always liable to realise only a fraction of their
P a per value. Still, by vigorous calling up of capital, the
liabilities to depositors and others are being reduced very
fast, and in consequence the loss is growing smaller each
year. Its last dividend was 10 per cent., paid in 1892.

Sydney and Suburban Hydraulic Power Co.
made a gross profit of £11,530 for 1898—its best record,
so far. Eight years’ profits have been as follows :
1891 .. .. £654 1895 .. .. £6,536
1892 .. .. 3,125 1896 .. .. 7,209
1893 .. .. 4,384 1897 .. .. 10,016
1894 .. .. 4,740 1898 .. .. 11,530
AlO per cent, dividend absorbs £7500; £1450 is added to
the reserve for renewal of plant, making it £4OOO ;
£1250 is written off for depreciation, and the re-
maining £1330, added to previous balance, leaves
£3033 at credit of profit and loss. Things are looking re-
markably well with the Hydraulic Co., which is a good
reason why it should make a start now at writing off that
paper asset—“ Parliamentary Rights and Concessions,
£15,000,” which is simply goodwill under another name.
A co. whose visible assets, as set forth in its balance-sheet,
don t equal its liabilities, and which, squares the account
with a goodwill item in any form, is a thing which ought
to seriously consider its ways, however well affairs are
going.

The Watson’s Bay Ferry Co. (Sydney), the
little co. with the asthmatic green boat, made an unusual
profit of £570 during 1898, and after putting £285 to a
reserve account the big accumulated loss is reduced from
£6247 to £4962. In 1897 the co. made only £34, so it
reckons the disaster has lifted a little. If things keep on
as they are doing the old losses wall be paid off in about
17 years, and then the co. might possibly begin to pay
dividends—only the prospective tramway will have killed
it long ere then.

Specimen of Bendigo’s sticking. The Macduff
has been call-making for over 20 years. Time after time
shares have been unsaleable ; of 28,000, 3000 were thrown
on the company, which could find no buyer for them.
Then a reef was cut at 1250 ft. ; shares jumped past £l,
and from the first crushing (1711 oz. from 23 tons) a div.
was possible. Mr. G. hansel] is said to hold 13,000 shares,
but Mr. F. Clark, the original pegger-out of over a
quarter of a century back, is still chairman.

Long Tunnel Extended (Vic.) half-yearly meet-
ing. Chairman said the offer for the mine was something
like £35 a share—£27 cash on the nail, and shares which,
on present production, would give them about 30s. a year
in divs., instead of £5 4s. as now. Another shareholder
worked the offer out as equal to £4l 13s. 4d. per share,
and intimated that if £43 10s. cash was offered it might be
accepted. Mine undoubtedly' looking well. By an acci-
dental encroachment, made and admitted by the parent
mine, the lode has been proved as good as ever 200 ft.
below the mine’s own workings, and it will take two or
three years to reach that point. Market value, £3O a
share. Betting even that the buyers will come along
with £43 10s.

The New Moon Co. (Bendigo), during its last
year, paid £16,800 from stone averaging 7dwt. 19gr. in
one half-year and 7dwt. 9gr. in the other ; the latter
half-year’s return was 45890 z. from 11,728 tons. This is
among the best results of the year. The co.’s balance-
sheet, lately issued, shows how the thing is done. Mine
timber cost only £lBO ; surveying, £4 4s. ; and, especially,
management, £139.

Black Horse —the grand old mine of Ballarat—
comes to the front again at last. Shares have lately'gone
from 50s. to 87s. 6d., and it will not surprise some people
if they see ±'B. A div.—the forerunner of many—will
probably be paid this week. It has about a mile of
ground, has come on an entirely new make of stone run-
ning over an ounce per ton, has plenty ground opened
out, and, in the opinion of a well-known mining-manager
who saw it last week, is again one of the few great mines
of Vic.

* * * *
A private cable says the value of copper is really
£2 to £3 above the market quotations—now just on £7O.
It will prohably be found that one or two of the world’s
biggest producing mines begin to look shaky in the
bottom. Is it the Anaconda? If so, copper may go to
£BO. Some copper magnates say that but for this mine
the French copper syndicate would not have collapsed.
At that time the Anaconda could, without loss, land
copper in London under £3O a ton.

The copper-mining activity in S.A. has led to
the digging up of the glittering statistics of Moonta and
other famous local mines. Thus : Value of ore from
Moonta before ’9O, £5,113,252; ditto from Wallaroo,
£2,229,096; ditto since the amalgamation, £1,876,134
Grand total, £9,218,428. This is not inclusive of ’9B,
whose returns are yet incomplete. Divs. have amounted
to £1,702,254, of which £104,000 has been paid since the
amalgamation in ’9O.
The old Burra mine, too, abandoned rather
mysteriously nearly 20 years ago, is showing life again.
One diamond drill bore gave fair prospects, another is
being started, some tributors have meantime gone on, and
other ground near by is being turned over. The Burra
and surrounding mines are freeholds.

Lyell Tharsis cash-balance on 30th Nov. was
£2344. The aerial ropeway for the delivery of ore to the
big mine should be completed by end Feb. The ropeway
cost £4299 to date of sheet. The Tharsis provided one of
the surprises of the Lyell field, as it was just down to the
last bean when a secondary’ formation of pay-dirt was
blown open. The Knox-Orr galaxy would have sold the
Tharsis lease, woodheap, postages, and prospects for a
fiver a week before manager Williamson struck 6 per cent,
copper stuff.

Steadily the buddies disappear from the concen-
trating mills at the Broken Hill mines, though some of
them were put in only a few months hack, and vanners
mostly on German models, take their place. In fact,’
Block 14, which had just arranged for more buddies, re-
jected them at the last moment in favor of the newer con-
trivance. All these frequent, changes make it absolutely
necessary that oo’s. should make regular allowances for
depreciation in their balance-sheets—either that., or charge
additions to these tentative plants to ordinary working
account. Co’s, that only make a depreciation-debit when
directors darn well please (and several of them do that)
simply humbug shareholders.

The British B.H. continues to make up lost
ground by liberal extractions of carbonates, for which
lock 14 is a ready buyer. But the mine up there that
can’t get through on sulphides is as good as lost.

The much-talked-of sample parcel of ore from
Grasmere copper-mine, Wilcannia wav, consisted of
18cwt., which cost £5 to land in Broken Hill. Thence it
was sent to Wallaroo, where it was paid for on a 12 per
cent, basis, 2i per cent, being deducted for the gross of
Ilk* per cent, shown by wet assay. Silverton Tram-
way Co. doesn’t charge for anything less than 3 tons
(at least, it doesn’t charge less for 18cwt. than it does
for 3 tons), and as sampling and weighing cost £1 Is., and
a few other disproportionate items were thrown in the
little lot means a debit balance of £3—without reckoning
cost of mining. But still, in view of the small parcel
handled, the figures other than those as to assay value are
to be accepted cautiously.
Cobar notes : Cobar Cold Mines, Ltd., as sleepy
as ever re arrangements for the big start. They have ap-
plied for six months’ exemption from labor conditions,
and got three months, alt hough the warden, on a previous
occasion, said that he would grant no more. At the rate
things are going, shareholders had better bequeath their
interests to their grandchildren. Chesney talks
definitely of smelting. Is opening up, and proposes
widening the old shaft instead of sinking a new one. The
old shaft is slap-down on the lode. Why not sink a new
one ? The mine is all right, hut needs proper handling,
and a good shaft means much for the future. Young
Australia has almost cyanided its valuable tailings-heap
another week or so will see it done. As a gold-show, its
race is run, and, to all appearances, nothing has bees
done to develop its copper formation. No wonder the
shares are coining into Sydney market again. The
Great Western has abandoned its one sane notion, i.e.,
to continue sinking its new shaft, and has gone to work
rooting fora rich patch. Shares, a shilling buyers.
Occidental spending heaps in enlarging its tank, which,
when filled with water, will keep things going. Additions
to cyanide plant proceeding, and when rain comes the co.
can expect something like a return.

The Great Blayney mine —the old Annandale —
is being put into shape. A 100-ton smelter is under
way, power being provided to drive three such. The
mine itself is strongly believed in by its owners as a very
big thing indeed ; also the co. considers it has an un-
rivalled stand for custom-smelting.
• • * *
Wentworth Mines, N.S.W., reported in London
five weeks back that the month’s production was 1020 oz
In this there should be some profit. Why are companies
allowed to keep their returns secret from the public?
They are lessees of public property. The land is freehold
in this case but the gold is reserved to the Crown. Pub-
licity can do no possible injury and might help along the
industry in the district.
“It is reported from Parkes that the Bush-
man’s mine will soon be in full swing again.” Very un-
likely that owner Dick will tackle the job again. He has
plenty of money and pluck, but he is a man upon whom
experience is never exactly lost. The Bushman’s appears
to be unpayable.

The most notable instance of what cyanide has
done for Maoriland mining is the Waihi mine. For years
the same show was dependent on battery treatment ; it
existed miserably on a 6dwt. average, and did not pay a
single div. In those days it was known as the Martha.
Then a new crowd got hold of it, introduced cyanide, and
it is now turning out a monthly average of £25,000
bullion.
“ Where is the I van hoe gold ? ” After turning
out big- cakes every month for over a year, the directors
announce a ss. div.—half of what was expected. Has any
other co. been financed with it? Is the mine so extrava-
gantly managed in London as not to be able to pay more,
or is the directorate anxious to bring down price of shares?
Estimated that £BO,OOO is unaccounted for, and the bal-
ance-sheet is eagerly awaited.
• • • •
Again something wrong with the works —Rone-
mann’s. The inventor himself has been laid-up—suffering
the effects of a dose of cyanide fumes, which won’t stand
any monkeying with. But what has cyanide to do with

things in Westralia?
“ Grumballa ”: D. L. Doolette’a statement
(14 1’99) that Brookman and Pearce dollied ICoz. from
cap of present Boulder lode is all rot. That lode does not
anywhere show above the surface, and its existence was
unknown till Dave O’Neil struck it in a costeen consider-
ably later than Sept., ’93. Same man found present lode
in Lake View, which was also pretty “ wild ” when floated
in Adelaide.
A find of rich alluvial gold reported from Pil-
barra, Westralia. Two men dollied 750 z. from some loose
quartz, and some natives got 720 z. The good thing was
kept, quiet till some 600 oz. were got—one slug almost all
gold turned the scale at 231 b. Then the rush started, and
another sensational find is reported in the neighborhood.

■ Chief Justice Way was so very ill last week
that Jews, Catholics, Protestants and Mahomme-
dans in Adelaide started praying for him ; to so
comprehensive a petition a favorable answer was
almost assured. The little chief will be <l3 in
April. There is a general hope that his recent
breakdown will induce him, in future, to divest
himself of his ornamental and talkative offices, so
that he may contiue himself to his judicial duties.

The recent gruesome coach accident near
Port Macquarie (N.S.W.) is a whole sermon on
the cold, calculating cussedness of inanimate
objects. The road was a lonesome one ; probably
there were not more than 10 minutes in the 24
hours when a tree cduld fall with a certainty of
hitting somebody. The tree was probably 100
years old ; it had been waiting its opportunity
ever since the days of the First Fleet and taking
careful ami all the time. It was the last run that
®°*« h was going to make ; also, the driver had
handed in his resignation, and it was bis final trip.
Ihe coach would have passed with half a minute
to spare, only the solitary bagman on the roof
grew tired, and just ten yards away the driver
{railed up so that he could get down and go in-
side. And that stoppage fitted the time in so
accurately that the tree killed both baginan and
driver. It was about the most deadly accurate
tree on record.

About 18 months ago in a Q. country
district, a young man was tried for attempted
rape before a District Court Judge and a jury of
12 of the most intelligent residents. After a short
consultation the jury agreed and returned to
oourt with a verdict of guilty and a strong recom-
mendation to mercy ; but when the Judge asked
on what grounds they recommended mercy the
foreman could give no reason. One of the other
jurymen, however, spoke up to the effect that
they recommended prisoner to mercy on the
ground that they were not certain he teas there
when the offence teas committed .” The Judge
stared and sent them hack again, and after a little
guilty F d V^ y they . brought in a verdict simply of
guilty. The sapient juror in question is now
administering “justice” on his own behalf Ja

* • • •
The attempt to continue the Boot-Box in-
quest on Foundation Day was severely jumped on
and collapsed miserably. This possibly because
nearly all parties concerned are Australian
natives. Tod Radalyski, and Dubberke (the
three prisoners), the dead girl, Mabel Ambrose,
detectives Dungey and O’Donnell, coroner
Morrison the coroner’s orderly, the two lawyers
for the defence, and 24 out of the 27 witnesses
cafied all claim Australia for their birthplace.
Ihe boot-box affair, it will be admitted, is emi-
nently creditable to Australia. Every true-born
Australian should be proud of it.
• * • *
Of the famous boot-box trio Madame
Radalyski appeals to one most. Deep furrows
underline her eyes, and over her ashen face a blur
rests like a veil, but behind it one can trace a sym-
pathetic trait very common amongst stone-deaf
people. Iraviee Tod is a small man, with small
shrewd blaok eyes and thick lips. Thekla
Dubberke now and then seems to possess the face
of an angel. Fair, with blue eyes, a beautiful
P[°™V talI > BVolte figure, she would make an
ideal Marguerite. Dress her as a mm, and the
passionless innocence of her expression would fill
a Jos. Symes with religious fervor, but if you look
closely into the light eyes and thin iips you
tremble as you realise that in her coquettish fancy
there may rest to a considerable degree the fate of
her comrades m crime.

and he was captured, drag-

for no visible offence—simply
as a suspected person. All
of which is justice, as un-
derstood in Botany Bay.
lhe “plucky civilian”
went on his way rejoic-
ing, and was comnli-
men ted in the papers.

got three months
had committed no
overt offence, while
the man who cap-
tured him had. There
is n law againstcarry-
ing loaded weapons
in a public place.

NIGHT’S DAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
All undisturbed by conscience pangs
Your taintless life to mine I knit;
So sunbeams on a muddy pool
Rest goldenly unstained by it.
You see me fair ; I know me foul;
But, without foul, naught old you view.
Impure, I give you purest joy—
And yet the world will pity you.
But no apologies. I give
My contrast to your perfect light.
I frame in bold, becoming black
Your pictured soul drawn all in whita
If in the end good oonquers ill,
Has evil not its right to be ?
So pardon me my sins, sweet saint,
And I’ll forgive—youi purity.

•’• * •
The Burgess identification business con-
tains a tou of invaluable morals. Burgess has
been identified in three or four different towns as
the man who committed such-and-such a mis-
demeanor on such-and-such a date; everyone
knows him and his bald pate, and could swear to
him a mile off. Every petty crime perpetrated in
South Q. for the past six months was committed
by him or his twin brother—in fact, he was some-
times guilty of two offences on the same day in
towns hundreds of miles apart. . . . And now the
man has the cheek to produce almost indubitable
evidence that two-thirds of the parties identifying
him have made either liars or fools of themselves.
And the gods grin on, as usual.

More Gatton theory. “ 8.E.” writes :
Nathless “D. Busst’s ” jibe (B. 28 l/’99), it is very
probable that Harry Stockdale is right in surmising that
the Gatton murderer (if only one) was naked. The sug-
gestion recalls a crime committed many years ago in
Ireland, out of which arose a curious point affecting
the much-discussed question of identification. A girl was
found under a hedge, dreadfully mutilated and quite
dead. Some yards away the searchers came upon a man
stretched face to the ground, with his head badly fractured,
evidently by a big blood-stained stone which‘was found
lying beside him. He was still alive, but so injured that no
hopes were entertained of his recovery. The impossi-
bility of his having been the murderer was soon apparent,
and suspicion fell upon one Mulqueen, whom the girl was
known to have jilted. But proof was wanting, and
meanwhile the other victim recovered sufficiently to
talk. He said that, taking a short cut, he had
come upon a man, stark naked, standing over the
woman’s body with a knife in his hand. At his approach
the murderer fled, and he gave chase, only to be
tripped and head-battered by his quarry before they had
gone far. It was a dark night, and he acknowledged to
having caught only an imperfect glimpse of his assailant’s
face, but thought he would recognise him again. Mul-
queen was brought in, and instantly recognised as a man
who had passed him on the road earlier in the evening.
But now comes the eurious part, for the witness posi-
tively refused to swear that lie was the man he en-
countered in the field, because, as he said, “This man is
dressed and the other wasn’t, and I can't he sure ! ” The
presence, or absence, of clothes made such a difference
that the identification fell through, for ere th* experi-
ment of stripping Mulqueen could be tried, the witness
died from his injuries, and the Crown’s most vital evi-
dence went with him. Mulqueen got off at the trial ; hut
another murder was subsequently proved against him,
and before hanging-day he confessed to having killed the
girl, and that he had taken off his clothes, to avoid blood-
splashes, long ere the girl arrived, and hidden (nude) in
the hedge till she appeared on the road.
• • • *
Be Gatton : If everybody had his rights,
some of those good people who so readily identified
Burgess as the man at the slip-rails, &e., would
get a lot of gaol. When Michael Murphy
turned off that road into Moran’s paddock,
he did so of his own free will and without
hesitation or resistance on the part of his sisters.
It was part of the night’s programme. Also, the
slip-rails had been left down for the purpose. The
idea that a fourth party forced them to turn into
Moran’s paddock at the point of the revolver or
rifle, or induced them by a lying story, is unten-
able. When the weapon was levelled at him the
driver of the sulky would have stopped at leasb
till he was told where to go to, or he would have
stopped to hear the specious tale which led him
astray ; but there was no sign of even a momentary
pause ; only the clear track of an absolutely free
and natural turning, done without undue haste or
undue hesitation. The sulky only held three
with difficulty, and there was no room for that
party who, it is supposed, might have got a lift
and shot Michael from behind. The whole evi-
dence points to the fact that the party turned into
the fatal paddock because they had long before
arranged to do so. They didn’t even have to stop
to let down the slip-rails, because they were
already down. They had been lowered by the
man who was waiting there —by the man who was
expected to be waiting there. There was no row
or outcry on the road because nothing happened
on the road. The man at the rails was known to
them all—he wasn’t a stranger like Burgess, and
no one was startled at his sudden appearance.
The rug was spread on the ground quite deliber-
ately. And here The Bulletin’s theory ends (so
far as print goes) for the present.
• * * *
The bane and the antidote growing from
the same root are well illustrated in the Burgess
photo, business. The aid which the photo, gave
some to identify him as the probable Gatton mur-
derer it gave others to identify him as the sun-
downer who was miles away from Gatton at the
time of the crime.

There is a regular boom in the Bananaland
“orror” business. T’other day, a party went
out to the scene of a certain gruesome tragedy in
S.Q.; inspected the place; shivered a joyful,
little, horrible shudder; then . . . brought out
their baskets ; spread materials for a banquet,
and had a pleasant picnic only a few yards away
from the spot that has become so hideously famous
in the last few weeks.

From Brisbane Courier (3/2/’99) :
Townsville, February 2.
At the Police Court to-day Barboo, an Indian, was
committed for trial for an attempt to commit a Criminal
assault on a respectable married woman after breaking
into her house at Ross Island. Ayer, a Cingalese, was
also committed for trial on a charge of entering the house
of the Salvation Army and indecently assaulting a woman.
Three Japanese women were charged at the Police
Court this morning with having no visible means of
support, and were discharged on promising to leave
within a week. Immediately on the action of the police
the rest of the Japanese women here cleared to Charters
Towers.
Also, Tommy from Tanna and Mat from Malli-
colo have been running amok amongst the white
women of Bundaberg (Q..). Two more lepers have
been “ discovered ” at Mackiy, and another
woman has gone to the lazarette in Moreton Bay.
• • * *
A Thursday Island horror. Two white
children were recently vaccinated in that parti-
colored Island, and the discovery was made shortly
afterwards that they were suffering from leprosy.
Meantime lymph had been taken from the diseased
pair and used to vaccinate others. Terrific efforts
(as usual) to hush the matter up. Hard predica-
ment; for a Thursday Island parent. He vaccin-
ates his youngsters to guard against small-pox—
and discovers he’s filled them up with leprosy
bacilli.

A correspondent :
The curious ask by what singular coincidence it is that,
within three short months, the two Andrews brothers
have been appointed to leading positions on Melb. Hos-
pital stuff. I)r. W. Andrews, of whom the public had
previously heard very little, was appointed to the much-
coveted post of “ honorary ” by the back-door of pro. tern.
appointment by the committee, and now one of that very
committee, the dreamy T. R. Andrews, has been given
the powerful post of secretary. Among the rejected
was R. C. Norman, secretary of the Alfred Hospital.

Be the barbarous rot of Salvation Army
wedding-rites. The connubial corroboree lately
conducted by the Melb. S.A. buss—grotesque
enough, in all conscience- was quite a civilised
function compared with a ceremony recently wit-
nessed in a Gippsland township. It was a mining
centre, and every man and boy not on “night-
shift” attended at the barracks, with, of course,
the whole female population. The bride, a
lanky, flat-chested young thing of 35 or so, sat
on a raised platform opposite the groom—a
stumpy little chap, mostly black beard and
squint. Widower, too, with five children. The
brethern and sistern, fully equipped with
their devil-scarers, sat round, and a song,
fandango, and mandoline accompaniment by a
gymnastic staff-captain opened proceedings. The
overflow audience on the tin-roof having kicked
themselves cripply in applause, the visiting chief
read selections from the C.E. marriage service,
interpolating bad puns and clumsy jokes where
the text gave an opening. Coming at length to
the “ Wilt thou have this woman ? ” &c. “ Yes,”
whispered the groom, making a movement to seize
the desired one’s hand. “Ah, but you haven’t
got her yet,” interposed the jocular high priest,
and (with an expressive wink at the gallery),
“what would you do if she said ‘No?’’’ The
bridegroom scratched his pate while the audience
roared and whistled ; but the bride, looking more
wishful than ever, promptly climaxed the crisis by
stating firmly that she “ would have him.” And
she did.

Rationalist, declares that Parlia-
ment has outlived its usefulness,
and consequently must soon go.
But the fact that an institution has
out-lived its usefulness is no proof
that it must soon go. Look at the
pulpit !

At Ballarat, the other day, one Edwards,
whilst acting as bearer at a funeral, suddenly let go
his end and fell to the ground, bringing the coffin
down with him. Edwards was dead. You never
know your own funeral.
"MANY ARE SENT, BUT FEW ARE
CHOSEN."
A BULLETIN contributor dreams of the waste-paper
basket.
The duel between S. M. Herald and
Sydney D.T. for early Govt, information, which
ended in a fist-fight under the Stuart-Dalley re-
gime, threatens to recrudesce. On the former oc-
casion the Herald was Govt.’s white-haired boy ;
now Premier Reid holds his good winking eye to
the D.T., and the emotionless glass one to the
rival angel.
At Colac (Vic.), the other day, a bold, bad
man attaoked two ladies. One subsided with
wild cries, but the other, a spinster and quite
inexperienced, went for the assailant, knocked
him endways, scratched him, kicked him, and
tore his chin whiskers, and then twisted her scarf
about his neck and choked him till he was purple
in the face and his eyes resembled glass drawer-
knobs. The police arrived about this time, and
the bad man was afforded the protection of the
law.

“ Acdile ” : Re Sydney ’busmen. I rode
in a ’bus the other evening to the
railway-station, down George-
street. There were about 22
passengers, picked up here
and there. Driver had to
pilot his ’bus through the
crowded traffic, watch for pas-
sengers to right and left, take
the money inside and out,
give change, and see that no-
body missed payment. I
handed 6d. through the hole,
and found that the ’bus-driver
had put my change in his
mouth—as an extra hand, I sup-
pose—and handed it back to me nicely
moistened with tobacco-spittle. I suggest
that ’bus-boys be employed, or that a special
class of bus-man be bred with four eyes and
four hands, so that he may watch the traffic,
keep an eye on intending passengers, and
give change without half swallowingit first.
Big Donald M‘Donald, the Argus jour-
nalist and botanical litterateur, distinguished
himself last week by rescuing a lady-vocalist from
a watery grave. Miss Adelaide Bruce, the song-
stress aforesaid, was getting lost beyond the
breakers at Lome (Vic.) when Mac. was sum-
moned by a damsel in a bathing-dress, and, with-
out stopping to admire the figure of his fair
informant, he swam to the assistance of the
distressed damsel, gave her full instructions
bow to get saved, and straightway hauled her
ashore. Also, he gallantly credited her with
having made his achievement quite easy.
Mr. M'Donald, who is a 15-stone six-
footer, footballed with Essendon in his
youth, and is probably the most powerful
man on the Australian press. Mrs. Mac., a well-
known penwoman, holds the distinction of being
the pioneer bloomered cyeliste of Melb. Once
asked what her husband was like, she said, “Just
stroll down Collins-street till you meet a man like
a god—that’s my husband.” M‘Don aid hailed
originally from the Corowa Free Press, the
journalistic birthplace of several bright writers.

A certain “ghost” has lately haunted
Castlemaine (Vic.) on windy nights, and many
people have been scared off their base by the pale,
rushing apparition, which goes by with a chatter-
ing sound like teeth shaking in a loose skull.
Three local heroes decided to arrest the vision,
but thought better of it; one fainted, and the
other two ran for their lives, and the apparition
mowed and gibbered down the road unmolested.
Castlemaine is a town with a great record in the
way of apparitions, but its boss spectre was the
Greasy Man of twenty years ago. This buttered
visitant haunted the outskirts on Saturday nights
almost exclusively—his regular hours were be-
tween 8 and 12; and lie was seen only by women
carrying groceries. The Greasy Man had a fine
taste in groceries. A stout, middle-aged
female would be trudging home, carrying
her week’s supplies—lib. tea, 61b. sugar, 501 b.
flour, |c\vt. potatoes, 8 loaves, tin of kerosene,
21b. butter, large bundle mixed vegetables, one
hindquarter of mutton, bottle of vinegar, also salt,
washing soda, soap, blue, some clothes-pegs, a
bottle of beer, the child’s new frock, and the old
man’s bluchers just come back from being half-
soled. Then a tall white figure, apparently in
tights, would vault 12ft. high over the fence and
land in front of her with a blood-curdling whoop.
Naturally the lady would drop all her miscella-
neous groceries and run for her life. The larded
spook was always gone when help arrived ; so
were the groceries and the bluchers. The Greasy
Man had a long innings, but he met his fate one
night when a robust washerwoman, instead of
bolting, dropped her weekly tin of kerosene on
his toe and then fastened on him like a bulldog
till.help arrived. He proved to be a local resi-
dent, with nothing on hut a coat of grease, padded
shoes and a vaulting pile. Some tons of groceries
was found on his premises, and begot 18 months
—for robbery, being undressed in a public place,
vagrancy, and conduct calculated to lead to breach
of the peace.

The larrikin doesn’t always have it his owp
way. At S. Melbourne, one night recently, a
small party waylaid a grizzled veteran of 60 and
his fat missus, and began to “ mix it ” for the old
man.* The sturdy citizen entered into the spirit
of the thing, and responded in kind so earnestly
that he presently had one “ bloke ” spread out
under the stars and a second badly damaged, and,
while the old lady whaled the latter unmercifully
with a big stick, he dealt in a tradesman-like
manner with the remaining two. At this stage
another reputable ratepayer took a hand, and the
push was glad to gather up its wounded and limp
off. When tile police arrived the grizzled elector
and madam were getting on to the second citizen
“something awful” for interfering with a fight
that did not concern him, and spoiling their even-
ing’s amusement.

Dear Bulletin. —Everybody knows that
love and cigars should never be re-lit (with cer-
tain exceptions). Pass the love ; but can any of
your experts explain the cigar? What are the
physical or chemical changes in the leaf which
destroy the aroma when re-lit ? And does a nice
palate feel the same trouble in a re-lit pipe or
cigarette ?—B. B.

BURGESS'S ACCOMPLICE.
He was a myth ; his name was Smith; he was crowned
with pale-black hair.
His eyes were either dark or light:
He was a dwarf of extra height;
He lived next door to nobody, and the bunyip lived next
door but three,
In a nameless street, where four ways meet, in the town
of Godknowswhere.
No kin nor kith belonged to Smith. No person saw him
pass;
No one beheld him take the track,
Or noted that he ne’er came back.
He was an unassuming chap; his absence hasn’t left a
gap,
Bud on a certain midnight dree he helped to butcher
persons three, and left them on the grass.
The reason why he made them die no mortal man can say;
He left no footprint as he fled
In haste with blood upon his head.
No one can find on road or track the house to which h«
came not back.
He shot his victim with a gun, and swallowed it when he
was done ; then blew himself away.
No monolith stands over Smith, but folks are sure he’s
dead.
His blood no stain left on the ground ;
His body somehow can’t be found ;
And nobody has called to say that somebody has gone
away ;
No stray dog drifts adown the street, looking ’mongst
people it may meet for some one that has fled.
The hot winds blow, and come and go, but still no due
they bear;
No old hat hloweth to and fro
Whose owner people do not know ;
No boarder when the night was late from hash-tower did
evaporate.
II that accomplice is a myth-but probably his mimo
was Smith, from the town of Godknowswhere.
TEMPUS FUGIT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ As it was in the Beginning .”
A curse—for a chance thrown windwards ;
A sigh—for another’s pain ;
A pause—for a sin regretted,
And life goes on again !
Lips with their sweetness tasted ;
Eyes that beseech in vain ;
A tomb—for a dead emotion,
And life goes on again !
A careworn spirit moving,
Slow-pulsed, with death in train ;
A mourner, perhaps, at the grave-side,
And life goes on again !
Let Caution dwell with the greybeards,
We laugh at the jade’s refrain ;
A pause—for a sin committed,
And life goes on again 1
Mofee.
W.P.
AS THE QUEENLY MOON’S REFLECTION.
(After Heine.)
As the queenly moon’s reflection
Trembles in the waves of ocean,
While herself in splendour paces
Heaven with soft majestic motion ;
So whilst thou, Love, calm and stately,
Little heed of aught art taking,
In my heart thine image trembles
While my heart itself is breaking.
Q-
P. LUFTIG.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
. Sydney, February 7, 1899.
My Deab Myek —
Alternately, hopes have been coming and going
all the week. Tne nasty news that dear Lord
Beauchamp would not look at a woman unless she
was an assured Fright, and could further sup|x>rt
the assurance with a certificate from Clothing
Classes, and Cold Tea Unions, and the Society for
Doing Good to the Poor Whites, has given every
woman under seventy a severe shock. Many
won’t believe the tale at all. Others who know
very well they are not frights, but can see their
title clear to many good marks from the Y.W.C.A.
and other bodies in elastic-side cloth boots, are
brisking up, and beginning to take a younger view
of life than ever. As for tne male adjuncts of this
wicked city, it will go hard with them if they are
going to be forced into a comparison with a
juvenile fossil (genus: Crichtonus Admirabilis).
They keep rather a gloomv silence as each new
quality comes to light. We have had so many
varieties of Governor out here that, somehow,
nobody is quite prepared for a young man whose
only fault is that he hasn’t got one. Unless the
cables are cruelly keeping dark his redeeming sin.
Another Earl whom Sydney takes special pride
in is the new Lindsey, late' Lord Bertie. His
father died last week, and a Sydney girl. Miss
Millie Cox, who married Bertie while he was
aideing the Carringtons out here, is now Lady
Lindsey. This title is generally supjioeed to be
a hollow-sounding affair, but at any rate die ex-
Berties will now tie able to leave the lodge cottage
where rumor has always painted them living in a
very hard-up style, and dwell in a mansion more
worthy of people with a handle.
_ n^,? nd | )U8 bing young Australian pianiste,
Mdlle. Celinaki, who lias done so well in Mel-
bourne lately, shows quite a genius for fixing
herself up for the photographer. It isn’t the
commonest kind of talent in Australia either.
Our most graceful beauties have a way of stiffen-
ing under the camera’s fire that is not much of an
improvement oil a raw bushie having a shilling’s-
worth of “portrait taken while you wait.” CeKn-
®ki * pictures are not in the least suggestive of her
native place and her name proper, which are re-
spectively Ballarat and Maud Wilson. She throws
into her pose a remarkable liveliness and chic,
even when she is pictured striking ixwture-chords
at the piano, which might reasonably take the
sprightliness out of a Vital S|«irk. Her first con-
cert in Sydney comes off next Thursday by invi-
tation. On the programme is a piece called “ The
Bells, by Prof. Graham Moore, inspired by Edgar
Poe s poem. Both their Exes, are to be present,
and people who want to combine music with a last
good look into Lady Hamixlen’s back hair may
have seats reserved on the payment of one shilling.
I hope it will not be unfortunate for Celinski
that “The Geisha” opens fire on the same date.
As soon as the box plan was exposed last Thursday
a wild scrimmage for seats began, and three nights
booked away immediately. Society is going
to come down in large quantities from its rose-
hunting m the country, and everything points to a
brilliant beginning for this hungrily - expected
opera.
Our decamping panto, reckons it has had hard
luck in Sydney. Pattie Browne’s illness was the
wggest cross it had to carry, and then there was
Hassan’s accident, and an unusual epidemic of ail-
ments among the girls of the co. kept upsetting
things generally. The house grew very thin
towards the last. Pattie lias come through her
operation, but she is still very dangerously ill.
Everybody feels sorry for her. She hasn’t had a
rosy time since she came bock to Australia,
with bad business, a had part in the panto., and,
finally, a bad illness that will long keep her away
from the stage.
A well-known Sydney girl is earning much
commiseration at present. When she married a
naval officer a few years ago. her fond mother
stimilated that she should continue to live at home
all her life. At the time this suited the voung
wife very well, and she gave the required promise.
Hubby, however, has now been ordered away on
foreign service, but the observance of her bond is
insisted on. So the poor girl is doomed to grass
widowhood fora |*eriod of at least three years,
though there is nothing but her promise to prevent
her from going abroad with her husband. The
lady whose engagement to a Wallaroo officer has
just been annouuce-d, is being congratulated with
reservations.
Two Sydney weddings come off in March. Miss
Birdie Prosser's marriage with Mr. James Pick-
burn, of the junior Bar, is fixed for an early date
V. month. Another Sydney University-man,
Mr. Norman White, C. E., is to be married about
the same time to Miss Marion Horton, the pretty
girl grad, who had so terrible an experience some
years ago m the Redfern railway accident.
Mayoress Harris, along with other Sydney
iadies, is working up a farewell-party, flanked
with a solid souvenir, for Lady Hampden. There
was a meeting at the Town Hall last week to dis-
cubs the matter, and it was decided that the tear-
ful ceremony should take the form of a conver-
sazione. Lord Hampden is luckier. His vale-
dictory struggles are to be by wav of a banquet
which is fixed for Monday, Feb. 27. Lord Bras-
sy is coming over to help the parting guest
through the final Barter of Butter, and, when all
these dolorous junketings are over, the Hampdens
will take the sharp edge off their parting, as it
were, with a week at Melb. Govt. House. And,
after that, the cry of Long Live the King will
positively refuse to be suppressed any longer.
Public opinion is pretty eVen with a good word
for the Hanqidens— or was, until the dazzling rays
of the new Gov't lustra rather paled away the
light of the present comfortably polished op. Lady
Hampden has kept herself more or less isolated
from the giddy world, but has managed every-
thing with such a sweet motherly grace and iiecu-
liarly nice smile that society hasn’t noticed that
Her Ex. has never absolutely pressed it to her
bosom. Besides, the vice-regal daughters have
gone everywhere; even to the length of causing
some (icople to be surprised at meeting them in
the houses of dear enemies.
Since English opera failed all of a sudden at
the Lyceum, a more successful state of things
has l*-eu following the theatre’s return to
its original paths of melodrama. “The Flying
Scud ” is the latest production. Miss Burdett
plavs a heroine charmingly again, and looks slim
and simple in a long blue muslin, with a cinnamon
satin sash, and the same shade of mittens. Her
riding-habit was rather suggestive of tea-gown
draping; but, then, the riding-habit in a play of
this kind, where dressing doesn’t run to anything
absurdly extravagant, is always a ticklish matter,
for a smartly-built horsey suit can never be run
up on the cheap. Miss Ivy Gorrick—the amateur
who played Fouchi rather promisingly last year
is cast as an adventuress, but frocks herself too
sloppily for the part. A bright little bit of busi-
ness is the jockey boys’—by the way, they are all
gir s—hornpipe, where a tall, dark-haired horse-
girl in a natty black-and-yellow jock suit, shines
out from several other pretty beings.
But the actor I liked best in the play was the
horse that put its head out of the stable door,
ouch an adorable, spirited, but innocent-looking
white head gazed out on the audience ! All the
men and women in the house seemed suddenly to
grow common. If there had been other horses the
effect might have been less striking, but that one
solitary, beautiful creature quite took away the
good looks of just ordinary humau beings. It
made me think of a wise old worldling who said he
liked to get home out of the streets because all the
people looked like pigs and hawks. I fancy this
is a fairly universal phase of feeling, as another
m&n with his finger on the pulse of thing's was
recently heard to say “I have seen a dog killed,
and I’ve seen a man killed, and I shall never for-
get seeing the dog killed.”

People who read advts. were often amused last
winter with one about a lecture on Russia. Week
after week announcements would appear fixing
the discourse for a certain date. And next day a
very friendly, chatty sort of notice would intimate
that the entertainment was unavoidably post-
P°ned. The reasons for postponement ran on such
eccentric lines as a charity ball that must not be
interfered with, or the prior engagement of soine-
body wanted to preside. Every notice was headed
with a long and creamy list of patrons, beginning
with Lady Hampden, and including everyone who
could (tossibly be put on this kind of list.
" hether the lecture ever came off is a mystery, as
anyone who meant to go got lost in the labyrinth
of altered dates, and gave up all hope of reaching
the land of the Czars. ?

One more tribute to the fascinations of the short
woman !
Speaking of little women reminds me that Syd-
ney people often complain that they see so little
of their most popular woman-author, Ethel Tur-
ner. tor the last three years she has been living
quietly at Mosman’s Bay, where she occupies
herself chiefly with a lively edition of her “Story
of a Baby,” a year-old little girl named Jean.
Between whiles she has managed to write a new
book entitled “Three Little Maids,” a true and
delicately-painted picture of her own and her sister
Lilians early literary days, wherein the story of
her first book is set forth ; also, she edits the chil-
dren’s page m the Town and Country Journal
under the name of Dame Durden ; and keeps the
baby from emptying the ink-bottle on its downy
head. Lilian Turner, Ethel’s elder sister, now
Mrs. Thompson, lias been editing Cosmos up to
date, much to tho benefit of that tempest-tossed
little journal.
As already mentioned, that clever Sydney
artist, Mrs. Stoddard, is preparing for a visit to
England. Her water-color |K>rtrait of Miss Bur-
dekin, exhibited at last Art Society’s Exhibition
has been so loudly and deservedly admired that
the artist is going to try her luck with work of
this kind in London. Her gifted daughter, Mary
goes along with her mother to pick up a wider
knowledge of art than Sydney offers. Just now
Mrs. Stoddard is at work on the portrait of a cer-
tain classic-featured Maoriland beauty who lately
married a Sydney scribe. J

A local waltz with a good swing in it comes
from Paling and Co. in the shape of “ Le Geypt ”
waltz, by J. Edward Pople, with a -string of
music-meaning letters after his name. This i 3
unique in being decorated with a portrait of the
composer’s wife in the top corner, while the com-
poser stows himself away at the bottom of the
It ought also to run on its merits as a
it, melodious little waltz. “Beloved” (also
from Paling’s), by Angelo Mascheroni, is what
you might call a sweetly pretty song that wouldn’t
bite anyone, which attribute may further be
accounted for by the fact that the composer is
called Angelo Mashoroni.
From Nicholson and Co. comes a nice little
musical album, beginning its repertoire with the
same Chanson Triste that used to move Sydney
girls to tearful praying for autographs when Mark
Hambourg played it. Chopin, Beethoven, Handel,
are all represented here, and the only flaw to be
found in the shilling’s-worth is that some of these
classics have had their best creases smoothed out
by local fingers. There are still |*oople living who
don’t think Sydney can help Chopin and Beethoven
alongduuch, out these folk don’t patrouise shilling
albums, and need not be considered. Paling and
Co. send a trio of songs- stirring, sturdy young-
man declamations—“ The Patrol ” (by Edward St.
Quentin), “On the Deep Blue Sea" (by W. 11.
Jude), and a “ Sailor’s Star ” (by Godfrey Marks),
lhese are all good, honest songs, and may be
recommended to any man who likes to yell at the
top of his voice. The same firm sends a new and
rather Pretty little song of its own publishing,
called I’ll Ne’er Forget.” Esther Lewin is
responsible for the music and L. 11. for the words,
all blended together in harmonious wailings al>out
the sea, sobbing, and nevermore. As these four
songs are all about the sea, I gather that the
waves are still saying a good deal.
Local music has other pulls over local literature
besides its superior knowledge of the sad sea
rollers, rrom the back-block poet suffering from
sunset, to the flourishing Ilorsetralian novelist, ac-
ceptance or rejection is meted out to pen-wielders
from behind the screen of a postage-stamp, or from
the even more advantageous beating-ground of
an ans. to cor. column. But the man wno lets his
soul soar through the medium of a waltz or a
polka is seldom hooted off the field with a written
statement like, “Your waltz has galloped into the
w.p.b. Answers to local Mozarts are nearly
always personal, as the music-houses have no
correspondents’ column at their disposal, and
haven t enough water on the brain (in spite of
their musical occupations) to ruin themselves with
replies per post. Imagine the sufferings of
these publishers explaining to the composer's face
that it isn’t our style ! Several local firms have,
within the last two years, received tons of waltzes,
studies, nocturnes, songs, to say nothing of queer
hitherto-undiscovered animals that are neither
concertos, sonatas, fugues, nor good hearing of
any sort, but are marked simply in the highest
type of musical arrogance, Op. 100 by Johnny
Bach-. Tones, or Eliza Beethoven-Browne. Maud
Fitz-Stubbs’ Heather Waltz was about the best-
paying piece of music ever published in Australia.
You would be surprised to find what a long, long
way after are the profits of other local composers,
who, most of them anyway, never get anything
but newspaper notices and musical head out of
their heavy labors. And yet the unremunerative
music-market continues to be rushed, as if it were
going to turn demi-semi-quavers into diamonds in
six-eight time.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The cadets, who were body-guard to Lord
Brassey on Foundation Day, got drunk on glory,
flashed about in round shoulders, narrow chests
and crochet-needle legs, with pipe in mouth and
chin-strap on nose. Very little lord goes a long
way. But, strange to say, not one of them paraded
his girl ! Not even a mother or a sister. Whence
comes the difference between the 4 colonial ’ and
the imported toy-soldier ?
“With the Bland Holts, Frances Ross, as the
\icar's one fair daughter, affects white muslin and
blue ribbons, a velvet necklet and small black
crass imparting a churchy touch to the costume.
This innocent-sounding plumage looks more
worldly than you would suppose, what with its
up-to-date lace-flounced skirt, cluster of red-red
roses in the fluffy bodice, and long, narrow sash
encircling the wearer’s slim waist. Later on, the
comely mourner sartorially expresses her anguish
at her lover’s disgrace by modish black ruches on
a white gown and plumed black Panama hat.
Whou the clouds roll by, the heartened-up heroine
rejoices in gauzy black and sheeny scarlet.
Fine feathers make fine birds. The lengthy
process of stripping the decorations off the elabo-
rate Highland uniform of cashiered officer Baker
shows how much trimining goes to a gunpowder
hero. Divested of epaulettes, sword, medal, sash,
sjxjrran, phillabeg, frogged cuffs, revers, even the
basque of his coat, the accused was reduced at
last to short scarlet jacket and plain kilt skirt. A
mixed audience heaves a sigh of relief when
culprit’s official undresser stays his hand at said
stage. There’s much {>opular uneasiness, of
course, re a Hielandman’s nether toilette.
Mrs. Bland Holt as a bike belle is “one of the
best” she-breek-wearers hitherto encountered,
thanks to shoulders apparently broader and more
massive than her hips. Most feminine hips look
as if built on purpose to hold out skirts. With no
lietticoats to do their duty by, their width seems
meaningless, swaggering, waddling vulgarity.

“Some time ago a letter appeared in one of the
Melb. papers, from Portsea, complainipg of the
conduct of some of the male visitors at that place,
who, it was stated, had been bathing from the pier
in a state of nudity. The letter asked why the
pohoe did not take action. Accordingly the Chief
Commissioner of Police gave instructions to the
local constable to obtain the names of the offenders
m order that action might be taken. The names
were obtained, but—but—but—they were those of
two legal Dignitaries and one very large municipal
1 otentate. Up to now no action has been taken,
and no more letters on the subject have been
admitted to the papers—which evidently 4 didn’t
know it was loaded’ when they printed the
original complaint.

“ Presbyterian Parson Hume Robertson, of
Castlemaine, who, ’tis said, intends to stump the
country in op|iosition to the A.N.A. art-union, is
himself Australian-born, an only and substantially
dutiful son, generous brother, and one of the few

La Poupee,’ his wide-awake reverence ‘can
sing and dance,’ play tennis and billiards. He is
