M ith the advent of the boyish Earl Beauchamp,
Lord Lamington will cease to be the ‘ Little Ben-
jamin ’ of the Australian gubernatorial group.
Prior to its present vice-regal regime, Bananaland
had no experience of youthful Governors. Sir
George Bowen is chiefly remembered by old
Queenslanders for his long memory and as the
husband of a handsome wife—a dark-eyed Maid of
Athens, whose Christian name, ‘Diamantina,’is
commemorated in Northern geographical nomen-
clature.
“Sir George’s own namesake, the town of
Bowen, has begun the New Year with one of its
habitual hurricanes. Poor little port! Lulled to
rest ,of late by comparative immunity ; in its in-
fancy its nerve was cycloned to smithereens. Old
time Bowenites were known by their scared looks
and maunderings re their harbor’s familiar storm-
fiend— ‘Three puffs, and down goes the township
like a house of cards. ’ For months after such an
experience sufferers started and changed color if a
stone clattered on a galvanised-iron roof, or a goat
banged down street with its horns entangled in an
empty kerosene-tin.
“Gov. Blackall, another Northern pro-consul,
was a widower with one grown-up son. Steaming
into Gladstone (Q.) he saw a welcoming crowd on
the wharf. As the vessel drew alongside, a local
loyalist shrieked, ‘ Three cheers for the Governor!
Three cheers for the missus and the kids ! ’ Next,
the enthusiast caught sight of the gorgeously
uniformed aide, standing at his Ex.’s elbow;
‘ Three cheers for the man in the brass mountings ! ’
he yelled.
“A banquet was once given thereabouts in honor
of the old Marquis of Normanby. A visiting
somebody speaking to the toast of the ‘ Mining
Industries of the District ’ foamed at the mouth
with earnestness. ‘ Your neighborhood teems with
minerals,’ said he in his peroration ; ‘you have but
to give Nature an emetic and inside will become
outside, in no time ’! ! ! CJgh ! Fancy talking
like that at a banquet! And such a banquet 1
Served up in sweltering weather and shared by
swarms of buzzing bottle-flies! Lashings and
lavings of liquor and meat, the latter going from
bad to worse under the very noses of the diners,
very little vegetables or fruit, no ice.
“Re the antiquity of old-time northern pro-
consuls, Gov. Kennedy, in his Bananaland days,
was a fat, more-or-less infirm widower, addicted
to good jokes and Irish stews, and dutifully shep-
herded by his one unmarried daughter. As Capt.
Kennedy, in an Irish garrison town, he had
brought himself under the favorable notice of the
powers-that-be, through his energetic Famine re-
lief measures.
“Their Excellencies Musgrave and Norman had
each of them buried the wife of his youth, and
been married for long enough to her successor
before his Q. appointment, so neither was a guber-
natorial Infant Prodigy. The first-named had
digestive and consequent temper-troubles. Aboard-
shipj bound from ’Ome to Australia, he got huffed
with his fello.w-passengers, the late Baronet W.
Clarke and family, because one of the youngsters
accidentally hit him with a ball. During an official
tour, he set aside prior hotel-arrangements, and
put up at whatever seemed to him the staidestand
least rorty of available caravanserais. Despite
these precautions, one night in a sultry Centralian
town ho was disturbed by a crying baby. First
thing next morning he sent for the landlady, and
in awful tones exclaimed —‘ Woman, you have
undone me! ’ She was horribly frightened, and
coidd have screamed with relief when he went on
—* Governor or infant! Choose which you will! ’

Marshall-Hall is now in Sydney—staying
in camp at Cremorne. His latest—" My friend the
Rev. Mr. Fidgett, of uneasy-virtue celebrity.”
Mayor M‘Eacharn, of Melbourne, is a
many-thousand shareholder of Sydney Daily
I klkgraph—not in his own name, but as executor
of J. B. Watson, his father-in-law.
George Kingswell, erstwhile proprietor of
Coolgabdie Miner, is in China. He has gone to
the I lowery Land to see if he can’t get Chinese
railway-sleeper contracts for the Forrests’ timber-
firm.
“General” Booth, who is expected to
begin his third tour of Australia on 22nd inst.,
will be 70 during the progress of it. His “first
appearance ” this time will be on the Westralian
goldfields.
Mabel Ambrose’s trip in a box was not
her first experience as a parcel for delivery.
• ?>'rf S once Posted to her father, who was a clerk
in P. 0., Albury, by her mother. That was when
she was a baby.
Again N.S.W. draws a non-aquatic Gover-
nor from the Imperial ballot-box. Brisbane drew
Norman Melbourne Brassey, but Sydney, of the
Beautiful Harbor, etc., draws a man who gets sick
at the smell of brine.
Francis Druce, a middle-aged Brompton
(S.A.) bootmaker, claims to be heir to the
Portland estates. He is grandson of Jonathan
Druce, brother of the T. C. Druce who, it is al-
leged, was the Duke of Portland.
The Pope has created “Tim” Quinlan,
M.l. (W.A.), a Chevalier of the order of St.
Sylvester. The new “Chevalier” is one of the
wealthiest men in Perth, a city father, and mem-
ber tor an agricultural constituency.

off, after all. The man who prefers to camp in
the open with a bluey for pillow, instead of com-
fortably snuggling up indoors, would have been
m the Divorce Court in about six weeks.

Walesa three-years’ damage in its new Governor.
His name will be Bewchamp, Bawchamp, Bew-
shong, Boshang, Bochamp, Beechamp—anything,
m fact, to avoid clashing with the pill-man.

Charles Hardie Buzacott, ancient father of
pleonisms and platitudes, says the late Premier
Byrnes was a small man till he went to England
and talked with great men. “ When he returned,”
says Charles, “ I discovered he had grown a great
man—and was glad.”
“Cabbage-tree” Ned Devine, one of the
first Vic. drivers for Cobb and Co., is “jobbing ”
in horses at Coolgardie and Kalgurlie. Ned was
bailed up by most of the bushranging gangs, but
never tells any yarns about them. “Not such a
bad lot,” he says, “ take ’em altogether.”
When a Melb. Metropolitan Board of
Works commissioner accused Chairman Fitz-
Gibbon of attempting to boss the show the
humorless old busybody replied : “ I follow out
the Biblical injunction, ‘Magnify thine office,
saith the Lord.’ ” Fitz is a Lord unto himself.
Since “ Mines ” was made a special N.S. W.
Ministerial Department, a quarter of a century
ago. Cook, the present Minister, is the only one,
except Copeland, who had worked as a miner for
daily bre «d. Several Ministers dabbled in mining,
both coal and gold, but only as speculators on the’
labor of others.
They tried to “ get off one ” on Peacock,
Vic. Education Minister. He is not a perfect
speller, but was an authority at a spelling-bee.
liume-Cook asked him to spell “ Pan tom me ”
thinking to trap him in the penult letter. Pea-
cock gave it an “ n ” and won, stating that if he
had l)een asked for “ Pantomime ” he would have
spelt it differently.
The first Australian posterist to appear
before an English audience is caricaturist Laing—
he of the big-footed sundowners. Laing lately
sent to England copies of several of his posters for
sale by auction to collectors. His caricature of
“ Quatre Bras ” brought <£3; his poster for Dyson’s
“On Top and Below,” £2 ; and others sold down
to 10s. apiece. The poster is a department of art
to which Australia has so far given no encourage-
ment, though there are several men in the country
who can turn out excellent work.
Dear Bulletin. —Oapt. Pentecost, of
the Lady St. Aubin, speaking of finding re-
lics of the expedition of La Perouse, says
that “ one hundred years had passed away,
when the remains of his two vessels were
found on this island of Vanikoro.” Rats!
When I was a kiddy, and in London, I
met Chevalier Dillon, a tough old salt, who
had been pensioned and decorated by the
French Government for discovering (in the
twenties) the remains of the lost ships of La
Perouse. And, as this same John Dillon
nearly succeeded in preventing my existence,
I want to stand up for his rights. It was
this way. My dad, in 1808, having much
§rize-money, purchased a brig called the
pring, and started for this part of the
world ; and he shipped Dillon as first-mate.
On the way out, my progenitor, looking over
the log-book, read this entry : “ Boar up
two points N.E.,” to which he humorously
appended the note: “Boar, a male pig!”
Dill on, as Irish as they made them in those
days, resented this comment, and challenged
his skipper. They had it out at the Cape,
and Dillon sent a ball through the pater’s
bell-topper beaver hat. Then they shook
hands and jogged along. Dillon soon got a
vessel of his own, and, some years afterwards,
stimulated by the offer of a big reward by
the French Govt.—after the Restoration—he
spent some time in the South Seas, searching
for news of La Perouse. At last he struck
oil, and was rewarded. La Perouse left
Sydney i" 1788, and Dillon traced his wreck
in 1826 —38 years later. When Dillon visited
our house in London, in the forties, he came
in a glass coach, and had to be edged out
carefully, for he was enormous of height and
girth. I had heard of him often, and had
gazed upon the historic hat, with many
fancies of what might have been. With
the ingenuousness of youth, I brought this
fateful tile from the lumber-room, and asked
him if he remembered it. My father ex
plained. There was an awkward moment.
Then the old chevalier exclaimed, “Ha!
my boy, you will make a spoon or spoil a
horn.” And I spoiled the horn. But Dillon
nearly spoiled my existence.—O.J.
Gov. Tennyson is the first genuine book-
man sent to govern an Australian province.
Bowen was a bookman of a kind ; he was brought
up in the atmosphere of books and contemplated a
University professorship as his career, but early
withdrew his mind from serious study. Tennyson
is 47, and books mainly possess him.
Old Col. Phillips, a peppery army veteran,
is in charge of Westralian police. Recently,
when addressing the Perth bobbies, he said that
the three cardinal sins possible for a policeman to
commit were—insubordination, intoxication and
giving information to the press. The public, he
considers, exists solely for the police.
Obit., at Devonport (Tas.), Michael Quin-
lan, an old-time V.D.L. policeman. He was for
many years stationed at Oatlands, then the
favorite lurking-spot of bushrangers, and could
tell stirring tales of affrays with the Robin Hoods
of Lagland romance. Deceased, who suicided,
was 90 years of age. It took him close on a cen-
tur} 1, to find that life was not worth living.
Paddy Perkins (aboriginal), whose name
comes up in the Gatton affair, is probably the
champion tracker. He is credited with having
once tracked a nigger murderer who walked along
li post-and-rail fence for two miles, said nigger
giving himself away with a pole he carried to pre-
vent himself from falling. “ Paddy ” tracked the
impressions of the pole in the grass, one about
every quarter-mile.
In the gay days of the Tas. capital, when
the late John Donnellan Balfe was in his glory, a
Misther O’Reilly was its king of tailors, and his
shop the resort of swells of the first rank. “A
gintleman walked into me emporium this mom-
ing,”said O’R. upon one occasion, “and purchased
the most be-utiful shoot of sooperfine cloth ; and
this afthemoon I percayved the most magnificent
costoom of sooperfine cloth lying in the gutter,
and Mr. Bawlfe in the middle of ’nm.”
Two of the most prominent applicants
for the vacant “Governorship ” of Norfolk Island
—open again, no doubt, owing to the misfortune
which has befallen Governor-elect Knapp—were,
it is stated, ex-Judge M‘Far]and, of Sydney,
and Capt. Strachan, chiefly famous for his libel-
action against a Tasmanian daily, and his over-
whelming failure to oust Labor-member Hughes
from N.S.W. Parliament. M'Farland, who re-
tired from the District Court some years ago, was,
in his time, the butt of many humorous yarns told
out of his hearing by irreverent junior counsel,
and festive bagmen tell tales about him in the
commercial room all over N.S.W.
R. C. Luscombe, formerly M.P. for
Northumberland, N.S.W., afterwards inspector of
scaffolding in Sydney, has turned up—after many
had thought him dead —with a long article on
Westralia in S.M. Herald. His facts are four
years old, but no matter. This paper is glad to
find the old battler alive. It once suspected that
the Dibbs crowd gave him his scaffolding billet
so that he might fall off something tall and
become a denizen of a better land. R.C.L.’s first
trip after election was to the opening of the rail-
way to Glen Innes. A. G. Taylor, unknown to
him, marked the railway-car “ H.H. the Duke of
Northumberland.” This was confounded with
the Duke of Manchester, then in the country.
Luseotnbe got all the cheers at wayside stations,
and several times returned thanks from the car.

MATRON: “My dear girl, marriage is the easiest

Wilson, the Oxford graduate now being
taken back to Brisbane from Albany by three
Q. police on a serious charge, was easily traced on
account of having constantly with him a cripple
son, whom he wheeled in a go-cart. The boy
contracted paralysis while at Broken Hill, where
his father was a schoolmaster. He was only one
of several child-victims of lead-poisoning. Some
died ; others, less fortunate, face life disabled.
As a result of the now-deceased Adelaide
Hospital squabble, Dr. Napier, the able and
popular imported surgeon, was recently black-
balled on his nomination for membership of
Adelaide Bowling Club, an institution containing
a majority of medicos and their Tory friends.
This latest action of the interested medical clique
which “kept the sore open” in the early days of
the dispute, is, perhaps, the most despicable in
the whole business.
Monsignor O’Brien, president of St. John’s
College, Sydney, and a possible successor to Dr.
Higgins as coadjutor bishop, if the latter returns
to Ireland, contradicts the assertion that he is
related to Card. Moran. Frequent jibes have
been made at the alleged preponderance of the
Cardinal’s kin in good ecclesiastical billets, but
two clerics only in Sydney diocese are related to
him—private-secretary O’Haran and another mem-
ber of the cathedral-staff. Dr. Verdon, now Bishop
of Dunedin, is a cousin of the Cardinal.

He then lived at Maccles-

Apropos the liberation of Mrs. Gordon
Baillie, at least one Melb. man is praying that she
will not come to Australia with any more philan-
thropic projects. Mrs. G. B. made extensive use
of the poor man and the poor man’s banking
account, and when he became too affectionate had
him hoisted out on a boot. The sufferer returned
to his home, a broken and contrite man, and was
kicked off his own doorstep for his sins (uncom-
mitted, alas !) by his own sons, aided and abetted
by his indignant wife. He was two years winning
his way back to the family bosom, and has ever
since repented his uncommitted sins.

tralia in 1818, but in 1808, and that he did nob

When Melb. detective Dungey (of the
“ boot-trunk case”) was senior-constable at Epping
(Vic.), a recidiviste came into the neighborhood,
plundering houses and frightening women.
Dungey went in pursuit, came across him while
camped, jumped off his horse, and tried to hand-
cuff the man, who, however, turned out to be a
wrestler, and tumbled Dungey about till he gob
him down. Then the Frenchman set about
throttling him, but Dungey, winded and almost
powerless, by an effort got out his revolver and
shot the man dead. So exhausted was Dungey
that, for some time afterwards, he could not shake
the corpse off the top of him. Dungey, when
orderly to Gov. Loch, was one of the showiest of
such ornaments in the Govt, service.
“ C.M.G.,” to The Bulletin :
You lately published a par. re death of Joseph Bosisto,
in which you mentioned that the lamented deceased was
a ‘ ‘p atron ” of Jimmy Thomson, who formerly ran Melb.
Evening Standard. I knew them both, and rather think
it was the other way about. Jimmy was a pressman, and
always kept Bosisto’s name well in front during Calcutta
Exhibition, after which the pair were associated in the
Colonial and Indian show in London. I see by the old
gentleman’s will that he has bequeathed hisC.M.G. insignia
(a big pewter sort of medal) to his nephew, George
Joseph Stirling, surgeon, of Melbourne. He also men-
tioned in his will that his “ sword of State and garments”
presented by the Queen were to go with the rnedal. As a
matter of fact, both sword and garments were supplied
by an ordinary trading firm in London, and paid for in
the same fashion as those ordered by other C.M.G’s. Dr.
Stirling is scarcely a nephew in the ordinary acceptance
of the term. “ Old Bos.” married a widow, and they
were both childless. Her brother had several children,
one of whom is “ Dr. Bob,” but he is no blood relative of
the late father of the gum-leaves, as Tom Carrington
called him. The only surviving relatives are William
Bosisto, for many years a railway-guard in England, and
his daughter, both residing at Reading.”
Melbourne Age’s London correspondent
re the retirement of old Justice Hawkins in Eng-
land :
His vigor, mental and physical, is as great as it was
when in 1873 he led the case against the Claimant, and
was coached by me in the topography of Victoria, and
the different names by which the same places were called.
“Me ” in this case is George Collins Levey.
Hawkins married his cook, about whose mala-
propisms many queer (but doubtless apocryphal)
stories were told. But she was, as the writer
happens to know, a very good and kindly woman,
and the old Judge was very devoted to her. One
used to see Hawkins out with his dogs on Sunday
mornings in Hyde Park— a genial, hairless-headed
little fellow in a brown-velvet coat, who would
stop to give a child a pat on the head and a six-
pence, and always had a gentle word for those
who approached him. Yet he was England’s
“great hanging Judge.” Hanging Judges and
hangmen are, domestically, dreadful “ take-
downs.” When this scribe entered the private
residence of an Australian man-butcher who has
slain his scores, he found the chimpanzee-like
gentleman with a little daughter on his knee teach-
ing her to spell out a paragraph in the Bible! And
when the writer was departing, the hangman
snuffled to him in quite a genial way: “ When
you come again be sure and bring the missis and ”
(pointing to the flowers in his garden) “ I’ll give
her a fco-kay.”
About 14 years ago, Willoughby Andrew
(who narrowly escaped the scaffold and is now
confined for life in Darlinghurst prison for shoot-
ing dead a Sydney draper) was, on the eve of »
general election, attending at Molong (N.S.W.) a
meeting addressed by Dr. Ross, the sitting mem-
ber. Andrew was wordy with whisky, and had a
difficulty with a pugnacious Rossi te, whom he
BILL HOWELL—perhaps the most popular cricketer of the hour.
Almost a certainty for the XI.
speed lly laid in ruins on the floor. Then he
mounted a form and announced his determination
to wipe out Dr. Ross, politically, as he had wiped
out his follower, physically. A red-hot contest
for the seat ensued, and Andrew was narrowly
beaten ; and, loth to leave the town without a
tangible record of the campaign, he gave hints
about the propriety of a testimonial. But, as no-
body rose to the proposal, he decided to manage
the business himself. Preparing a subscription-
he button-holed the first man he met with,
Here, Smith ; we cannot allow Willoughby
Andrew to leave the electorate without recognising
the spirited way he fought the election. What
about a gold watch—say, 50 guineas? What shall
I put you down for? A guinea ?” “ Really. Mr.
Andrew, times are too hard.” “Great Scot, man !
I don’t expect you to part-up. I only want your
name. I pay the money. There, you’re down for
a guinea, and many thanks. Come to the pre-
sentation. I’ve ordered the watch from Sydney—
suitably inscribed.” And off went Andrew to
Jones and Robinson and the rest. The watch
duly arrived, a party assembled, wine flowed, and
Andrew made a “ feeling ” speech in reply to the
toast of his health. He would, he said, “ treasure
the token of their generosity,” &c. Then he
calmly invested himself with a 50-guinea ticker,
inscribed : “ Presented to Willoughby Andrew,
Esquire, by the electors of Molong, as a slight
appreciation of his distinguished political talents.’

The man had been dead two days. The
ground had been lumpy always. The dog had
been trained for two years. The man, just before
crossing the golden range, had given the dog to
an old friend. Large crowd at funeral; and,
barring “ Daddy ” Doolan’s glass optic, nob a dry
eye in the lot. Dog very quiet; seemed to know
that he and his master had parted for ever.
Clergyman picked up a clod ; dog’s face bright-
ened. “ Dust to dust, ashes to ashes ” —and the
clod was dropped into the grave. Instantly the
dog bounded in after it and “retrieved” it,
scrambled up a plank, and laid the clod at the
clergyman’s feet. Men struggled to look grave.
The pastor repeated the solemn words, and
dropped a handful of clay ; but when a mouthful
of it was rescued as before, the mourners had to
smile loudly—with lumps in their throats.
“A.G.” : Once travelled from Albury to
Sydney on a pound of tea. Got packet of tea in
Albury. Vile stuff it wan, but that was uiy dart.
Didn’t want a good article or 1 might have gob a
few orders. Was travelling for a firm that didn’t
exist. Came to a house and a»ked if I could do
them a tin or a chest of tea. “ Well, no ;we have
just given an order.” Produced my sample, ex-
patiated on its merits and asked leave to brew a
little. Was ture they’d like it. Made some tea
and, incidentally, had something to eat —a feed,
in fact. Next house (when hungry) the same;
and so right through. Dropped in for three
socials, and a fettler’s wife wanted me to stay for
a month and teach the young ladies ” (her
daughters) to danee. Was 23 days on road, and
occupied 19 feather beds. Not a bad record !
Don f t catch me cadyiny tucker.

Still more animal companionship. Or-
chardist Dorahy, of Dundas, N.S.W., possesses a
greyhound which has “ taken up ” with a mother-
less kitten. Picks puss up by the scruff of the
neck and gallops playfully round a few acres of
orchard ; kitten, not in the least disconcerted, on
being released ooyly taps greyhound’s nose—and
thus tbe twain gambol for hours. The other day,
while carrying the kitten, the greyhound spied a
hare, dropped his playfellow, chased the hare, and
killed it. Then he returned to the homestead
dead hare and live kitten in mouth.

Re animal friendships. Got
a dog at our camp ; a fearful cat-murderer excepb
in case of big Tom—reared with him; and when
cat catches bird he always divides with dog. Also,
at tucker-time, Tommy has first go, and if strange
dog comes along Tomaso helps in the fight.

A Two-wharves pig-farmer went duck-
shooting with his man, Terry, and when the boss
had bagged nine black ducks be handed the gun
to Terry to have a try. Terry stalked up to the
reed-edge, got within 40 or 50 yds. of a small mob
of ducks, and took careful aim. Then he lowered
his gun, aimed again ; then lowered again, and
scratched his head till his boss sneaked up to him.
“Why don’t ya shoot? Y’ave some splendid
chances.” “ Chances, is nt ? Yiss, begorra !
Plinty ovum ; but they was spoilt. Ivirry toime
Oi got ta good aim onta wan ov thim ducks two
or three other divvies wild shwim betune me an’
th’ wan Oi wizz aimin’ nat. Yiss!” Later on,
another beautiful chance offered, and the boss
insisted that Terry should try again. The un-
willing Terry took the gun, aimed carefully at
the right-hand ducks of the flock, and then, as he
{mllea the trigger, swung the gun round to the
eft. Down went Terry, but no ducks were hurt.
Boss rushed up—“ Well, Terry, ya break me up.
Wot did ya swing the gun round for ? ” “ Whoi,
Oi wantid ta pepper th’ whole blessid flock. Yiss.
Am Oi hurrt?
Miss AMY GOURLAY (of Charles Holloway’s co. at Sydney Royal).

Re discussion on boot-destroyingest places,
I will back the scoria cone of Rangitoto moun-
tain, near Auckland, M.L., against any other
place in Australasia. The volcanic rocks cut like
razors and grind like emery wheels.—Old Boots.

A Waanyarra (Vic.) farmer recently saw a
brown snake coiled round a milch-cow’s leg ex-
tracting the milk from her teat. The farmer
watched the operation for some time, fearing to
disturb the thief lest the cow should suffer.

Blank, an affluent N.S.W. squatter con-
siderably over seven, was accused by a neighbor-
ing grazier, suspected of similar practices, of show-
ing hand-fed sheep in grass-fed sections. Blank
watched his opportunity, and when his enemy ex-
hibited in the grass-fed section at the local show,
and the pen was surrounded by admiring wool-ex-
perts, he strolled up, pulled out a handful of oats,
and held them through the rails, saying, as the
jumbucks hustled over each other to get at the
grain—“ These are Mr. Jones’ grass-fed, sheep,
gentlemen. He disna’ call ’em hand-fed till they
can use a knife and fork 1 ’’
* » • *
A N.S.W. squatter, going round with a
Crown Lands assessor, passos a paddock in which
there is a very fine horse. “ Fine animal that,”
says the assessor. “ Give you £2O for him.”
“ Best horse on the run. Wouldn’t take a hun-
dred for him.” “Let’s see, what rent are you
paying?” “ Threepence all round.” “Ah, you
have 20,000 acres of C.L. at 3d , that’s £250.”
Then meditatively, “ A very fine horse ! I really
think, Mr. Blank, you’ve got this land a lot too
cheap. Now, at 6d. your rent would be £500.”
Pause. “ Yes, a very fine horse.” “ Oh, take the
d d horso at your own price,” burst out the
squatter, “if you admire him so much ! ” So £2O
bought tho horse and the rent stayed at three
pence.

“ F.S.” on captive eagles :
Had two Australian eagles in a large netting enclosure
for some time ; but one morning found the smaller of the
pair killed and disembowelled by his friend. Curious to
observe the business-like way in which the remaining
bird kills a rabbit or other small animal. When a rabbit
is put into the enclosure, he simply drops on it like a
stone, grasping it by the neck with one foot so hard that
the victim cannot even squeal. Not long ago, while I
had a trap-door at the bottom open, my cat walked un-
suspiciously into the enclosure, was dropped on, and
duly strangled by the potent claws—the eagle the while
not taking the slightest notice of my yells. He looked a
bit askance at the first guinea-pig I put into him, but
eventually scoffed it, and will now kill as many as I like
to put in.

* *
A corres-
pondent, hav-
ing been be-
guiled into
rapid motion
for once in his
life, and being enraged thereat, writes as fol-
lows :
If country people are slow, they are sure. Got down to
country railway-station half-an-hour too early, thinking
train left at 8.30 a.m., when 9 was the correct time. On
the way, was passed by all sorts of people, who were
hurrying, as though to hurry was a new experience but
imperative, and I put on a spurt myself, thinking watch
might be wrong. When near station, people were
beginning to run, and I was scared into a bit of a sprint
myself. Found waiting-room full of people, who looked
as though they had almost missed the train ; yet found it
did not go for 30 minutes. Fancy some must have spent
the night there, and that those I had seen running were
really bound for the 5 p.m. train, and were anxious not
to be late.

He was a N.S.W. P.M. who lived by rule
and was very particular never to be out after 7
p.m., but at 4 o’clock one evening a well-known
squatter burst into his room at the hotel, and,
slapping him on the back genially, said, “ Come
on, Jones, get your coat on and come out with me.
Just got in from Melbourne.” In vain Jones
pleaded his rule. “Oh ! hang the rule,” said the
pastoralist. “It’s only 35 miles, I’ve got four
demons in the trap, and I’ll rattle you out in three
hours.” They reached the station, had dinner,
and then the squatter asked to be excused for a
few minutes; had to see his overseer. He’d
hardly gone, when a man was announced as
wanting to make the then necessary sworn declara-
tion about his land, and the reluctant P.M. was
thereafter kept busy taking about thirty other
similar perjuries. Soon after he was through, the
wool-king returned full of apologies, and the in-
dignant Jones recounted how he’d been kept hard

SILAS SNELL.
THE EDITOR COMES TO TOWN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was the man from Jindabye,
And none might hustle him ;
He got aboard the Sydney train.
And, oh, but he looked grim !
His eyes beneath his bristling brows
Shone with a savage light,
And two revolvers in his belt
Gleamed truculently bright.
His luggage was a carpet bag.
For very light flew he ;
Yet by his side a vxuldy lay,
Ferocious for to see.
“ I am,’’ said he, “ the editor
O' th' Western Eaolk Et« ;
It circulates in Cudgeroop,
Cow Flat and Jindaby e.
“ I’m going down to Sydney now
Some type to buy -ahem!—
A den of thieves is Sydney town,
But I'm prepared for thkm I ”
Then up and spake a passenger—
“ Your talk is mighty free ;
Not all are thieves in Sydney town—
Take back your words," said hr.
" I mean ihe Sydney editors,"
The other man replies—
“ They steal my leading article*—
their eyes.”

“ M R.” ; We had always considered Tom
a very quiet specimen of the bush native, but the
other day he quite climaxed his reticence. He
kept the li very-stables in the little township near
which we spent our summer-holidays, and used
often to drive us out, on which occasions our
efforts to make him talk were dreadful failures.
The other day he drove us out as usual, and, on
the way home, we saw smoke in the direction of
the village. “ Hello ! ” exclaimed one of our
party, “ there’s been a fire.” “ Yes,” replied
Tom, with a slow smile, “ that’s my stable —it was
burnt down this morning.”

The story of an out-back wedding :
A wealthy squatter employed one Jones as rouseabout,
dummy, boundary-rider, or cattle-lifter to suit the occa-
sion. This adaptive genius had a daughter who favored
her father. She was the best horse-breaker in the dis-
trict, her vocabulary of abuse was more extensive than
that of any local station-hand or bullocky. It was quite
common for her to go alone after cattle. Then she would
boil her hilly and sleep under a log, quite oblivious of any
feminine misgivings. Her friends called her “ Clothes-
peg ’ on general principles. She chummed in with
another versatile genius in the cattle-duffing line; and
lived with him—clearing out. at intervals when thrashed
with unusual severity. A neighboring cocky’s wife
sought to reclaim Nelly and to remove such a scandal
from her front slip-rails. So she interviewed Bill and
induced him to promise to marry Nell. But that lady
had cleared out for the time, and had “to be fetched.”
After some persuasion she consented to become a wife,
i But,” said our stout tramp-chaser, “ remember, Nell,
if I take you out you must stay with me, and not go to
Bill again till you are married.” “All-right, Missus.”
The Missus drove Nell out of town, and all went well till
the gate near Bill’s hut was reached. Nell got down,
opened the gate for the cart, and then—“ So-long, Missus,
thanks for the lift.” “ But, Nellie, you promised to stay
with me till yon xvere married ; you know it is very wrong
to live with Bill like that.” “D n the odds,” quoth
Clothes-peg, “ it won’t be worse than it's been all along.
So long!” The good dame naturally felt angry at being
fooled so neatly. However, when the bush parson next
appeared, she hauled him and his wife into her cart and
drove to the dummies’ hut. The bridegroom was found
stretched on his back under a “contagious tree " smok-
ing contentedly, but the bride was axva.v rounding up a
mob of horses. Presently she appeared, seated “ clothes-
peg” fashion on a half-broken colt, which raced among
the timber in perilous style. Presently she saw the
visitors, and most demurely the bride rode up to greet
the parson. “ Good morning, Nellie. This is the minis-
ter; he’s come to marry you. Bill’s willing.” “ But I
ain't got no rings. We can’t get married.” “ Have you
a door-key?" asked the parson ; “ that would do.” Then
the missis lent a ring. Bill went “to clean himself,” and
presently appeared in clean flannel-shirt and moles. Liz
produced a gorgeous silk jacket, and asked if that would
do; but she considered a clean-up quite an unneces-
sary luxury. Her filthy face and hands, matted hair, and
dirty, tattered dress strongly contrasted with Bill’s neat-
ness. The pair were arranged in one room of the hut,
with the pledge of their affection, now a big lump of a
boy, in the background. The ceremony over, the parson
grounded his black pants on the dirt floor and prayed
long and earnestly for the couple. Then Nell got out
the billy. “ Now, you must ’ave a tot of tea, Mister;
you're very dry." But the guests could not stay for
the wedding-breakfast.

“ Tassy ” ; Term “ bluey ” never applied
to swag in Tasmania. The Tas. “ bluey ”is a
rough overcoat of blue-grey woollen, and never
-ecn by writer in any other part of Australasia.
GARRY, a Wide Bay black athlete, and his wives.
Perroux, photo.
Gurry, the Wide Bay aboriginal, pictured
herewith, with his two wives—“one,” as he
used to say, “for week days, and the other for
Sundays is the man who, getting tired of his
week day partner, bashed her on the head with a
nulla, was arrested on a charge of murder, escaped,
was recaptured, had no “ true bill ” found against
him, hashed his Sunday wife in turn, got away,
and is still at large.

The M’Cooey on limbed
snakes and legless lizards :
The carpet snake has posterior rudi-
mentary limbs, as has also the diamond
snake, which (bar color markings) is
strictly identical with the carpet snake,,
and should never have been classed os
a distinct species. Liasis childreni, the
mighty 22-feet Queensland and W.A.
snake,' will probably prove to have
rudimentary limbs. No venomous Aus-
tralian snake has these ; but a vtnoin-
ous legged snake is found in Papua.
No country has nearly so many venom-
ous snakes as Australia, yet no Austra-
lian lizard is venomous, not even the
so-called “ woodadder,” about which
the blackfellow and the bushmen have
made so much mystery. The bush-
man of to-day, however, is as ignorant
(scientifically) as the bushman of 60
.years ago, and still believes that the
death-adder has a sting in its tail,
and that, the young kangaroo grows on
its mother's (eat.
As to legless lizards, Australia has
many (very snake-like to the inexpert
and always called snakes in the hush).
Here, however, are two out of numer-
ous generic distinctions: All lizards
have ear-holes ; snakes have not; and
whereas in snake-like legless lizards the
tongue is single and somewhat leaf-like
in form, in all snakes the tongue is
bifid or forked.

“A.E.G.”: lie “hairy men.”
There was one once in Jmgera
Ranges. A Braid wood civil-ser-
vant was in the habit of stalking
wallabies, in a bear skin suit, and
Maloney, riding up that way when
the C.S. was out for sport, got such
a “divvle of a fright ” that neither
he nor his neighbors would ven-
ture out at night for years. A
man who goes about in a wallaby
country with a hairy suit carries
his life in his hand.
Mac had offered his place for sale, stating
amongst other things that the ground was timbered
with gidyea, willows, etc. Buyer came up and,
after riding about for a week, asked Mac where
the willows and gidyea were. Mac pointed to
some scrubby bushes. “ There they are.” Buyer :
“ That’s only rubbish.” Mac : “ Well, I call
them willows. I’m not a blanky botanist! ”
THE SUPERSTITIOU'S PICNIC MAID.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The picnic maid and her “ to be,” .
Far out at “ Lovers’ Bay,”
Sat silently and watched the sea
Against the islets play.
Around her neatly-fitting vest
His arm he coyly stole —
When on his breast her head found rest
He reached a blissftd goal.
“ But why so sad, dear Owyn ? ” he said,
“ What can the thoughtlets be
That throng your head, and that have led
You into reverie ? ”
The maiden sighed, and sobbed: “Dear Mac,
Oh, this is why Fm sad:
I’ve counted back, from you to Jack,
The sweethearts I have had,
“ And find that ” —here she raised a tear
To ease the parting-scene—
“ Our courtship, dear, must end, I fear,
For you just make thirteen ! ”

WHEN DOCTORS DISAGREE.
There is a heated controversy just now in Sydney scientific circles as to whether the typical Australian is a herbivorous or a carnivorous animal.
When the matter has been fully threshed out in the daily press, the truth will doubtless be found to lie somewhere between the two above extremes.

THE PUBLIC ARE WARNED against dealing with
Infringers of our Patents.
Business, Robbery, etc.
N.S. Wales G.P.O. is trying to block the smartiea
who make a saving by sending a long telegram in two
divisions. Thus a 20-word gram to Victoria would cost
2s. Bd. (la. the first 10 words and 2d. every additional),
but by making two separate messages of it it would go
through tor 2s. A clever Sydneyite, who was recently
prevented from doing this at a branch-office, now sends
two halves of the “ wire" from different offices ; ad-
dressee, of course, being posted in the dodge. If foiled
in this, he would probably submit the messages with dif-
ferent signatures.
• • * •
The small shopkeeper’s paper-bag fake is toler-
ably obvious. The bags are made to weigh from ljoz. to
Soz., by which amount the purchaser, therefore, receives
less than he pays for. Thus, in a pound of biscuits at,
say, 4d., there will be SJd. worth of biscuits, and a ha’porth
of bag. This is paying at the rate of fid. a dozen for bags
which don’t oust the tradesman 2d. per dozen.
• • * •
Australian wines get a poor Bhow in Sydney.
For a “ baby ” bottle (reputed half-pint) of wine” of any
well-advertised brand you pay sixpence in the restaurant;
but for a reputed pint of same wine Is. fid. is invariably
extorted. Why ? Also, how ?

“ Jack ” : IU “On Various Kinds of Grog,”
the advts. quoted by Bcllstis appear in London Times,
Telbgraph and Standard, or did, a year or so back. Also,
30 years ago similar ads. were to be seen in all leading
London newspapers. Likewise the manufacture on
chemical lines of superior clarets, ports, brandies, &c.,
has been going on for a century in England, HoHand and
Oeruiany. And, of cousse, the industry gets out this
side. Writer knows a man who makes a fat income at
the biz, and, what is more, drinks his own liquors. But
he takes pills—any quantity ; so, maybe, that levels up
the immorality of the thing.

Malle: Was in a Sydney tobacconist’s when a
ragged newsboy, about 3ft. high, came in and offered
Evks’ Noosk. Said the shopkeeper : “ The boy I got my
papers from hasn’t been here for four days. You bring
me a last edition every’ night; but mind, you’ve got to
buy a penn’orth of cigarettes from me every week. If I
spend money with other people, I expect them to spend
with me, so you’ll get flvepence a week and a penn’orth
of cigarettes." The Legislature will soon have to tackle
the juvenile smokers’ question on the Canadian basis.
• • * *
A rustic-looking youth is going about Sydney
taking down small tobacconists thus. With a wayback
look and a 3d. cigarette packet containing (say) five
“ fags " in his (right) pocket, he enters a smoke shop and
buys a 3d. packet, which he puts into his right pocket.
Then, as if wanting a smoke, he pulls out the “ readied ”
packet and “discovers” the shortage. The tobacconist,
being in the habit of breaking packets to oblige juveniles
with thrce-a-pcnnv, thinks he has made a mistake, and
hands over another full packet. Then the guileless one
walks off with five smokes to the good.

“J.F.D.”: The struggle for a crust. A big
Sydney tobacco-man lately advertised for a coachman,
and of the crowd of whips who turned up the majority
tried to indicate in some way their patronage of the ad-
vertiser’s firm (ciirarette-box sticking out of vest pocket;
cutting pipeful off one of firm's plugs, Ac.). The irony
of it all was that the employer hired a
non-smoker, and gave a bit of gratis ad-
vice to the rest about the evils of too
much cloud-blowing.

“ S.S.” : The favorite happy hunt-
ing-pound of & class of Melb. and Sydney
spielers for some time past h«& been cer-
tain Westralian steamboats, and the
brotherhood have made many journeys
East and West, mostly profitable. On
board ship a man will speculate on the
“ little queen,” or the alert, mysterious
pea, who would not think of risking a
copper on land where other dissipations
and distractions are comparatively numer-
ous. On a recent boat from W.A. the
“buttoner "of a party of three was a hard-
faced young man who spent most of the
first 48 hours sprawling round, obtru-
sively reading a large, fat, black Bible.
He looked about as pious as a Port Mel-
bourne plug-ugly; but the fake worked.
The party relied on that venerable de-
vice, the thimble and pea, to take down
the unwise men of the West, and suc-
ceeded wonderfully, the Bible'student de-
veloping a shocking and insatiable appetite
for rash speculation. The man with the
thimble cleaned out the passengers, and
the student cleaned out the man with
the thimble. Hints that stewards have a
finger in the sea-pie of the spielers are
not wanting.

A Bulletin correspondent dis-
courses on the don’t-vou-feel-well business:
“ Even the cleverest quacks give away
their game when in boastful mood. One
eminent Melb. nervous-dehility-and-opti-
cal'snecks man often makes merry over
his dupes. ‘When a patient comes in I
first feel him as to previous doc to is, etc.
Then examination and relation of symp-
toms. I>on’t matter what his complaint
is or whether he has any. With a look
•f horror I exclaim “ Good God, man ! how
long have you been like this? I>o you
know what’s the matter with you?” It’s
all the same whether he does or not. I
tell him it’s a very serious and complicated
case and will need a long and expensive
treatment. Then 1 demand P 5 down and
tell him the medicine is 10/6 a bottle—
two bottles a week and a guinea for a con-
sultation every week till you are cured.
My medicines are all made up on well-
known formulas, and my terms are dictated
according to the fright of my patient and
his appearance. The wearing of a gold
watch and chain would raise the first
demand to a tenner.’ This scientist., a
very uneducated mail, came to Australia
in a very menial * capacity. Another
quack—a very successful ‘ palmist’—says
tlxit he does not rare about men cus-
tomers. Women are the best because
their gloves, jewellery, pronunciation
are all good pointers. ‘ And as I do
the clairvoyant trance business I can watch the
effect of my “feelers” without alarming them. A
common dodge with women is for a single woman to wear
a wedding ring and for a married one to put hers in her
pooket, but when I hold their hands during my “trance,”
my first pointer is the mark on the ring finger, which
is easily read ; and a trance utterance commanding a
married woman to put on her ring—“ You cannot deceive
■me. madam ! is as good a start as you could wish for.’ ”
» • • *
A tenant (Mi*. Smith), of a large city house-
agent breaks all shifting-records. Smith’s family con-
sists chiefly of a wife and four villainous olive-branches ;
and the furniture of a boiler, two saucepans, four mat-
tresses, some chairs, a table, and two hair-brushes. The
family lives on old man Smith ; what old man Smith
lives on is hard to tell. Four years ago Smith
came to this house-agent, “rented” one of his slum
houses, paid 2s. deposit, and ever since his rent
has averaged Is. a week irregularly. When the
agent realised the unshiftableness of Smith, and the
fact that his props wouldn’t pay bailiff's charges, he
made terms with him. Smith moves about once a month
into one of the agent’s empty houses, and pays 4s. or so,
which the agent gets as his commission for letting the
house. In four weeks he reports to the landlord that he
has ejected Smith as an undesirable tenant, and Smith
goes into another cottage—same agent, but different
landlord. If Smith hasn’t the 4s. or so, which happens
frequently, he makes it square by banging the house to
pieces a bit—the agent has a relative who does the re-
? >airs. He isn’t always called Smith ; for fear the land-
ords should compare notes, he is put down as Jones and
Bartholomew and Brown and Ferdinand Poniatowski.
When he has been in every one of that agent’s houses,
and the game seems really to be getting played-out, the
agent reckons that he can get MX by selling Smith to an-
other agent who doesn’t know the game yet.
• • • *
The law which provides that the landlord must
have first cut at a debtor’s goods and chattels is made a
source of profit in Tasmania to both landlord and tenant.
The tenant of a shop stocks up on credit and doesn’t pay;
the creditor gets an order and sends along the bailiff ; the
latter finds that the landlord’s claim for rent is so big that
no other creditor has a show of a div., and retires from
the field defeated. Landlord and tenant then whack the
profits of the “ trade.”
• • • •
A Vic. P.M. lately recommended the registra-
tion of dealers in gold, apropos of a case wherein some
boys were found guilty of gold-stfealing. A Chinaman
was their “ fence,” and the bench at once waxed wroth.
Chinese dealers are always willing to buy a few grains at
a time, paying l$d. a grain, or thereabouts, according to
the locality. They buy from needy fossickers, both men
and boys* Doubtless some they buy is got “ on the
cross,” but it is not the Chow who is “ fence ” to the gold-
thief in a large way of dishonesty. Highly respectable
jewellers and the unimpeachable bank are in it. All gold
is honest gold as soon as it gets into the melting-pot—at
any rate, no one can prove it otherwise. The Chow
would probably conduct his operations despite Registra-
tion Acts ; and the jeweller, the country storekeeper, the
pawnbroker, and the hanker are so eminently respectable
that they would get licenses for the asking.
* • * •
The boss of a large Australian business house
opened his festive soul last Xmas eve. He wrote the
names of the employes—twenty-nine of them —on slips of
paper and put them in a hat, also put a corresponding
number of slips in another hat. On one of the latter
slips was written the lonesome word “ Ham.” They were
drawn out of the hats until the ham had been won. The
winner was notified of his luck, and solemnly handshaken
and congratulated by the boss, who said, with a break in
his voice, that they had worked together for many years,
and had always been friends, and that he (the boss)
rejoiced in his good fortune in winning the ham, and
trusted that he would live long to enjoy it. That was all
the Xtnas-box there was in that establishment.
* • • *
It is nearly 25 years since J. Bull “ fell in ” to the
tune of £30,000, or so, over the ingenious Haven mine
swindle. The man who brought off this coup turned up
in London with a few dozen genuine copies of the annual
report of the M.L. Mines Minister, which gave the gold-
returns of all the leading mines at the Thames —then the
star goldfield. Then he got lithographed in London a
gorgeous map grouping all the rich mines mentioned in
AT A SALVATION ARMY ENCAMPMENT.
the official report around a piece of ground which had no
existence in fact, and attached the faked plan to the
State paper in a manner suggesting that it was part and
parcel of same document. It would have deceived the
very devil himself.

Major Quimhy, who, from his long connection
with explosives, ought to know, tells a great story of
kalienite—called after one Callaghan, if you please—in tiv-
manufacture of which eucalyptus leaves are used with
much advantage. It is claimed to be clean, safe, smoke-
less, fumeless, and strong'; it will, the Major says, S> lve
Australians the best explosive on earth, and afford em-
ployment to thousands of skilled and unskilled people;
European armies and navies are to be supplied with the
finest smokeless powder ever made. Hitherto, the Major
has represented the California Powder Co., which had
much to do with the cheapening of dynamite in Australia
though it is much too dear even now. Last year, an
arrangement was made under which the Major s co. was
to “ pull out” of the country, and it seems a very season-
able time for the new power to “ pull in.” Anyhow, the
syndicate running the new explosive should do all it
knows to keep the Major, for there is no man on God s
great earth” that can “let off” on powder like that
warrior.
“ Softgoodsman ”: lb is quite erroneous, dear
Bulletin, that the Sydney wholesalers in our line unani-
mously favor Freetrade. Last year,
to my knowledge, three at least of
the York-street houses made no
profit. Cause not far to seek.
There are dozens of men here,
without either capital or stock,
calling themselves “ indent
merchants.” To these can the
smallest draper go and indent
any of the bread-and-butter
lines for as small an amount
as £5 worth. Most Sydney
drapers do so indent, and
only come to the warehouses
for the fancy lines in which
Fashion is the sole arbiter
of profit or loss. There
is tremendous risk in im-
porting a line of ladies’
hats, which may not
catch on at all; there
is no risk at all
in such lines as
Horrocks’ long-
cloths or lino-
leums, but the
warehousemen
with big estab-
lishments to keep
up cannot com-
pete in these arti-
cles with the kerb-
stone merchants. A
stiff duty would ham-
per this indenting busi-
ness, and throw the
trade done by “ in-
den ters” into the
hands of the ware-
housemen.
Vic. shires are
allowed to permit
farmers to put fences
out several feet
in the road-
way to protect
live fences un-
til they have
properly grown.
When the permit
is granted, it is
not uncommon
for the farmer to
plough up to the
protecting
fence, to
plant an-
other
live
fence
there,
to apply
for a permit
for a fence to
protect it, and—to get it. The
councillors indeed, being farmers them-
selves, are not at all averse to granting one another per-
mits. This will explain the peculiar narrowness of many
three-chain roads in agricultural Victoria.

More soldiers, more canned meat wanted.
More “ conquests,” still more soldiers wanted to hold the
new territory, also more canned meat wanted. So
the ’Murkan pork-millionaires have become enthusiastic
“ Imperialists.” And, knowing the importance of yelling
all together, they are forming a “ combine.”
Monsieur Lautier has conceived wnat he
thinks to be a novel plan of punishing those
Frenchwomen who sometimes get into trouble
and then wish to avoid the consequences. The
Chamber of Deputies, only too anxious to aid
in this direction, has received Lautier’s petition
eagerly. His idea is that the State should pro-
videaninstitution wherethe young mothermight
be afforded an opportunity of clandestinely dis-
posing of her offspring, and thus avoid the shame
which usually causes nine-tenths of infanticide.
Quite seriously the petition goes on to say that
“ Instead of condemning these poor unfortu-
nates to travauxforces (hard labor) they should
be condemned to tnfants fords, and despatched
to one of the colonies and there compelled to
produce one, two or three children whether
they like it or not, thus assisting in the work
of colonisation.” The Chamber of Deputies has
taken this suggestion much to heart, and has
forwarded the petition to the Minister of the
Interior, whoever he may be. All this recalls
Gen. Gallieni’s scheme for the taxation of all
bachelors in Madagascar, or of such of the male
population as fail to account for the addition of
three little colonials to the population. France
seems resolved to force fruit to grow upon all
fig-trees, promising or otherwise, that cumber
the ground of her colonies. But think of the
wild humor of sentencing a woman to three
years compulsory infant—or three years hard
infant, whichever you like to call it. Liberty,
Equality, and Maternity !
Ten Years of Mistakes.
“ Experience is nothing but mistakes, and a
memory,” a writer has said. From mistakes man
learns caution. Caution equalises judgment. So
man becomes mature. Mr. Edward I. Jones,
Financial Agent, writing from the N.S.W.
Masonic Club, Pitt-street, Sydney, says : “I am
writing purely out of gratitute, for I have hereto-
fore been very much opposed to patent medicines,
and it was only after a great amount of persuasion
that I was induced to try Warner’s Safe Cure. I
had been suffering for the last ten years from a
variety of complaints, including insomnia. I did
not get any permanent relief until I tried War-
ner’s Safe Cure. In all I have taken about seven
bottles, and I am happy to say I am now com-
pletely cured.”

HOW TO KEEP HEALTHY.
General Debility is a state which is generally caused by a combination of nervous exhaustion, indi-
gestion, deranged liver, and constipation. It is brought about by the system becoming run down.
The functions of the body are irregular and weak, the waste matter is not carried off properly, the
blood becomes poor, and sallow. The whole system, in cases like this, needs “ toning
up.” The diet must be regulated, and a remedy must be taken that will put the digestive organs in
thorough working order. For this work, Bile Beans for Biliousness are the very best specific, as they
act in a gentle manner, and are so compounded that they repair the waste of the nervous system, and
make, pure, rich blood. Bile Beans on every hand are effecting cures for debilitv and all summer
ailments, such as loss of tone, lack of physical force, &e., &c. Sold at a low price, they come within
the reach of all; and that the great mass of Australians appreciate this fact is illustrated by the fact
that last year over 20 million doses were taken in Australia alone.

POLITICAL
POINTS
(i The Queensland Ministry is going in for
an extensive building programme,” and elegant
atone court-houses, lock-ups, gaols, post-offices,
«c., are to be built, with borrowed money, all over
the province—with a judicious eye to the approach-
ing general election.
Premier Dickson, when Minister for Rail-
ways, was fined in Brisbane police-court 10s. and
£1 Bs. 2d. costs for employing a Kanaka contrary
to law. It speaks volumes as to the character of
the man, now apeing popular leadership, that on
that occasion he pleaded Parliamentary privilege.
The local R.C. Archbishop has announced
that, so far as the Catholics are concerned, the
coming S.A. elections are to be fought out on the
Capitation-Grant question. He asks for £4 for
•very child taught in a R.C. school who passes the
present compulsory standard. There is no yellow
aog in S. A., but this injudicious demand is round-
ing up all the heathen into one antagonistic camp.
It has come within the sphere of the
practical to speculate who shall play the nursing
Part towards the Commonwealth Bill which Deas-
Thomson and Wentworth played towards the
Constitution Bill forty-five years ago. Thomson
and Wentworth carried the Bill to England and
Btayed there till they saw it safely through the
Imperial Parliament. Who will stand on Barton’s
left?
That English press-suggestion to call the
Australian Federal capital “Britannia” doesn’t
go far enough. Why not call it “ Jaybool ” right
out, and style Sydney “ London,” and the Parra-
matta River the “ Thames.” The Bulletin
doesn't advocate such a choice, but it strongly
suspects that, “when the numbers go up,” the
name of the Federal metropolis will be plain
“Parkes.”
Common sense from Justice Chubb on Q.
Electoral Act: - “ lam of opinion tint if a man
camps under a gum-tree for s>x months in one
electorate, or in a tent or hut in the one electorate,
it is sufficient residence, the intention of the Act
being that if a man resides six .months in any
electorate it does not matter how often he changes
about in it during that six months, he is entitled
to be on the roll ” Truly—times have altered in
Bananaland.
S.A. Labor-party is in fear and trembling
le*t Federation be the big question at the approach-
ing Assembly elections. The Labor Ms.P. op-
posed it to a man at the referendum last year, but
now that individual seats depend upon the atti-
tude, the position is entirely different. S.A.
electors have a firm idea that Federation will pay
that province, and the candidate who opposes it
stands a big chance of defeat. And so Labor
Ms.P. ponder.
In the old lag days of N.S. W., every captain
leaving |>ort had to hand in to the police* 24 hours
before sailing, a complete list of his crew and pas-
sengers, so that the escaping convict could be
recovered and enthusiastically flogged. The law
still exists ; when the Shipping Acts were consoli-
dated last session no one had sense enough to
take it out, and it was solemnly re-enacted. It is
an impossible law nowadays, for often a vessel
doesn’t stay in port 24 hours altogether—yet the
penalty for non-oompliance is £5O.
John Forrest was a much quieter man this
Federal Convention than last. Last time Wes-
tralia was still staggering on the pinnacle of the
boom. Its customs revenue averaged £7 per head
annually against £2 in the other provinces, and
John was great on the injustice of asking his
great, rich province to join the others on even
terms. Within the last year all the other pro-
vinces have improved while W. A. is the falling
Hooley of Australasia, so Premier Forrest wasn’t
quite the same loud Bigjohn this time.
The S.A. Labor-party is running a can-
didate against Works-Minister Jenkins at Sturt;
more, it is giving its nominee a free hand on the
subject of the Capitation-Grant in the hope of pick-
ing up valuable R.C. support. The Conservatives
are hoping that the Laborists will also run a man
against Kingston at W. Adelaide as it is believed
such a course would help to drive the Premier,
admittedly the strongest man in local politics, a
gfK>d way towards the Tories. Each of the dis-
tricts named already returns one Laborist.
The Australian Star threw in its lot
with the Federationists last Monday, and as
S.M. Herald and Evening News were always
on that side, the Telegraph is left as the only
wobbler and the only hope of the Little Cornstalk
party. At time of writing that paper hadn’t com-
mitted itself in its leader columns, but it has been
tentatively pulling the public leg in casual sneers
among the news - matter to see if the leg
in question will come off or not. Knox and
Tom Slattery have also appeared as the lone
precursors of the crowd with the small
parish intellect; and Slattery in particular groans
about Victoria having got the best of the case—as
if it had been a question of prigging a handker-
chief at the Water Police Court. And the Man
Fridays of one or two men who haven’t spoken
out personally are beginning to send along para-
graphs which The Bulletin doesn’t print, and
which paragraphs show how the wind may blow
by-and-by if it doesn’t pay the wind better to
blow in the other direction. But things are rather
quiet as yet. Jack Want, now placidly fishing
for tittlebats in the Red Sea, is greatly missed.
How does Central Queensland’s claim for
separation from Brisbane look as compared with
Brisbane s old-time claim for separation from
oydney? Old Moreton Bay demanded severance
from N.S.W. mainly on figures of population and
revenue, and because it was big enough and old
to start for itself. Moreton Bay had then
a P°P ldat ' on ; Central Queensland has
now 160,000 ; Moreton Bay’s annual exports then
amounted to £523,000, the Central district’s now
are £2,240,000. And soon. Either, therefore, on
figures, Moreton Bay had no claim for separation
40 years ago or Central Queensland has a better
claim now.
I’he Vic. Premier sticks to his intention of
not moving re the new Federal Bill till it has been
formally and definitely accepted in N.S.W. He
v 8 *vr ! n an d the other Iscariots of the
Yes-No Ministry, and still regards it as possible
that they will permit the insertion of another im-
possible minimum, or demand more concessions,
or ,the unreformed Council to reject the
whole thing. If N.S.W. accepts, Turner promises
to push the matter through with all possible speed,
but if it doesn’t he will save his good red money
? nd have no referendum. The other Premiers
bold the same view—all save Kingston, who pro-
poses to save time and cash and stationery by
combining the Federal Referendum with the
general election.
Wales Ms.P. still plot to have their
letters on public business” franked, and as
Premier Reid is of opinion that the privilege may
be legally conceded by Executive minute alone,
franking may begin any day. It seems that
letters of Vio. members, if sent to the Parliamen-
tary Library, are stamped at public expense. S.A.
follows a similar practice. Tasmanian member's
are allowed to frank without supervision ; they are
even permitted during the session to frank their
private correspondence. Queensland permits no
postal exemptions. Maoriland allows Ministers
to frank letters of members when assured that the
correspondence _ is on public business. But the
practice lends itself so readily to abuse that the
arguments for extending it to N.S.W. are weaker
than are those for abolishing it where it now
obtains.
A meeting got together the other day at
far-out Milparinka (N.S.W.) for the purpose of
specifically calling the local member, Sleath,
M.L. A., a clumsy and bellowing Ananias. Sleath
recently declared in Parliament that the local gold-
warden and P.M. was 96 years old, that he could
neither see nor hear, that he wouldn’t go down a
mine if he was offered £lOOO and the Premiership,
that he had never given a decision which wasn’t
upset on appeal, that he had one foot in the grave
and the other in the public-house, that the Public
Service Board appointed him and also raised his
salary, and that Milparinka was 250 miles from
Wilcannia. And the meeting replied that its
P.M. was 76, went down mines constantly, could
see and hear well, was in good health, didn’t
drink, that not one of his decisions had ever been
upset on appeal, that he wasn’t appointed by the
P.S. Board, that the Board didn’t raise his salary,
but reduced it, and that Wilcannia was only 169
miles from Milparinka. And it oalled on the
leather-lunged Bore of N.S.W. Assembly to come
out of that famous wine shop in Park-street, Syd-
ney, and apologise.

Just seven months ago Barton was howled
at as a traitor to N.S.W. and a hireling of the
other provinces because he talked of a compromise
on the Federal question. In these days the only
all-wool patriot was the one who proposed to ex-
plain that N.S.W. wanted certain things and was
going to have all of them immediately. What a
fall it all is now ! The rivers question, and the
judicial appeal question, and the money bills
matter, and the financial settlement, and the
Braddon Blot all remain as they were, and Premier
Reid pledges himself, as a genuine and consistent
Blotter, to fight in defence of every one of them to
the very last. Even with his record as boss
chameleon of Australia the spectacle of Reid
perishing in the last ditch before a hair of the Blot
shall be'injured looks a little unusual.

Alleged that one Bear, of Thursday Island,
is being run against Hamilton for Cook (Q.) elec-
torate by the Thursday Island pearling-boat-
owners and the Japs. Hamilton supported the
Bill to prevent Japs, from owning or renting boats,
a restriction which, while naturally disliked by
the little brown men, is also disfavored by the
boat-owners, because they cannot depend upon the
wages-Jap. to bring back all the shell taken. It
will be a new development of the alien difficulty if
the Japs, practically elect the next member for
Cook.
Announced for the several-billionth time
and a fraction that the Maoriland Opposition
“ intends to make a determined effort to overthrow
the Government at next elections.” The Opposi-
tion Samson has been making that same deter-
mined effort ever since ’9o—and this is ’99. Sed-
don goes to the country with a treni ndous handi-
cap this time, for the country grows a trifle weary
of any Ministry when it has lasted out three com-
plete Parliaments. But The Bulletin stakes its
dollars on the Seddon Cabinet for next election
all the same.
In Freetrade Eng-
land just now some kind
of an effort is in progress
to raise the age at which
children may start work
in factories to 12 years—
at present they are al-
lowed to start at eleven.
Out of 1,084,000 persons
occupied in the British
cotton and woollen fac-
tories, over 80,000 are
children between 11 and
13, about 180,000 are boys
and girls between 13 and
18 ; and the females of
all ages number 656,000,
or more than three-fifths
of the whole. In Vic-
toria (which has the larg-
est proportion of female
workers in Australasia)
one-fourth of the factory
operatives are females,
all over 13, and the shoddy
press always calls this
“one of the manifest evil
results of Protection.”
After all, Queens-
land Premier Dickson is
not an utter fool. The
late Premier Byrnes
processed through the
remoter parts of the
country in a limelight
flare of promises and
rosy pro g n osticacions.
His successor tours the
North in the pose of a
painfully non-committal
politician, whose modest
reserve seems to subtly
suggest that all the for-
mer heads of the Con-
tinuous Ministry have
been reckless blowhards
and unreliable windbags.
“ Of course, I can’t pro-
mise,” or, “I never make
a promise I cannot keep,”
are the qualifications to
every speech of his, and
people hear and say, “Lo
and behold ! here we
have the conscientious
statesman at last ! ”
Already Goulburn, Junee, Cooma, B.om-
bala, Delegate, Bathurst, Forbes, Albury, Armi-
dale, and other prospective Federal capitals begin
to wake up again. The agreement that the capital
is not to be within 100 miles of Sydney shuts out
Moss Yale, Katoomba, Mount Victoria, and other
applicants, but all the rest are left in. N.S.W.
will want the capital just as close to the 100-mile
line as can be arranged. Queensland will want it
north—as far north as possible— from Sydney ;
Victoria and S.A. will want it south or south-
west. But what the Conference quite forgot to
specify was that the capital should be not less than
2000 ft. above sea-level—2sooft. for preference—
so that the Federal Government won't be an aim-
less, collapsed, floppy wreck like some of the pro-
vincial Legislatures down in the lowlands.
Queensland Govt, brags about having
thrown open one million acres to selection within
the last two years, and it is certainly able to show
that it has sold somewhere about that area of land.
But it transpires that the whole area has been cut
up into sixty selections, and that 40 out of these
60 comprise close on 20,000 a. each. There isn’t
one “ selection ” among the lot small enough to
be within the reach of the common variety of
selector. A million acres have certainly been
thrown open to the big squatting freeholders, and
the speculators and banks—but that isn’t exactly
the same thing.
Postmaster-General Parkes (N.S.W.) in-
tends to open the post and telegraph offices on Sun-
day. How does Holy Brunker manage to stay in the
same Ministry with this reprobate ? But for that
matter, now that ho allows the banana-shops and
tobacconists and soft-drink places to be open on
Sunday, and half the Sydney pubs, are doing
business that day, and Manly is a howling scene
of drunk on the I-)ay of K,e*t, how does Brunker
manage to stay in the Ministry along with himself
nowadays ? The spectacle of any sainted auc-
tioneer winking at his own conscience for the sake
or office is sad to look upon ?

N.S. W. Animals’ Protection Society should
thoroughly sift the story that some cattle in transit
from lenterfield to Sydney are kept 2i days in
the trucks without food or water. What the lack
of the latter means, for such a time and in such
equatorial weather, the people responsible should
be given an opportunity of personally experi-
encing.
There’s a charming flavor about the request
of a lawyer in a Sydney court, t’other day, that
the bench would deal leniently with a defendant,
as it was the first time the latter “had been
found ” evading the law.
The dust-storms have howled with unusual
malignity this summer in S.A. Lately, it was
reported that the town of Virginia was blocked by
sand-drifts at both ends, the invading desert being
3ft. deep on the main road and still accumulating.
Some houses even had to be abandoned, because
the sand poured in at all crevices. There were
obviously two things to be done : Either the in-
habitants could turn out en masse and shovel away
the sand, so as to keep their town from being cut
off and possibly obliterated by the sand-heaps or
they could moan to Govt, to do it for them. They
moaned, of course—they always do in Australia—
and Govt, spent .£25 on the job, and, when it was
finished, the drifts rolled in again. But the in-
habitants even then didn’t get a shovel apiece and
rush at the job. They were left moaning.
J.F.D. on certain Sydney ’bus matters
“T.D.”(B. 7/1/99) states that when riding on the railing
of a'bus he was (on the driver receiving a signal that a
transit officer was about) ignoininiously packed inside
because the driver “ being a pore man couldn't afford to
be fined 16 bob.” Then he spreads himself about the
absurdity of having uniformed officials to catch 'busmen
tripping. But the 'busdriver is often so busily occupied
in driving in a crowded street that he does not perceive
persons of the “ T.D.” stamp who will persist in jumping
on a ’bus already carrying its full complement, and thus
not only making t he driver liable to fine but inconvenienc-
ing those already seated. If private-clothes officers are
wanted, it is to deal with every hoodlum who hoards a
full ’bus, treads on other people’s toes perches on the rail
and calmly blows cigarette-smoke in the face of the seat
occupants. Then poor hussy would not have to endanger
people's lives by franl ically endeavoring to get aforesaid
hoodlum “ off ” or inside as soon as an M.T.C. heaves in
sight.
A Hinglish paper cells the usual little
story of how a certain lord was walking along a
country road towards the house of the local squire
when a poor, ignorant baker, mistaking him for
an ordinary mortal, offered to give him a seat
among the crusty loaves, which his lordship
graciously accepted. The lord in this pirticular
case is Brassey, and the baker is said to have mis-
taken him for a valet, but the average valet is so
much younger and tonier than Brassey that Thk
Bulletin was already regarding the story with
grave suspicion, when it went on to state that the
vice-regal personage gave the baker a shilling.
At this point The Bulletin’s credulity utterly
collapsed. Besides, Brassey would have fallen off
the cart. That eminent statesman falls off every-
thing, and the man who doesn't fall off isn’t
Brassey, however violently he may pretend to be.
The house-shifting industry in Melb. has
increased hugely of late, and nowadays it’s quite
common for an unoccupied dwelling to be dis-
mantled in a few hours, everything of any value
being removed. But last week some enterprising
individuals began operations on an inhabited
dwelling. In the course of the evening the family
heard noises on the roof, which they put down to
the pleasantrios of the local toms and tabbies. It
was only in the morning, after the rain had been
pouring through the roof all night, that a damp
and dilapidated family discovered that the noise
aforesaid had been caused by thieves removing all
the lead from the roof-gutters. This was in Carl-
ton, of course. By-and-by some respectable
citizen will go to town in the morning leaving
behind his wife and family and the house and the
family dog, and during his absence his wife will
become a missing woman and his children get. lost,
and when he returns at night he will be staggered
to find his house Vanished and only the family dog
howling lonely in the midst of a vacant allotment.
But notice how the Carlton J.’sP. will flatten out
these destroyers of property—if they get them.
“I throw up this brief! ” cried the counsel;
“My client is hardened in crime,
For I find, to my just indignation,
The fellow’s been caught every time.
“ But here is a case for y r our mercy—
I pray you, forgive and forget;
For, save on this luckless occasion,
Defendant was never caught yet! ”

Telltale sentence from the N. Territory
account of the murder by blacks of Moore and
MacKenzie, the buffalo-hunters :—“ Moore (head
of the party) was a curious character. . . . Wher-
ever he went he had a quantity of blacks attending
him.” Moore was a well-educated man, daring
and reckless, a son of Dr. Moore, of Melbourne.
But the murder seems to have been just simply
butchery, inspired by desire for loot and the
belief that the third member of the party, Tuck,
had been killed further out.
According to a legend of the Prussian
royal family, there will oorae a period when three
Hohenzollerns will reign in a single year ; also the
third of them will have seven sons and will ruin
his family and his empire. Three Hohenzollerns
(William 1., Frederick, and the present William
the Sudden) all reigned in one year (1888). Also,
William the Sudden has six sons already, and
may have a seventh, and he looks eminently fitted
to wreck something.
Sydney D.T. lately declared that Bun
Tuck Seddon didn’t exclude the Austrians from
Maoriland because ho had any fear that they
would become a burden on the State, but because
they were intended to deprive the present gum-
diggers of their “monopoly of a lucrative em-
ployment.” And the Tki.kgraph might have
added “therebyrenderingthe presentgum-diggers,
or some of them, a burden on the State,” and the
State very properly objected to its own people
being pauperised by cheap aliens. But gum-
digging a lucracive employment! !! The sublime
donkeyness of it! Had Dante known of those
dreary mud and scrub flats where Maoriland’s
miscellaneous failures, and hard cases, and people
with no mozzle worth speaking of, toil unceasingly
to find sufficient gum to give the storekeeper
enougli on account to keep credit going, he would
probably have made his worst sinners kauri gum-
diggers.

Prevention of Seasickness. —The extra-
ordinary nature of the now remedy is the subject
of a letter from Mr. G. Cunningham, of Auckland
(N.Z.). He writes :“ I was very pleased with the
result of the ‘ Seajoi ’ I purchased from you.
I have done a lot of travelling, but this trip
is the first I have enjoyed; on all former occa-
sions I have been a martyr to seasickness.”
“Seajoi ” is sold by Albert Sharp, 257 George-st.,
nearly opposite Bridge-st., Sydney.*
