SPORTING NOTIONS.
One of the most excitable Australian metalli-
eiaua is—well, call him Aaronhofenberger—whose big hat
is os well-known as Zox'a white vest. HU peculiar
characteristic U to take hU beatings howling, and baokers
exist who swear that they have seen him absolutely weep
salt tears on a favorite getting home. At the time of the
big bank smash he was a viotim, and when be heard that
his bank had suspended he rolled off the sofa he was
sitting upon and fell upon the club floor with a wild
shriek. He is also afflicted with terrible anguUh when-
ever he is struck for a double, and the occasion when a
man battled hard and succeeded in inducing him to lay a
big price about two outsiders who eventually got home,
makes him miserable to recall even unto the present day.
Regarding the races as a foregone conclusion for the
favorites, he laid an extravagant double and afterwards
failed in his attempt to get a bit of it off. Consequently
he had to stand by and see the nags come in, and so
chagrined was he at the action of the backer that he paid
the whole wager—£loo—in threepenny bits. But even
this solace failed to bring balin to his heart, and to the
present day anybody who refers to thU particular double
will see Aaronhofenberger haul off hU big hat, mop his
brow, and go away to a corner to pine.
• • • •
Wren, the Melbourne tote-man who so elabor-
ately and successfully safeguarded hU establishment
against the police, seems to be a bit of an all-round
phenomenon. Teetotaller, non-smoker, good cricketer,
tiger at football, no slouch of a pug, runner of two boot-
factories and several pubs, uniformly lucky speculator,
and prospective millionaire. Related of him that when,
as a lad of 15, he lost £B5 in one day, “ backing," be stuck
to it until, before the week was out, he had netted £2OO.
• • • •
The tote ia essentially a Democratic institution.
It doesn’t give the big inner-circle backer an advantage
over the small outside punter. One-man-one-price.
Under the bookmaking system, early information from _
the stable ranks as Proputty, and secures longer odds
than the general public obtains.
• • • •
N.S.W. “sports” need go no further than
Sydney for the strongest argument in favor of the tote.
An illegal tote, paying placed horses, is run by a Sydney
“ shop " on all races—pony and horse—and in 19 out of
every 20 events the aiv. paid by said machine on the
winner amounts to longer odds than those laid by the
“books.” When a man can pay first three, and then
knock out big profits, what must be the profits of the
individual who lays shorter odds and pays first only 1
* • • •
Not generally known that Rand wick paddock
has seen an official tote worked. It is 20 odd years ago,
but, for all that, the late Mr. Franck ran a machine there
successfully for two meetings, It paid decent dividends,
too. An outsider named Poverty paid £5O lor every £1
invested.
<• • • •-
“ Blister ” : What chance has the casual backer
of receiving,.if Ikey Mo should he suddenly struck dead,
say, by excitement on seeing a hot favorite lose by a nose T
There is nothing to make his heirs, executors, or assigns
pay-up, whereas the living bag-man must: either pay-up
or shuf-up.- Again, s’pose a bet is booked by a punter
and hr dies, and his horse wins, will lkey inform the rela-
tions and pay-over ? I should smile 1 The tote does no
booking, doesn’t repudiate, and can’t die from apoplexy.
• • •
“Jack Shay”: The only basis I can imagine
for the idea (8.. 20 11 ’9B) that a horse with three white
legs can’t win a race is that a white foot or stocking on a,
horse is always accompanied by a white hoof. That is to
say, the hoof is clear, almost like white horn instead of
the usual slatey-grey of horses’ hoofs. Now, white hoofs
are tenderer than grey ones—softer and more liable to
ailment, therefore it is harder to keep a white-legged
horse on his feet while training hard.
••• • j
At a Sydney pony-meet, a while back, owner
X. discovered that his newly-engaged jock was related to
two bookies. Concluding he would be forestalled if the
boy knew he was to try, owner X. fell back on the pony’s
peculiarities. ThenobleVx-ast.it seems, only goes the faster
when a pull is taken and stops like a clock when the flail
falls; consequently the boy was told be was not to *’ take
any,” and to “catch hold" at the distance. As was
anticipated, the apparently “deadening” orders were
reported to the books, which was handy for owner X.,
whose commissioner gathered up £2BO to £4O. Boy
caught hold, pony went all the harder, boy pulled ditto,
but pony knew its business, and beat the boy badly and
the rest of the field by two lengths. Next day, one of the
bookies remarked sympathetically to owner X., “ "Fraid
you did no good with yours yesterday. Damned noosance
when they get 'orae on you dead, ain't it? Conianava-
drink.” And then, when the whisky had trickled thorax-
wards, and owner X. gently remarked, “ Oh, it wasn’t
so bad ; 7‘s is a fair average to get on £4O at. .1 think
you accommodated us with £BO to £lO, didn't you?”
there was a gasp that nearly blew out the electric lights in
the Marble Bar.
• * * *
One of the smartest examples of “ ringing-in ” a
trotter occurred years ago over a horse which died not
long since near Sydney. The moke was landed from the
’Frisco boat heavily clothed and bandaged and takeri up
town. Next morning he was trained to Richmond to
await the arrival of a drover who was coming to Sydney
with a draft of cattle. At Richmond the drover picked
him up and rode him behind the cattle to Homebush. On
the day his cattle were sold the drover boasted much
about the horse he had picked up at Pilliga, a natural
pacer, the end of it being that his owner made a bogus
purchase before a crowd of sporting butchers. The good
thing did not come off, however, as the horse never
showed his true form in Australia, but, strange to say,
the story of his ringing-in didn't get abroad for a long
while.
* . * •
“Poker Ned”: It has been quite common in
M. L. for years past to back a horse in a race and have a
contingency bet on a race preceding it, the object of the
backer in most cases being to try and save his stake. For
instance, a Derby being the 3rd or 4th race on the card,
the backer would put, say, £5 on the favorite, the win-
nings, or a stated amount, to be put on his fancy in a
race run prior to the Derby. These bets were mostly
made on meetings at a distance, where the results would
likely all he made known together; and so long as the
contract was made before either race was run it didn’t
appear to make much difference whether the contingency
was on a sooner or a later race. Such Bets have often
been made on Australian events., Of course, in cases
like* the “Scorn-Bobadil" wager, where the full stake
had to he put on, there could be no reason for making
the first Investment on the second race, but the principle
is the same. However, it was cases of this kind and the
fact of backers sometimes wanting a second contingency
and, so to speak, jumping all over the programme, that
was the origin of the rule, “Contingent bets cannot be
made on races to be run prior to the race on which the
first investment is made.” (

“A.E.G.”: There is a racehorse in Illawarra
district with a moustache as splendid as that of Jawbone
Neild’s. Owner says that, in a long experience of horses,
he has only seen one other thus embellished.
During a recent Vic. road cycle-race two men
came down, one smashing his face, the other his mount;
whereupon the latter seized his fallen rival’s machine
and bowled ahead, securing a place. Afterwards he ex-
plained to his admiring backers that he “ seen the other
bloke was dead to the world.” How dead he didn’t stop
to enquire.
* • * •
Luck. At Nelson (M.L.), not long since, a
Cyclist fell over a hen which bolted across the road in
front of his machine. Another cyclist behind came down
heavily on top of him. A trap in the rear drove over
both of them. Horse injured ; both bikes wrecked ;
riders scared out of seven years' growth ; hen, quite un-
injured, peacefully digesting worm at roadside. •
» * * *
A Melbourne cyclist who had a spill on the St.
Kilda tram-track, the other day, arose with a valuable
opal pin stuck in his cheek. He swears the pin was not
his—that it must have been lying on the track.
The knpe-long bicyclerskirt affected by Ameri-
can damsels of all classes is now prohibited in N.Y. State
unless the wearer i» accompanied by her machine or can
prove that she left her home with it. This, at first sight,
seems an extraordinary rule, but It is evidently directed
at a certain class of women who would never miss the
chance of doing the pavement in ballet-skirts. When
all s said and done It was Tottie far more than male oppo-
sition who killed the bloomer in Melb. As soon as a few
women bifurcated themselves on the bike Lonsdale-street
did likewise, and its fatted calf brightened Beaumaris
every Sunday, while bloomered beauties, drinking beer
from bottles, were common objects of every suburban
road.

Conundrum set for the sporting editor of a
Gippsland bi-weekly : “ Hear sir,—ln the cricket-match,
South Star v. Ramblers, the ball, batted by H. Smith,
struck a stout man 70 yards off in the stomach and re-
bounded into the hands of cover-field. Was Smith out?”
What about the stout man ? Was he out?
• * • *
One of the most successful Australian profes-
sional tipsters follows a system which, to an extent,
answers the question why tipsters do not back their own
fancies and make a pile. The prophet in question makes
it a practice to back his own good thing consistently. He
attends evety race-meeting of any size around Melb., and
as soon as he has scored a win on one race, the whole of
his winnings are popped on to the dead-bird for next
event. By following this system to its logical conclusion,
the tipster argues, one day, when he strikes a sequence
of six winners, his pile will be made, and he will need to
tip no more ; but, though he has been at the game for
10 years, and with eonsiclerable success, he has never been
able to get beyond four consecutives. Racing seems to
be like music in one respect—there are forbidden con-
secutives. Also, his system is a fool of a system, in that
after he had doubled and won three or four times he
could not possibly obtain wagers of the amount needed
to bring off the long-waited-for coup.

Rockhampton has a one-handed athlete of all-
round excellence in C.B. St. John. He is a representative
cricketer and footballer. Is a consistent scorer, a brilliant
field, and bowls fairly. Plays three-quarter at football,
runs fast, and fends strongly with the maimed arm.
* * * »
Sydney footballers are suggesting that because
Melbourne club contests have ceased to excite the multi-
tude as of yore, there is now a chance to popularise the
Rugby game down South. The wish is father to the
thought, and the thought is absurd. Newchumsin Vic-
toria are pretty unanimous in admitting that the local
game provides a brighter show and is better adapted to
the typical Australian physique than the Rugby game, in
which “ Bull’s ” beef gives him an advantage.
* « * *
At the annual Newmarket (Eng.) blood-stock
sales, the highest price paid for a colt was 1200 guineas.
The mare Simoon, St. Slmon-Shotover, covered by
“ Best Man,” fetched 3000 guineas. Four mares covered
by Carnage realised 210, 500, 300, and 490 guineas, while
a more fashionably bred mare, covered by Ladas, only
brought 450, and a mare by Bend Or, covered by Carbine,
onlv 200 guineas.
* * * *
Call the farmer smart who wanted a 40-acre
paddock ploughed and so promoted a ploughing match,
offering prizes of £6, £2 and £1? (queries a BuliiKTIS
correspondent). The pub-keeper in our township would,
if he were sober enough to read the par., call him a fool
This is what he does. He has half-a-dozen barrels of beer,
and ten cases spirits in—at least, he says they come in
cases. So, wanting them drunk, he promotes a monster
sports gathering ; once it was races, but that wasn’t com-
prehensive enough. Give prizes? Not he! Canvasses
the township for them ; sends to all the merchants doing
business with everybody in the township. Gets them,
too. If there’s any liquor over, it’s consumed at the
‘‘ settling.” And what our pub.-man does the pub.-men
in scores of other townships, and even in places where
there aren’t any townships, have been doing for years
past.

A SONG OF THE "CYCLE."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To the buzzing- of the bearings,
To the whirring of the wheels,
To our lawless down-hill tearings
With the street dog at our heels;
To the low, long, level places
Where we gaily get along,
To the cool air in our faces,
Do I sing this song.
To the long, lean, raking racer,
To the man who turns not back,
To the pace that is a pacer,
To the track that is a track,
To the noiseless even gliding,
The exhilarating time,
To the scorcher’s joys abiding
Do I sling this rhyme.
Some men glory in the beating
Of the horse-hoofs on the turf—
Some men revel in a meeting
With the rough-and-tumble surf )
Give me two steeds of metal—
And a mate of mettle, too—
And a point of speed to settle
As we rush things through.
When death at last shall take us
And we’re scattered to the wind,
Surely “ Peter ” will not make us
Leave our bicycles behind.
On the sand shore of the Jasper
(Where to “ coast ” is not a crime)
We will make the pace a rasper
Till the end of time.
Queensland.
O. COWLEY.
"BIDING WORK."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The coldest game I know of is on the training-track
On a foggy winter’s morning, rUiing work;
When the cold nigh nips your head off, and an over-
coat you lack
(They’re too heavy to be worn at riding work),
While the fog's so thick and heavy that you hardly
see the “ moke,"
And it settles in your throat and makes you cough
and almost choke;
And the only way to ease it is to have a puff of
smoke—
When you go out with the horses, riding work!
You strip the horse's rugs off, and the girths and all
make tight
(For it'? awkward if they get loose, riding work),
And you get a leg-up, and the boss sings out “All
right! ’’
(For he’s always out to see the horses work);

When the Stampers Start.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Said a somewhat cynical mining man,
“ You may take it as truth from me
When a mining-town goes suddenly down
The reason is this, d’ye see :
“ They peg their claims ; they whose trade it is,
And making a deal of fuss,
‘The district is rich,’ is the yarn they pitch
In the company prospeet-us.
‘ vVe sent a parcel to Freiburg town,
A sample from every part;
It’s payable ore and the divs. are sure—
When the crushing-stampers start.’
“So a boom begins and they float their mine,
Or several, all in a batch ;
But never they tell, when tie y want to sell,
The gold is in one little patch.
So the boom goes on, month after month
(Delay is promoter’s art),
And shareholders wait their investments’ fate
Till the thudding stampers start.
“ But sad is the story of wasted work
By the turning cam-shafts told.
And the lifting cams grind a chorus of woe
To the shoes’ blank verse * No gold.’
Then down drop the shares on the market list
As fast as the falling dart.
The mines are shut down and down goes the town
When the dropping stampers start.
“These are the facts,” said the cynical man,
“ And are proven in every part.
For we learn the truth with but little ruth
As soon as the stampers start.”
PROSPECT GOOD.
A grave-digger at Grenfell (N.S.W.)
dropped dead in the grave he was making. His
epitaph :
Two neighboring farmers at Borung (Vic.)
planted wheat early last spring. Soon after the
grain sprouted one “ cockie ” flooded his crop.
During the dry weather in October it bore up
splendidly, while the un-irrigated crop wilted
away and had to be cut for hay. The final result
to the irrigationist was 24 bushels to the acre !
Same soil, same seed, same climate, same cultiva-
tion ; water the only difference. Let us irrigate.
“ Sub.” : Travelling in the Australian Alps
district recently, I inquired ofß., who was always
talking California-in-the-fifties, whether he had
known a former employe of mine who had left me
to go to California. “Know him, sir! Why, I
seen him hung l ” Intimate acquaintance with a
vengeance; but, as B. explained, they had only
two punishments in those days—flogging and
hanging. “If they weren’t busy they flogged
’em, but if there was a rush of work on they
strung ’em up. It saved time.”
He had an order for a grave
(So cynic Fate had willed it),
And like a honest man and brave
He digged the grave—and filled it!


The firm of J. C. Ludowici and Son, Ltd.,
with branches at Brisbane, Perth, Adelaide, and
Hobart, is steadily though surely getting all Aus-
tralia to know that the firm’s leather and leather-
belting is unsurpassable for wear. The firm has
just executed one of the largest orders ever given
for leather belting for the Westralian gold mines.
It has also supplied several of the largest belts in
Australia to the electric-tram service of Sydney ;
while it has recently sent to Queensland the
largest belt now running in the Southern Hemi-
sphere.*

Captain Phillip, Australia’s
first Governor, reserved the whole
of what is known as Sydney
Domain for the use and recrea-

The committees of the met-
ropalitan hospitals will soon move
effectively in the direction of
forcing well-to-do people to pay
for dependents whom hitherto
they have foisted, when ailing,
on public charity. The course
under consideration is to impera-
tively direct the secretary to bill
every such person on the death
or cure of the patient, and to
carry the account, if repudiated,
into court. A little bit of polite
blackmail.
An obvious moral attaches to the recent
Sydney case, in which a man mortally wounded, at
her employer’s house, a nursery-governess—whose
acquaintance he originally made through her
Daily Telegraph matrimonial advertisement.
He believed her to be playing fast-and-loose
with him, after he had given her a ring and pro-
vided her with money. The slayer is Hibernian
and carrotty, and the moral is that it is a parlous
thing to trifle with the affections (if a red Kelly ;
though perhaps, after all, it is more apparent
upon the evidence that the swain’s severest pangs
were in his pocket. He appears, at all events,
to have been dunning the faithless one,
not for affection alienated, but for cold cash
advanced. A further squalid flavor is im-
parted to the affair by Kelly’s espionage upon
the lady in the matter of a certain surreptitious
visit to a public-house in quest of long-beers. The
circumstance, too, that after “getting outside’
sundry such quenchers the 38-year-old Miss Flynn
further got outside a ’bus has a bearing upon the
current question of the susceptible ’bus-driver’s
liability, under the fascinations of the fair, to let
hisgee-gees wander from thepathsofsafety. Kelly’s
assumption, also, that “ the man in the tall hat
whom ho caught talking to the woman in a dark
side-street was “not her social equal,” tends to
show that the divinity which has so far hedged
that badge of stodgy respectability is on the
down-grade at last. Kelly, after killing the
woman, shot himself twice, but failed to a
mortal wound, and is in police custody at St.
Vincent’s Hospital. He will, in a few days, have
sufficiently recovered to appear at the inquest.

Good attendance and fields at Rosehill, Sydney,
on Saturday. There were a couple of reversals of form
on Hand wick running that fairly subdued the crowd. As
soon as betting opened ou the Nursery Handicap, Par*
thian, on his great showing at ltandwick, was fairly
rushed by punters, 2to 1 being laid on the colt Felicity
jumped away, Parthian getting oil slowly. Coming into
the straight. Felicity was lengths in front, and stayed
there to the finish, Mat. Harris not pressing Parthian
when he thought the case hopeless. Hopscotch was the
other surprise. At Randwick, in the Challenge Stakes,
the grind chestnut ran last, but on Saturday, in the Fly-
ing Handicap, he downed the favorite. Platinum, in the
last few strides, the general opinion being that he could
have taken the I tail at any portion of the race. Since his
victory, Hopscotch has been nibbled at for Newmarket
Handicap, but as the stable also holds the favorite,
Beachcomber, it will take punters all their time to pick
the right pea.
A serious accident in a flat race is becoming
almost a weekly occurrence on Sydney courses. No one,
not even the jocks themselves, is ever able to exactly
set forth the cause, hut a combination which has much
to do with it is—big fields, weedy horses, and weedy and
incompetent jockeys. A mosquito-like six-furlong animal
starts in the big field, in a mile-and-a-half handicap, with
a wasted mannikin up, and, jumping away, leads for half-
a-mile, when it begins to “crack." The half-starved rider
hasn t the strength to keep his “done” mount straight,
and, getting back into the ruck, the beaten “ weed ” either
crosses its legs or gets in the way of something else, and
down go half the field. The public gasps, looks anxiously
to see that the favorite hasn’t fallen, and rushes to the
fence to watch the white-faced jockeys being carried in ;
then it forgets all about the occurrence in the excitement
of picking the winner of the next.
The racing rulers eau’t undertake to do away
with weeds, nor can they run races on the invitation
system and allow only the best horses to start, because
the nags that are evidently the !>est generally succeed in
getting badly licked by what are considered the worst;
but they can do much to minimise accidents by a few
simple rules. Limit fields to, say, 15 starters; draw up
strict regulations re the apprenticeship of a jockey before
he is allowed to ride in a race; and raise the minimum
weight to do away with all excuse for wasting, and flat-
race accidents will be few and far between.

The Summer Cup winner, Strathroy, put up a
big performance in Rosehill Feb. Handicap, won by Ailie,
by carrying the thumping weight of 10.2 into second
place, lieating such horses as Eastern King, Toxleth,
Boscobel and others. Strathroy once was thought unre-
liable, but he has developed into a very consistent race-
horse, and is, bar Merloolas, perhaps the best handicap
horse in N.S. \V.
• • • •
Merloolas, by the way, is in the market for sale,
and although a high price is asked for the large-hearted
bay he should prove a good investment for anyone who
will treat him well and supply him with a good horseman
in his races. Merloolas is an exceptional horse who has
been badly mismanaged ; anyone who has seen him finish
under the whip for the last half-mile of a gruelling, two-
mile handicap, and answer challenge after challenge
without a sign of faltering, knows that there isn't a gamer
or truer horse living—and weight makes little diflereuce
to such an animal.
• • • •
Three of the late Richard Craven’s horses,
Woodlark, Gauleon and Oceana, have been leased by
Humphrey Oxenhatn, who should get some good races out
of them. Woodlark gave promise of being something
out of the common, and Gauleon, although he has never
jet won, is a full brother to The Grafter, Gaulus and
Aleuiene, so that he is bred to gallop. Gauleon would
pass almost anywhere for The Grafter, being of the same
gaunt build and possessing the same prominent nose.

Another mannikin sacrificed through the evil of
big fields. And so it will continue with gruesome mono-
tony until the ruling powers of Vic., N.S.W., and S.A.

• » * •
In pre-telephone days, betting was often carried
on in Sydney after the race was actually run. On one
occasion sundry sports were awaiting, in a billiard-
room, result of a Hawkesbury Handicap. They knew
the race must be over, when the marker, entering into the
conversation, asked the price of a certain outsider. A
big bookie answered, “50 to 1." “And the favorite?”
“4 to 1.” The marker offered to take 40 to 6 the favorite,
if the book threw 50 to 1 the outsider in ; and the bookie
closed with the offer. In about ten minutes the results
came to hand—the outsider the winner! The bookie
“ tumbled,” but paid, and eventually the marker gave
awav the “have.” Had a confederate at post-office to
get a wire. Confederate did not enter the room, hut
went round to the back and wrote winner’s name on a
wall where the marker could see it out of a window.
Bookie, knowing the marker had not left the room and
that nobody had come in with the tip, thought he was
taking a fair chance.

The Council of Churches in S. A. has decided to
make the abolition of the Totalisator a test question at
the coming- general elections. The Anglicans, however,
are neutral on the question, while It.C. Archbishop
O’Reilly favors the instrument, and has asserted that he
sees no harm in gambling so long as the speculators keep
within their means and pay up when they lose.

M’Kibbin, playing for Glebe against E. Sydney, did not
improve his chances of a trip ’Ome, for, although E.
Sydney only got 163, Mac. couldn’t capture more than

In the Waverley-Paddington fixture, Gregory
performed his usual running-out-his-partner trick. Noble
was in great form—caught and bowled Iredale, Bell and
Gregory, scooping in 8 wickets for 61 runs.
• * • •
Gregory and Kelly had a misunderstanding
when tossing in the Paddington-Waverley match. Kelly
threw the coin, Gregory calling “ Woman,” and upon tail
coming up Kelly claimed to have won, saying that
“ woman ” meant head. Which was, of course, absurd,
as in tossing “ woman ” invariably stands for the reverse
to “ head,” be the head that of a king or a queen.

N.S.W. Cricketing Ass’n.’s decision that five
colts shall be picked in the team against M.L. is wise, as
there are several rising young ’uns little, if any, behind
the ruck of the big guns. In picking them, however, it
is to be hoped that real colts will be chosen, and not old,
spavined, aged ’uns that have been running for a life-
time. Marsh, the fast South Sydney bowler, has pace
little behind Jones’, and should be tried ; and then there
are the “other" Duff, Fartpihar, young Gregory, Hop-
kins, Poidevin, and Karl Quist—all good men and bona
tide colts.
• • * ■
It is generally accepted in cricketing circles that
next all-Australian team will consist of 13—“ the Dei il’s
number ” —the selection committee, it is said, having
decided to take no more l»ecause 14 would mean ad-
ditional expense and a smaller “ div.” for each man, to
which the nine “certs” all object. This again draws
attention to the rottenness of allowing players to have
anything to do with the selection of the team. Also it
raises the question as to who has the authority to decide
the number of players to go—the selection committee or
the Aus. Cricket Council ? If the team is merely a
money-making concern it doesn’t matter how few go,
but if, a« is understood, it is being sent 'Ome to do its
best for Australian cricket, then the cricket authorities
should see that the 14 best men go. Several previous
teams have found 13 insufficient, and have been compelled
to borrow men in England.
• • • •
An open secret that Iredale and Howell are the
two men selected to complete the eleven. The extras
will not be picked until after the test matches.
• • • •
Some people behind the scenes prophesy that
the Australian team, about to visit England, will quarrel
even more than previous Elevens. Apparently Joe Dar-
ling will be captain, an initial mistake, for Darling has
been a failure as captain of S. A. this year. When Harry
Trott fell ill Australia suffered a big blow, for he was the
only man of any capacity in the position since Murdoch.
The man for captain would be Jim Kelly, but Jim can’t
make a speech, a fatal failing in these banqueting days.
• • • •
London papers (E. News in particular) are
already threatening that Jones will be disqualified in Eng-
land. Apparently some of the English writers think Jones
should have been left out of the Australian team because
Phillips “called” him last year in one match only.
M’Kibbln’s delivery, it seems, will also be questioned.
The Australians will be in a nice fix if two of their lead-
ing bowlers are no-balled. And it is certain that if Jones
throws so does M’Kibbin.
One of the certainties for the Australian XL
has developed a complaint which utterly defies the best
medical skill. The leading symptom of his malady is
terrible swelling of the head.

“M.”: On Anniversary Day writer went to
Sydney Cricket Ground new stand for lunch. Nothing
but scones and tea. Waited quarter-hour for rush to
abate, then went to ladies' pavilion, and sat there lOinin.
unattended to. Next tried members’ room, where were
plenty of waiters rushing round with empty dishes ; but
none of ’em came my way, so returned to new stand, and
made shift with scones, and tea in a cup of wine-glass
size. At ladies’ pavilion, where cold lunch is served at
Is. fid. a head, the board outside had the shilling dis-
played boldly and the sixpence carefully obscured by a
blot of blue ink, after the manner of small hucksters.
This, however, was altered before the conclusion of the
match.
• » * «
Bathurst guaranteed the expenses of the two-
days' match against Victoria. Worrall rewarded this by
asking the locals to pay Is. on the second day to see
Graham bowl. Country teams are generally treated like
this. No wonder they fight shy of entertaining crack
players.
« * • •
Giller’s cricket-life to date has been brief, if
happy. A few years ago he was an unknown junior, and
joined S. Meli). (captained by H. Trott) for practice
merely, but Trott and one or two more of the S. Melb.
cognoscenti at once picked him out for the first eleven.
Time after time he has saved his side when in a hole, arid
to-day, since the retirement of Trott, he captains his club.
Is most unassuming, lives a temperate life, has a cool
head, and a stout heart at a pinch.
* * * *
London National Sporting Club has resolved,
with a view to preventing the recurrence of fatal contests,
that before signing articles for a mill each boxer is to
furnish the manager with a certificate of health ; during
training and on the day of the contest he is to be ex-
amined by the club doctor ; the rounds are to be reduced
to two minutes each, with a minute’s rest; and the gloves
are to weigh fioz. Of these new regulations the first three
are designed to safeguard the sport against cases of heart-
failure, and they will do this effectually as long as they
are carried out. The increased bulk of the gloves will
not be liked, and the new rule may lead to the making of
other fioz. articles, harder and smaller—which would
defeat the committee's purpose. But the evident inten-
tion is to diminish the chance of administering knockout
blows, and to do so without a specific regulation exclud-
ing them.

The lone, red-haired N.S.W. middle-weight,
Jim Richardson, went under in seven rounds to Maori-
land Heffernan in England last week. Ileffernan has
been abroad for some years. Went hence to S. Africa,
earned fame there as a boxer, for he is a plucked ’un from
the ground up, invested his savings in an itinerant
saveioy-and-tater plant, and did roaring biz in Johannes-
burg streets ; sold out, went to England and America, but
did little good in the squared circle till last week's luck
came his way. Writer says "luck’’ because the cable
reads that way. For when both Richardson and Heffer-
nan were in Sydney, one Partridge defeated the Maori-
lander in a round-and-a-half; then Richardson came
along, and, after a fierce battle, bested Heffeman’s victor.
• • • •
Golden Gate (Sydney) Athletic Club started
another novice tournament on Saturday night; 600 or 700
people turned up to view the lively proceedings. Science
was thrown to the winds, and thumps rained thick and
fast, while arms flew about like flails—neither side in any
bout escaping unscathed—till one or other cried ago
through sheer exhaustion.
* • * *
Champion pug. Fitzsimmons, when asked re-
cently whether he would meet Sailor Sharkey if the latter
beat M'Coy, replied that he could not say, as Mrs. Bob
had not yet released him from his promise never to “ put
'em up ” seriously again. As a lover-like husband who
abandons the ring because of affection for his wife and
the fact that his further connection with “flghtin’”
would cause her great mental pain, lanky Robert stands
before the world in a much better light than many of his
predecessors; let us hope he will “ live up to his blue
china.” Pugs., as a rule, don’t make good husbands.
Those well up the ladder are usually vainerthan peacocks,
and in the days of their prosperity are the subjects of
much adulation from women who would not notice them
but for their athletic celebrity. Legion are the marital
infelicities of prominent pugilists. Tom Sayers was
“ up” at least once for wife-beating. “ Yankee” Sullivan
threw a lamp at. Mrs. Y., which burned her so severely
that she died. Joe Goss, when in Eng., resented the
shrewish tongue of Mrs. G., setting her down upon a hot
stove till she was badly broiled. Mike M’Coole, the deck-
hand champion of America, always treated his spouse
brutally ; while Mrs. Heenan, wife of the " Benicia Boy,”
made no secret of the fact that her husband had wronged
her in a terrible way. John L. Sullivan’s wife got a
divorce from him. Corbett and the first Mrs. C. parted
company, as also did Fitzsimmons and his Australian wife.

Strong-man Sampson and Prof. Miller are not
going to meet after all—at least not just yet; Miller has
taken his money down because the coin-breaker, instead
of keeping certain appointments, went on tour through
Vic. country towns with a co. of his own organising.
Wrestler Dunn has, however, offered to throw Sampson
20 times in an hour for a big side-wager. If he had the
slightest show of beating Miller in the “ Rastle ” part of
their proposed match, this offer of Dunn's should well
suit the man of muscle, but present scribe doesn’t think
Mr. Sampson is “ taking any.”

With the exception of that at 100yds., swimmer
Lane has this season taken every championship so far—
utterly outdistancing his fellow competitors. The mile,
half-mile, 300yds. and 220yds. premierships already belong
to him, while the quarter-mile—the last of the year —to
be contested to-day (Sat.) looks a moral for the brilliant
vrangster. At the Palace Emporium club’s gala, on last
Fat., he secured absolute possession of the President’s
Challenge Cup, having won the event two years in sue-

regatta was “ M'Cluken.” “George "also rowed in the

Sydney larrikins have found a new way of
getting the cyclist’s heart into his mouth. They wait
half-way down a hill until a scorcher comes, and as he
rushesbv thev dash a ‘•throw-down ” cracker just behind
the wheel. Bikey eases up, thinking his tyre has gone
bung.
• • • •
Two English trout-experts took a trip to the
Great Lake (Tus.) recently, and on one day became
separated at the start. Towards evening the junior part-
ner set out round the lake to look for the old Tin, whom,
after long search, he discovered stretched out on the
bank in a gasping state. To all enquiries the ancient
merely pointed to his tongue, which hung out of his
mouth like a dried fig. The other moistened it
with a few drops of grog, which immediately revived the
owner. “ Mav deah fellow,” he said, “ I fawgot my
flawsk, and heah I have been all day long perwishing for
a drink of watah.” “ Why, you old idiot,” said his mate,
“you’ve been standing in good fresh drinking-water the
whole day ! ” The old gent thought, by the sound of the
sad sea waves, that he was fishing in mid ocean.

A medico in practice at Putney —the place where
the Thames rowing matches start from—wrote an in-
teresting letter to London Rbfkrek on the subject of
American, Australian and English training systems.
Sppaking from 16 years’ knowledge of professional oars-
men, he says that the Yanks and Australians are more
careful in their habits, and more scientific, lly teachable
than any 'Ome men, who train on certain I ard-and-fast
rules, and have (as another rule) a lot of intemperance to
make amends for when they are getting fit. As to the
healthfulness of an oarsman’s life, the Putney doctor
declares himself convinced that, it is the best of all athletic
lives. Also, he goes out of his way to curse the bike—
“ Whereas by running only the heart is injured, by
bicycling both the heart and lungs and limbs are
damaged.” All of which remains to be proved, doesn’t
iff Lightning pedalling is still in its infancy. Thus far
no champion cyclist has dropped dead on the track,
whereas James Itenforth, the last of England's old-
fashioned great oarsmen, died in a 4-oared boat in
America. They were preparing to start for an inter-
national race, when Itenforth fell hack fainting into the
arms of ex-champion Kelly. Heart-disease. The tragic
picture is carved upon the base of a monument erected
to his memory.

* * . * *
As usual, during the recent holiday meetings,
riders of the easy-running Massey-Harris were successful
in securing a large proportion of the chief racing events
in the different provinces. The company’s record for the
week shows more than 60 firsts, more than 30 seconds,
and more than 20 thirds, and more than £3OO prize money
won on its bicycles. The wins include six International
Scratch races, three Championships, the four principal
N.S.W. League Handicaps, the E.M.B.C. Wheel Race, and
pretty well all the good things at the country meetings.*

Justdead, H. B. Hinton, an unregistered
medico, practising at Sofala, N.S.W., and in
charge of the hospital there, aged 82 years. He
had lived on the Turon for 40 years, and for many
years was the only medico in the neighborhood,
his certificates being accepted by the Registrar-
General unquestioned.
A very old colonist, Thomas Jones, died
at Parramatta last Saturday, aged 94. He arrived
in N.S. W. in 1821, and in ’24 was stationed at
Bathurst as a police constable, where he did good
work in suppressing bushranging. He had a
memory well stored with old-time facts, and was
officially acquainted with the Cato-street con-
spirators, and with Hunt, the accomplice of
Thurtell, murderer of Wearo, the gambler. De-
ceased has left a brother of 88 years in Bathurst
district.

Few things are more genuinely pathetic
than a long-way-back township, too small to run
a doctor even with the help of a Govt.-endowed
hospital, and too poor to call over a doctor from
the nearest big township when an epidemic
breaks out. The blacksmith, or the local con-
stable, or some other long-established worthy,
who generally provides the medical advice of the
place, confesses he is lost; and then, as one in-
habitant after the other sickens and perhaps dies,
the scare is positively affecting. Pooncarie, a
poor, forlorn little township on the Darling,Jhas
for months past been, off and on, in this state of
pitiable fright. Diphtheria has been the enemy ;
it has swept two or three out of a single household;
the doctor has been got down there once or twice;
and a public subscription has been made to buy a
few tubes of anti-toxin, which the local constable
injects on sufficient provocation. Meantime,
nobody who can avoid it drinks water in Poon-
carie; for both the wells and the river have fallen
under suspicion.
It was proposed in Sydney City Council to
send to the retiring veteran, Alderman Kippax,
an illuminated farewell letter—illuminated, of
course, at the expense of the Corporation funds.
W hereupon Boanerges Norton said No—he was
willing, however, to subscribe a guinea out of his
own pocket if the other aldermen did ditto.
Result—no illuminated testimonial for Kippax,
who will get a plain sheet of foolscap instead. What
a hollow sham is the mufciial-admiration*at-public-
expense system ! The Corporation is willing to
grease Kippax with public funds, but its indivi-
dual respect for that distinguished citizen doesn’t
run to the extent of 21s. Yah !
Be Tennyson, whom South Australia has
got on as Governor, the Argus remarks
that ‘ if the new Governor were not his father’s
son he would not be a lord, and if he were not a
lord he would hardly be thought of ” for the pur-
]>ose. Exactly, a young man has no qualifications
whatever, but his pa happens to be a lord, and
therefore a province has to keep him. In this case
it is to be remembered that the elder Tennyson
would not have been a lord if it had not been for
his son. His biography shows that he would not
have taken a title had it not been his son’s wish
that he should.

The Bulletin notices with pleasure the
Melb. announcement (Jan. 27) that “ Miss C. H.
Thomson and Mrs. Evelyn Gough have purchased
the Sun newspaper, and that it is their intention
to make it a high-class and interesting family
journal,” and that they invite original poems.
And just below this The Bulletin notices with
astonishment that the Sun reprints Roderic
Quinn’s poem, “A Western Garden,” from Bul-
letin of Nov. 19 last, without a word to show that
it has been borrowed by said “ high-class and in-
teresting family journal.”
Both Orleans and Prince Napoleon are
probably far too tired to make any determined
bid for French monarchy. If there is any attempt
to overthrow the Republic, it will be stirred by
the mutual jealousies of the claimants —both of
whom, by the way, had allegedly abandoned their
claims—and it is just this rivalry that will most
likely render their kingly aspirations ineffective.
The Dreyfus embrogiio seems to have afforded at
least three psychological moments for a sudden
coup, but one prince spends his time in talking
big nothings to people in Brussels, and the other,
who is actually in Paris, seems so inconsequent
that nobody will take any notice of him.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
5 of this issue.*

A most particular fool is that United
States official who admits that, at the beginning
of the trouble with Spain, his country had only
enough ammunition to furnish two rounds for
each gun, and the quarrel was so long coming to a
head simply because the States couldn’t fight
earlier. A big naval Power, if it had gone into
war with a rush, could evidently have crumpled
up the Yankee navy like a wet rag simply because
it hadn’t enough shooting material for a good
hour’s battle, and then blockaded the coast and
battered down the coastal cities. Other nations
will make a careful note in their hats now that
the U.S., after a long spell of peace, is liable to
be found in some such state as this. Plainly, the
row with Britain over Venezuela was all a bluff ;
a country that hadn’t many guns, and was only
ready to fire two shots from each one that it had,
couldn’t twist the lion’s tail to the bitter end.
And when the next similar row is on, the other
party —if he is a good-sized Power—will probably
remember this and refuse to be bluffed, and give
the (States 12 hours to get off his tail and apologise.

Government Notices.
Public Service Board,
60 Young-street, Sydney,
17th December, 1898.
A COMPETITIVE EXAMINATION of CANDIDATES for
JUNIOR CLERKSHIPS in the PUBLIC SERVICE
of the COLONY will be held in Sydney, and at various
Country centres, which will be fixed as far as possible to
suit the convenience of candidates, on the 20th MARCH
next and four following days. The estimated number of
appointments to be made is 50. Candidates must be not
less than 16 nor more than 21 years of age on the first day
of examination, of good diameter, and in sound health.
Persons already employed in the General Division of the
Public Service will be admitted to the examination, pro-
vided their age does not exceed thirty years. The sub-
jects in which Candidates will be examined are .’—Hand-
writing, English (Dictation, Composition, Precis writing,
Parsing, Analysis, and derivation of words), Arithmetic
(full course), Geometry (Euclid, first Book), Geography
.{outlines of physical and political geography, and the
geography of the Australasian Colonies in moderate
detail), English History from the Middle of the Eighteenth
Century to the present time, especially with reference to
the Colonies and Dependencies. Candidates will also be
allowed to take any two, but not more than two, of the
following subjects: Mathematics. English, a prescribed
Author, viz.: Shakespeare’s “ Richard lll.,”Latin, French,
German, Elementary Chemistry, and Elementary Geology.
Applications to attend the examination must be made
on a form for the purpose, which can be obtained at
the office of the Board, and must reach the under-
signed not later than the Ist March. Candidates
will be required to pay an examination-fee of 10s.,
which must he lodged with a Clerk of Petty Sessions or
the Treasury, and the receipt therefor forwarded to the
Secretary to the Board with the form of application. A
list of registered candidates with centres of examination
will he published in the Govf.rnmf.nt Gazkttk, after the
time for the receipt of applications has expired, and can-
didates will also be advised direct of the place at which
they will be examined. Candidates may be examined at
any centre (outside the Sydney radius) where persons
approved by the Beard of Examiners are willing to super-
intend the examination, and a form containing the signa-
tures of such persons should be forwarded to the Secretary
of the Board of Examiners not later than Ist March, 1899.
Forms for the purpose may be obtained from the Secre-
tary of the Board of Examiners, 7, Richmond-terrace,
Domain, Sydney. The requisite stationery will be sup-
plied by the Public Service Board. The Regulations and
copies of the papers set at the last examination can be
obtained at the Government Printing Office and the prin-
cipal booksellers.
By order of the Board.
9a 60 .J. W. HOLLIMAN, Secretary.

“ Bargo ” : A six-year-old Hebrew young-
ster (father a tailor) has just been telling me that
he is in a c lass with youngsters “ three sizes ”
bigger than himself. Heredity or environment ?
Slab of Sec. 40 N.S. W. Criminal Law
Amendment Act:
And whosoever has carnal knowledge of a woman with
her consent induced by the language or conduct of the
accused that he was her husband shall be liable to penal
servitude for 14 years.
Under this section the only way in which a hus-
band can cohabit with his wife without endanger-
ing his liberty is to represent to his wife, either
by language or gesture, that he is not her hus-
band.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

little all this had diminished »Tane’s vigor was evi-

“ Comp.” : The course n£ the journeyman
comp, never did run smooth. The Linotvpe has
admittedly done great havoc with type-snatchers,
but, even before that contrivance was introduced
into Australia, the out-of-work printer had griev-
ance to contend with. I once called at a country
(N.S.W.) office, looking for work, and, in the coni-
posing-room, saw a Boy and girl (aged about 13
and 11 respectively) setting type. Conversing
with the boss—a very tired-looking person—lie
told me he couldn’t “give me a start,’’ things
being so bad that he had of late been seriously
thinking of reducing his present staff !

PUYYARA.


Schoolmaster: “I am thinking of the
very best boy that ever was in this school, and
where do you think he is?” All the boys; “In
Heaven, sir I” “ Well —er —no, not exactly. He
has a grocery-shop of his own.” — French.
Russian humor: “Little brother, have
you any vodki?” “No, have you?” “No.”
“ Little brother, have you any money with which
to purchase vodki ? ” “ No, have you ? ” “No.”
“Then, little brother, let us sit on the grass and
talk about vodki.”
Two and three years ago, just after the
homestead “ blockers’ ” idea got into working
order in S.A., and when orchards were planted
by the dozen, the jaui-makers sat down hard on
prices, and hundreds of tons of fruit rotted on the
trees and under them because it didn’t pay to
cart it a dozen miles to market. Then desperate
growers went round with an axe, and chopped
down the trees that were too big to be dragged up.
This year the jam-maker can’t get a look-in—the
retail fruit people want it all, and more.

BRIDGED PROSPECTUS OP
The Ovens & Auckland
Alluvial G.M. Co.
(To be registered as a “ No Liability " Company.)
Capitali
£30,000 In 60,000 Shares of 10s. each.
80,000 shares as paid up to 6s. are retained by the vendors
(the Ovens and Buckland G.M. Syndicate No Liability),
and
30,000 shares are offered to the public on the following
terms Is. 6d. per share on application, and Is. 6d.
per share on allotment. The whole of the money
thus raised to be placed to the credit of the company,
less brokerage and flotation expenses.
DIRECTORS:
To be appointed by the shareholders at the first meeting
of the company).
BROKERS:
CLARKE & CO., 309 Collins-street, Melbourne.
SOLICITOR:
H. HALE BUDD, Bank-place, Collins-street, Melb.
LEGAL MANAGER (pro tern.):
EDWARD W. HUBBARD, Norwich Union Chambers,
34 Queen-street, Melbourne.
OBJECTS OF THE COMPANY.
This company is being formed to acquire from
the vendors (the Ovens and Buckland G.M. Syn-
dicate No Liability)—Lease 3656, Beechworth,
35a. 3r. ; lease 3750, Beechworth, 16a. 2r. 35p. ;
lease 3914, Beechworth, 17a. lr. 22p. ; together
with the southern-most portion of lease application
4 P.P., Bright, 130 a. 3r. 19p., making in all 200
acres 2roods and 36 perches, or thereabouts. All
these leases are contiguous, and contain the very
pick of auriferous ground in the Ovens Valley,
and are within one mile of the Eurobin railway
station.
In placing this property before the public, the
vendors would point out that they have spent over
£l2OO to determine the value of these alluvial flats
during the past 18 months, and have tested the
ground in the first instance by putting down bores,
which disclosed a Imdy of wash AVERAGING
Oft. in DEPTH, from 3ft. to 4ft. from grass. To
make assurance doubly sure, shafts were sunk on
the sites of bores, as well as elsewhere, and
in each instance the wash was struck and pro-
spects taken out, all of which were of a highly
payable nature. In order to drain the ground
and so determine the extent of wash, a race was
put in from the Ovens River for 24 chains. This
race cut through 19 chains of payable dirt.
Drives were also put in on the high bank, and as
much as 17ft. of WASHDIRT WAS DIS-
CLOSED, CARRYING GOLD FROM TOP
TO BOTTOM. Mr. Reginald Murray, late
Government Geologist, has visited the property,
and extracts from his report will be found in full
prospectus ; also, a report from Mr. W. B. Gray,
of Maldon. The services of a practical engineer,
Mr. R. R. Hedley, of Yackandandah, who has
special knowledge of the barge system (now being
so successfully worked by the Hon. J. A. Wallace,
of Staghorn Flat and elsewhere), were obtained.
His report gives a good idea both as to the great
value of the holding and the adaptability of the
ground for working with a barge. Mr. A. E.
Glover’s report determines the value of the gold
won, £4 2s. llsd. per oz.
When 20,000 shares are applied for the company
may be registered.
SPECIAL FEATURES.
The vendors are so thoroughly satisfied with the
value of the property that they prefer taking
shares rather than stipulating for a cash considera-
tion, such shares only paid up to 6s.
There is no further prospecting work required, |
the ground having been most exhaustively tested 1
and its value determined, and it now only requires ,
suitable machinery to make this a paying com-
pany.
Full prospectus can be obtained with extracts
from report of Mr. Reginald A. F. Murray,
together with full report from Mr. R. R. Hedley
and letter from W. B. Gray, Esq., of Maldon,
from the brokers, and from the legal manager, as
above.

October 3rd, 1898.
Mr. W. Webber,
Proprietor of “ Vitadatio.’'
Dear Sir,—lt is with great pleasure that I write you
these few lines to tell you what your great remedy,
“ Vitadatio,” has done for me. At the beginning of my
illness I was living in America, and was attending a ball
to see the “ New Year in,” in 1897. On my way home I
took a very severe cold, through going from the heated
ballroom out into the cold air (the snow being two or three
feet deep). I was laid up for some considerable time, and
'J
r
BEFORE.
m
fm
J 'ji * *
X
1
\
AFTER.
treated by some of the most eminent physicians in
America, but all to no advantage. Hemorrhage of the
lung set in, and 1113' doctors said that I had “ gone into
Consumption,” and that there was no cure for me, and
advised me to return to my native land, Australia. So
on the 4th June, 1897, I arrived back in, Melbourne. I
then consulted many doctors, and tried all sorts of medi-
cines, but got no relief, so naturally began to despair.
My husband heard from our friend, Mr. H. M'Kenzie,
sewer contractor at South Yarra, how his son had been
cured of Consumption by the use of “ Yitadatio.” Mr.
M'Kenzie urged my husband to .give me a course of the
remedy, which he did ; and right glad I am that he did,
as the hemorrhages have censed, and also the terrible
racking cough that used to keep me awake all night. I
can now attend to my duties, and can truly say that
“Yitadatio” has cured me. For the sake of other
sufferers who may be in the same plight as I was, I give
you full permission to use this letter as you may think
fit. We are well known in these colonies, as my husband
was in the furrier business for years.
I remain, Yours truly,
F. A. CLARK.

The wirelens-telegraphy notion has pro-
gressed so much that Tesla, the celebrated eleo-
trician, expects to be able, without cables, to
transmit energy from Niagara Falls to London.
N.S:W. Govt. Architect Vernon says that
brick is a more desirable building material than
stone. He does not know of any premises in
Sydney 80 years old.in which the stone is any-
thing like in good condition, whereas he knows of
brick buildings of that age, the bricks in which
showed no sign of deterioration.

A woman was seriously, perhaps fatally,
injured by being jolted out of a vehicle on a Vie.
bush road, the other day. The children ran to
seek assistance, and when they returned the
woman’s head and face had been eaten away by
pi/s. Recalls Ambrose Bierce’s ghastly story,
“ The Coup de Grice.”
When Queen Victoria recently visited
Netley (military) Hospital, she asked a small boy,
who presented her with some flowers, where he
came from, and, upon being informed that he be
longed to a certain training ship, asked him if he
had a “ good time ” on board. Thus has this
much-needed and long-disfavored Amurkanism at
last made good its footing as literally “ Queen’s
English.”
“New York may have its ‘hoodlums,’
’Frisco its ‘ toughs,’ London its ‘ hooligans,’ Aus-
tralia its ‘larrikins,’ but Birmingham alone has
its ‘peaky blinders,’” writes an English-exiled
Australian to The Bulletin. [Where does
the “ ]>erky perisher ” come in ? Isn’t he
from Birmingham ?—Ed. B.] “The ‘peaky
blinder’ has his special tailor, who advertises
himself as the ‘ only genuine maker of flash tog-
gery.’ He is the‘champion builder,’and wishes
to ‘ put them awake to the fact that he has dabbed
his fins on a nobby swag of stuff for his ready
money, which will be made up in the best styles
at low prices.’ A pair of ‘ ikey cords, cut slap-up,
with the artful dodge and fakement down the
side ’ (now, Riley-street and Chowder beat that,
if you can), for half-a-sovereign ; while ‘proper
cut togs, turned out up to the knocker,’ will only
cost a sovereign. A pair of ‘ kerseymere or fancy
doeskin kicksies, any color, cut pegtop or to drop
down over the trotters,’ will run the would-be
elegant into twelve-and-six, but the wearer of
these ‘ kicksies ’ will be ‘ fit to toe it with any
swell.’ ‘Lavenders, built spanky, with a double
fakement down the sides, and artful buttons af
the bottom,’can be had at all prices; while ‘fancy
sleeve vests, cut very saucy,’ cost from nine
shillings.”

THE ILLUSTRATED LONDON NEWS of
Sept. 28, 1895, says:
“ If I were asked which single medicine I should
prefer to take abroad with me, as likely to be
most generally useful, to the exclusion of all
others, I should say Chlorodyne. I never travel
without it, and its general applicability to the
relief of a large number of simple ailments forms
its best recommendation.”
A perfervid Bulletin correspondent, with
a pretty turn for sarcophagi, and mausoleums,
and mementi mori, writes thus on suicide: —
One’s life, I suppose, is one’s own —perhaps? But,
postulating this, how would one like to die ? I
would die in a Roman bath—blood-warm, a gentle zephyr
fanning with an air caught from a sea of Araby, laden
with all the aromatic spices of the East, the recumbent,
naked, Pagan body, tired to satiety of pleasures, housing
an alleged soul (a thinking and loving something), wearied
with the ardor of search for love and friendship, and inte-
rest—if only in the newspaper-hoarding score of a cricket-
match ! I would die to the scent of the flowers that
bloomed in Eden, to that of the humble mignonette
that struggles for a sweet, sad life on a cottager's
window-sill, to the odor of the rose that blooms
on my lady’s bosom, to the fierce energy of
the god-blossom magnolia. I would die to the sounds
of some soft Lydian melody, or a German chant to the
All-father, voiced in the organ tones of Bach, or to the
myriad-orchestrated harmonies of Berlioz when he damned
Faust past all the redemption of all future tear-dropping
weaknesses of the King Almighty. I would die to the
dulcet tones of a nocturne by Chopin, to the weird musio
of a Hungarian march, to the savage strains of the down-
trodden who chanted unpublished “ Marseillaises,” and
to all the glory of the hymn-triumphant of him who rode
through the gates of Death in splendor at Persepolis 1
“Phoebus" tells The Bulletin some-
thing about old Potts’ dog:—
At Booloocooroo old “Dad” Potts had a dog,
upon which he spent an amount of affection that
disbursed in a proper quarter, would have suffloed for
the sentimental needs of a score of lovers. The dog’s
name was Jerusalem, but it should have been Dan-to-
Beersheba. He was not much of a dog to look at; you
could pick up a better-looking dog than this one any-
where. On the contrarj', his ugliness and inbred coarse-
ness of demeanor had passed into a local proverb. Had
he been favored with as many legs as a centipede, Jeru-
salem would not have differed much from others of his
kind ; but it was queer to’ see such a wealth of dog
wedded to such poverty of legs. It was beautiful to see
Jerusalem rounding the angle of a wall and turning his
head to see how the remainder of the procession was
coming on. He was once circumnavigating a small out-
house, when, catching sight of his own hindquarters, he
flew into a terrible rage. The sight of another dog
always had this effect on Jerusalem, and more especially
where, as in this case, he thought he could grasp an un-
fair advantage. So Jerusalem took after the retreating
foe as hard as ever he could hook it. Round and round
he flew, but the faster he went the morethecircle widened,
until presently he lost sight of his enemy altogether.
Then he slowed down, determined to accomplish his end by
strategy. Sneaking closely round the outhouse, he moved
cautiously forward, and, when he made the full circuit,
came smack up against his own tail. Making a sudden
spring, which must have stretched him like a bit of india-
rubber, he fastened his teeth into his rump, hanging on
like a poor relation. He felt sure he had nailed the other
dog, but he was equally confident the other dog had
nailed him, so the contest was simplified into a question
of endurance—and Jerusalem was an animal full of
pluck. The conflict was maintained with deathless per-
severance, until Dad Potts discovered the belligerent and
uncoupled him. Then Jerusalem looked up at his
master with a shake of his head, as much as to say, “ It’s
a precious good thing for the other pup ; but who took
him off me? ” And then (Thanks. —Ed. B.]

There’s a man in Melbourne who has
written a book about Westralia. It isn’t a book
of statistics. It doesn’t deal with gold-mines or
jarrah forests. If it touches on pearls it is only
to show at what cost of aboriginal life and suffer-
ing they are procured. It is, in fact, an indict-
ment, and the man says he is going to make many
people wish they had never been born. He has a
big trunk-full of records from which he has got
his information, and he declares that when “ A
Modern Inferno ” gets circulated in Groperland,
there will be gnashing of teeth. But authors are
very sanguine—and Groperlanders’ hides are
tough.

avoid intense suffering. Never lie without it.
Over fifty years in use. Refuse all substitutes.
Pots, 2s. Sold everywhere.*
Another old London landmark is about to
be broken up, lest it tumble down when least ex-
pected. The shop in Fleet-street facing the
entrance to Chancery-lane—the shop with a first-
floor front protruding over the pavement in true
Shakespearean style, and many dingy evidences
of bygone splendor clinging to its decay. An
inspiration on the gabled front states that the
queer old fcrib was Cardinal Wolsey’s ‘‘palace”
350 years ago. For as long as anyone can remem-
ber it has been a barber’s “saloon,” much fre-
quented by barristers. There was a rival shaver
quartered within one of the stone supports of
Temple Bar until the historical Bar passed away,
but he died of antiquity and a broken heart when
the city improvers drove him from his cavernous
old home. His customers took their patronage to
the man at Wolsey’s palace, and now that this
last of the historical places to get shaved at is
under sentence of demolition a number of old
fellows will, The Bulletin presumes, lay their
heads back and beseech the tearful barber to cut
their throats.

Hope deferred maketh the heart sick, and more
particularly so when the health is concerned. No
eye can see the depth of despair, where the heart
of a person with broken health lies. Mrs. Jew, 61
Wickham Terrace, Brisbane, for years proprietress
of the Freemasons’ Hotel and Golden Age Hotel,
Gympie, does not go into detail, but states clearly
what she means in a few words. She writes :
“ For some years I have suffered from a, sluggish
liver, accompanied at times by severe pains in my
chest and shoulders. These pains not only caused
me intense suffering, but they often kept me from
doing my household work, and confined me to my
bed. No medicine gave me permanent relief, and
many failures made me despondent, so when I
tried Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills 1 had little
hope that they would give me any great relief, but
I can assure you that one bottle made me feel a
different woman. I find they are a very good
pill, acting mildly and searchingly, benefiting the
whole system, and I can recommend them to any-
body needing such a medicine. You may make
what use you like of this letter.”

to take a firm hold on me. They were
recommended to me for that terrible complaint.
Previous to getting influenza for a long time I had
been listless, languid, and quite unable to do my
work, owing, I believe, to the change of life. I
can only describe my life as a most wretched one,
owing to this condition. Well, the Pills not only
cured me of influenza, but I was delighted to find
that I was cured of the depressed, run-down feel-
ing and I became bright and energetic; in fact, I
am'far and away better than I have felt for years,
and I take pleasure in recommending the pills to
all who need a medicine for such complaints.”

Nero at his worst was overflowing with the

street, Sebastopol, Ballarat (Vic.). Pity the sub-
ject of King Biliousness ! The tyrants of old did
not upset the stomach and derange the whole
human system. This is what happened to Miss
Jones. She relates : “ For a long time I was
subject to biliousness and fainting fits. Lately I
have heard a lot of people saying what a splendid
medicine Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are, and
this induced me to try them. Now I can endorse
what others have said, as the Pills have cured me
completely.'* I have not finished a bottle of them,

need any more medicine. I have spent a con-
siderable amount of money trying to get better,
and Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills have cured me
at scarcely any expense. I should say that there
can be very little sickness in the house where

In his case it may be said that instinct saved him,
as it lead him to try Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills : “ For a long time I had a very violent cold,
such a cold which leads to consumption, or some-
thing equally as bad, and had a most uncomfort-
able rasping cough which used to shake my whole
system, and seemed to break me up. I got a
notion that Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills would
relieve me. I took them and the result is that
they cured me.” To show his good faith, Mr.
Creed has got Mr. James Allison proprietor of
the Mount Pleasant Hotel, Drake, to witness his
signature to the above statement, which we have
received.

The Exercise-Yard —A Prison
Poem.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’m walking about in the exercise-yard,
Brick walls around me, a warder on guard ;
Time after time the ten paces I measure,
One hour a day for my health and my pleasure.
Blue sky above me—my God ! but it’s hard
Walking about in the exercise-yard.
Tommy the Nark’s in the next yard to mine,
Put me away to “ the demons,” the swine !
He got a couple and I got a “ fin ”
By the Lord Harry, the Judge rubbed it in !
Said with the devil’s own brush I was tarred,
Sent me to walk in the exercise-yard.
T’other side’s walking a gentleman bloke,
Lost someone’s money a-backin’ a moke.
Don’t take it well either, looks a bit blue,
Got no more sense than to barge with the screw,
Thinking, I s’pose, of the life he has marred ;
Good place to think in—the exercise-yard.
As I’m tramping I think of the missus in court;
The lawyer had told her my time would be short.
When the Judge gave me five—well, she looked a
bit off
(I hope she won’t go and take up with that tofE
Him with the nose that got broke when we
sparred—
I wish I had him in the exereise-yard).
But it’s no use of fretting—ah ! there goes the bell.
“ Put on your masks ! ” Now it’s back to my cell,
Back to my peter and hominy in,
Satan’s own mixture of maize-meal and sin,
Then safe for the night I am bolted and barred,
Done for a day with the exercise-yard.
Pentridge, ’9B.
BUNT.
English church papers are very hot over
the fact that Mahometanism is to be taught in
the Khartoum Gordon College, while Christianity
is tabooed.
David Syme is lavishing bawbees over the
new Age office in a manner that is simply awfu’.
The building will occupy a little over twice the
area of the old one, and although it is merely
cemented after the manner of most of the boom
edifices, the usually canny Dawvid is flashing
money around in a neatly - sculptured group
with three figures emblematical of what happens
to a disobedient Ministry, while on Ihe apex of
the pediment will stand a 12ft.-high figure of Mer-
cury skipping through the air with a hunk of
“copy ” in his fist.
The Boom of the Surf.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was eight o’clock, and the evening air was
filled with the music of the cicadas. Through the
open door could be seen one brilliant star flaming
beautifully in the dark blue sky. The nurse had
left the lamps unlit as the moon came creeping up
and poured its light down on the desolate hills,
the lonely Coast Hospital, and heaving ocean.
A great rest stole through the ward, and the men
forgot all their anxiety and half their pain. Their
silence was eloquent ; but x»resently, when the
lamps were lit and conversation started, Llewel-
lyn, the patient in the next bed, turned over, and,
fixing his big, hollow eyes upon me, asked
languidly : “ What, in your opinion, is the most
remarkable feature of the present age ?
I considered for a moment and replied : “ The
scientific development and industrial organisation,
I suppose.”
“Not at all,” he said. “The strangest and
most inexplicable thing about this epoch of the
world’s history is the silence of God.”
“ The silence of God ! ” I repeated, be-
wilderedly.
“ Yes, the silence of God. The world is full of
unbelief ; faith is dying, and only a faint, linger-
ing hope of future life remains ; for man, in spite
of all, hungers for immortality. And one little
word of the Deity would set all straight. I would
give my life to hear God’s voice as did the holy
men of old. (His eyes blazed with enthusiasm).
Think of it, my friend— to hear God speak ! -The
glory of it ! I would give my life to be so blessed.
I have longed and prayed for it, and all niy-re-
quests but that have been granted. God will not
speak ! ” He turned over and sighed, and a few
minutes later was asleep.
* • * *
I was suddenly awakened by Llewellyn grasp-
ing my arm. “ Wake up and talk to me, my
friend —for lam going to die !” he said. I sat up
hastily, and would have called the nurse, but he
begged me not. “I have heard it,” he went on,
“ and understood ! —the voice of God. Listen ! ”
And he placed his hands over his ears.
I listened, awestruck ; but heard nothing save
the long, monotonous boom of the surf. I looked
down the dimly-lit ward to where the nurse sat
quietly writing by the night light. Hearing our
voices, she made a sign to us to speak in whispers.
“ It’s only the sea, old man ! —only the noise of
the surf.”
“ Yes, yes,” he gasped. “ But you don’t under-
stand. The sea makes the noise—but it is the
voice of Omnipotence—and I understand it !
Listen to it telling of the eternity of Deity—lo,ooo
aeons ago it was testifying as it is to-night—lo
million years hence it will still be booming on —
mighty, tireless as ever. And, now I have heard
it, I cannot bear to live. The tremendous sig .”
I stopped him : “To-morrow I’ll tell the doctor,
and he will send you to Camden, or somewhere
where you can’t hear the surf.”
“ Useless,” he replied. “ I might have got better
if I had never heard it—never understood its awful
meaning. But now it would find me in the centre
of the globe ! Mortality cannot bear even the
voice of Immortal Omnipotence. Goodbye ! ”
He threw himself down on his pillow, and I
oalled the nurse and told her. Bh*» looked grave,
and sent for the doctor.
m • • •
When I awoke in the morning Llewellyn was
dead.
HEMES.
A Brixton (London) grocer is catering for
decayed-tooth customers. With every two pounds
of tea he gives away an order on a dentist for the
withdrawal <>f one tooth.
The monster White Star liner, Oceanic, is
now afloat. Eight such vessels, placed end to end,
would exceed a mile’s-length by U 7 yards ! The
ship’s coal capacity will carry her 20,000 miles
at 14 miles an hour. And the White Star Co is
building an even bigger cargo-boat.
A delightful piece of unnatural history
appears in an Argus leader, dealing with the
sewerage system—of all subjects in the world.
The scribe complains of the ugliness of the tin
pipes attached to houses, and grabs at a
simile: “ The district bristles with these pipes as
the back of an angry hedgehog does with quills.”
But, unfortunately, the hedgehog has no quills, and
such an object as an angry hedgehog is beyond
conception. As well speak of an angry mouse.
The hedgehog is the most timid thing that lives in
English woods, and when he rolls himself into a
ball with his spikes—not quills—up, he does it for
his own protection. It must have been the same
leader-writer who wrote, some months ago, “ The
bells that ring from the dome of St. Paul’s
Cathedra], in London to-day, will echo round the
world ” —when there are no bells in the dome of
St. Paul’s.
“ Scotty the Wrinkler” to The Bulletin :
“ C.S.” talks of prostitution as a “ guild-organisation ”
in the Middle Ayes, and about the special protection of
the municipality, Crown and Church. Let me tell him
of it in the lDth century. In 1866, to be precise, Colonel
Walker Fooks was then commanding the K.A. atMooltan.
I was adjutant. One day the colonel asked me if there
were any public women (lal bebees) in the regimental
bazaar. T said no. Then the colonel—“ I am surprised
that you are unaware that it is your duty to ascertain
that there is a proper supply. There are four empty
houses, and you must see at once that they are occupied
by two women each. I will not have the men going to
the infantry and the sudder bazaars.” To a Mussulman
chuprassie (a regular bulwar or ladies’ man) 1 gave the
order to announce that applicants for the position of
artillery lady-helps should parade in front of my tent
next morning. When that arrived it found a circle of
some hundred shrouded forms (some accompanied by
male friends) awaiting inspection. They were an average
lot, so I chose the four fairest and the four darkest
(“ black but comely ”), and, for anything I know, they’re
in business unto this day.
A few of Queen Victoria’s Thoughts,
alleged to have been uttered to a Maid of Honor,
who gives them away in the latest number of the
Quiver magazine : “ When I was a child my dear
mother took me about a great deal, and I saw
people at work in all kinds of ways and in every
sort of industry. I have never ceased to think of
them. . . . See what they have done since I came
to the Throne by their thought and toil; they
have made the empire what it is. The work will
continue after I am gone, but I sometimes wonder
in what way. Sovereigns have their influence, and
when they die it stops, or seems to stop. In only
a few instances it is otherwise. King Alfred
turned the national mind to learning, and perhaps
the influence he exerted never wholly died. The
last Henry made the country Protestant. Eliza-
beth—the great Elizabeth—transformed it into a
nation of heroes. ... I can hardly hope to
leave such an influence, and yet, under my rule,
the people who were counted by hundreds have
grown to thousands and millions, and that has
come about because, for the most part, my reign
has been one of peace. There have been wars,
but they have been to establish peace. ... If,
when f am dead, the English-speaking people
honor me enough to think of what I would wish
and pray for on their behalf, I would have them
always associate my name with peace. ... In
what way could they please the Almighty more
than by promoting the ends which during my
reign have been the means of causing so much
general happiness ! . . . Nothing that I know of
would give me so much pleasure as to be assured
that my spirit could in any way watch over and
aid the accomplishment of that noble work.”
Merciful powders !
Some “BULLETIN" THOUGHTH.
Our Sovereign has influence for evil or for good ;
Anointed by the Lord she was, we’ve always understood ;
King Alfred made his people wise, Queen Bessie made ’em
brave —
So, how about Her Gracious, whom we pray the Lord to
save ?
What’s the bearing of Her Gracious
On her kingdom “ free ” and spacious?
Let’s be honest and veracious
In our calculations grave.
She became a wife and mother of the ordinary sort,
And the watchful Mrs. Grundy spoke no scandal of her
court; . ,
She has shirked the gaudy duties which pertain to royal
And preserved her mind and body from the strain of
working late;
And by placidly persisting
In the pastime of existing.
She has somehow been assisting
To make Britain truly Great.
Sing hey ! the modern railroads, where locomotives steam;
Sing hey ! the puffing ironclads—how wonderful they
s eein J
The telegraph, the phonograph, the telephone as well,
They all have been invented ’neath our monarch’s magic
spell.
Yea, she smilingly assented
When those marvels were invented,
And she sat at home contented,
Thinking “Ain’t I doing well!”
In Asia and in Africa and sundry savage parts
She has multiplied the number of her people's loyal
And her foreign land possessions, lo ! they rapidly in-
crease,
For Hingland takes the freehold, which is better than a
Fruitful savage-land possessions,
They are labelled as “concessions ”
For the blackamoors’ “ transgressions "
In the interests of peace.
Now, can the work continue when the Queen is called
away ?
Will the People of to-morrow toil as toilers toil to-day ?
Will science, arts, and commerce still as resolutely thrive
In the sorrow-stricken future when the Queen is not alive ?
There’ll be no more jubilating,
Eating, drinking, populating—
We shall simply all be waiting
For the Deluge to arrive.
