"Agonising Spasms of Pain
"THAT MADE ME VELL.
99
“My present state of good health
i owe to CLEMENTS TON
The Case of
Mr. WILLIAM T. EATHER,
(From the Windsor and Richmond
«* Gazette ”)
(by our special reporter.)
A Gazette reporter, having frequently
heard of Mr. Eather’s case, made it his
business to interview that gentleman, who
lives in East Market Street, Richmond,
N.S.W.
“ Have you any objection to tell me
about your illness, and how you got cured,”
asked the reporter ?
“ Not the least,” answered the persoi
addressed. “ I suffered
From Poorness of Blood
and nervousness, and from a general
derangement of all my internal organs, with
the exception of my lungs. I don’t know
how it came about, but probably through
cold and damp, and an improper attention
to my diet.”
“ What was the first symptom ? **
! ‘A Blotched and Marked Skin
and intermittent headaches. I frequently
had heartburn, and nearly always an acrid
taste in the mouth. I didn’t care for my food.
My wife used to call me to a well-cooked
dinner, and I would just look at it, and
perhaps taste it, and then say
I can’t eat any now,
111 wait till tea-time. When tea-time
came my appetite was still wanting. I
could never eat anything approaching a
decent meal, and everything I ate gave me
pain and discomfort. The consequence was
that I
Lost Strength and Flesh.”
Any other sufferings ” ?
“ Pains in the head started. It eeemed
as though a load was strapped on my head,
and it felt so heavy that I had to support it
with my hands. Other pains came on in
my loins and back. Sometimes they would
fetch me up with such an agonising spasm
of pain
That I yelled again.
Then nervousness came on; and proper
sleep at night was a thing which I never
had now. Often and often, when, after
hours of lying awake tossing about, I fell
into a heavy slumber, I should wake with a
sudden jump, feeling as though I was falling
down from a great height. My wife often
used to notice me jump in my sleep, as
though I had been struck by something. I
think I told you my food gave me pain after
eating. \\ ell, sometimes I would wake
suddenly with
A Peculiar Suffocating Sensa-
tion
in my throat, and I really thought some-
times that I should strangle before I could
get my breath, but I suppose this was but a
symptom of indigestion. I got so ill and
weak that I could no longer carry on my
work. It was too much for me. The pains
in my back made walking almost an
impossibility, and I was only too pleased to
sit down to
Rest My Aching Limbs.
My liver now' gave me the most trouble.
Nothing would make it act. I tried all sorts
of medicines, and my wife often used to
bring me physic from the chemists, but all
to no purpose, and I got a bilious, yellow
colour. At last I called in a doctor.”
“ Did he relieve you ?
“ No, he didn’t. I soon got tired of
doctors and their medicines. I must have
gone to six or seven altogether, but not a
bit of good did I get from them all. I
didn’t know what to do, nor which way to
look for relief. I was completely prostrated,
and in a terrible state—a mere wreck of
what I used to be. I had just strength left
to wander about in a dull and despondent
way, in the vain hope of the fresh air doing
good.”
“ I am informed you successfully used
Clements Tonic. What first made you
think of it ” ?
“One day I was walking about in the
bush, when I saw a bottle and a little b<«>k
lying in the grass near each other, and I
have often since thought what a bit of luck
it was that my steps should wander in that
direction. The bottle was empty; it had
once contained Clements Tonic, and the
book contained a number of instances of
cures effected by that remedy. I tat d-wn
then and there and read it through, and the
same day I gave Clements Tonic a trial.”
“ With what effect ?■”
“At first, none ; it had struck a bad case,
but I persisted in the use of Clements
Tonic. I am not a man to do things by
halves. There was a rcinaiKahle change.
Somehow' I felt better all over, .and began
To Stand up Straight
in like a man. The periods of sleep
wnich I had at night grew longer and
longer, till I slept lhe whole night through
without once waking. In the morning I
felt refieshed. By st 11 keeping on with
Clements Tonic I was able to get rid of
indigestion, and once more came to my
meals with an appetite.”
“Jlow many bottles did you find ib
necessary to take?”
“I think seven bottles completely put
me on my legs again, cleared my skin of all
blotches and pimples, and rid me of my
nervousness and the other troubles to which
I was subject. The way Clements Tonic
drove the pains out of my back was a marvel
to me.”
“ Well, you look O.K. now.”
“ I feel splendid, and can work all day*
and all night for the matter of that, if it
was wanted, and not feel weak or
unusually tired. My present state of
excellent health I owe to Clements Tonic,
and it is not the only proof I have bad of
the wonderful recuperative properties of
that remedy. There’s my wife, she was
troubled with fearful headaches.
She Suffered Terribly,
I can assure you. Well, two or three
bottles entirely cured her, and she has not
had them since. Then my eldest son took
Clements Tonic. He got completely run
down, very thin and weak, couldn’t touch
his food, and was a mere shadow of wdiat
he is now. A few bottles put him as right
as a trivet, and now he can eat with an
excellent appetite, and weighs twelve stone
or more; he weighed far less than that, I
can tell you, when he was ill. If anyone
wants to know mote about Clements Tonic
let them come to me. It put me right
when doctors and chemists couldn’t even
give me relief, and consequently I shall
always recommend it.”
“ If you look in the Gazette, Mr. Eather,
you will see an account of all you’ve said
to me.”
“I don’t mind ; its but the truth, and
that can only do good.

Some Sydney doss-house literature. “Out-
cast ” to The Bulletin :
Gouli-Gouli" describes the Salvation Army People’s
Palace, but has bad no actual experience of it. Poverty
has given it to me. The 3d. doss might be imagined a
great comfort; It looks it. But think of 300 men sleeping
in one compartment! The snoring among that 300
would frighten the bravest man. Then there is always
an element of rowdy ism among the men. Many will dis-
turb for the mere love of it. The other night one stal led
a mouth-organ ; time midnight. A hundred voi'ses cursed
him, but only made the brute worse. Another .veiled an un-
scene request, and then pandemonium started. Army
men seized the first offender, who was naked, and tried
to put him out fliot followed. .Matters looked serious,
but at last the naked offender went.into the street in his
skin ; his clo:h-s were thrown after him. Then things
were lively till 4 a.m., singing and other “harm-
less'' amusement being indulged in. Three hun-
dred men in one «oinpaitinent opens the door to
riot. Lodging house keepers who have suffered
from Salvation Army competition could pack the
place and .in one night have it wrecked. I tried
sixpriiii.v doss for change. Worse than other, in some
ways. \ou get a spring mat trass, and sheets, &c., but
you are in the gallery over the 300 in the old Natatorium
sw minting hath. You re-brentlie the breath and exhala-
tions of 301. the noises from 1 a l l ow are always wit h you,
and, while there is a semblance of cleanliness, the attempt
at keeping the place sw cel is none too successful. : alva-
tion Army don’t believe in a minimum or even living
wage. They don't believe in wage at all. Result, poor
loan without shelter or food goes to them. They send
him to work for one p onyworth of soup and bread and
a doss on the floor. Not likely he will, or can,
do much for that accommodation. Blankets are not
fumigated daily, and are consequently getting vermin
into them. Shower-bath is very acceptable, but what is
one towel among one hundred? Food .You can get a
plate of porridge for Id., or cup of coffee, or tea and bread
and butter, same price. Then there are curry-and-rice,
and Irish-stew, made of cold meat, begged from largo
caravanserais in city for Prison Gate Brigade. Booth
said over £2OOO was spent on place, and expected public
to pay it. .Surely the price of beds, &c., is a handsome
return on the money laid out? Another thing, clean
your boots, penny weekly ; leave your parcel, penny
weekly; needle and cotton, Id. ! Salvation Army’s
creed seems to be “ bbg, take all, and start at one penny."
And here is another correspondent:—
Somewhere about 350 people sleep on the premises
every night, bringing in a revenue from this source of
£5 10s., or £33 10s. for the week;and its meals, which are
of the cheapest and coarsest, probably bring in as much
more. The beds certainly are at present cleaner
than the average run of even shilling doss-houses,
but, of course, everything is new ; the meals can
he surpassed at any of the city 3d. ami 4d. hash-shops.
If the beds, however, are cleanl.y there is not much en-
couragement given to their casual inmates to adopt the
same virtue. In this old home of swimming, the Natato-
rium, where in the cubicles the mattresses rest on disused
baths, there are but a dozen basins, with an equal num-
ber of towels, for the use of over 300 men, and otic
shower-bath, to get at which latter it is necessary to pass
through a urinal! As for dressing and undressing, the
demands of decency have been overlooked altogether.
Those who want to get to sleep need not think of turning in
before midnight, for every word spoken by the 250 three-
penny bunkers down below can be heard up above by the
sixpenny and ninepenny inmates, and for a choice selec-
tion of oaths and obscenity generally the conversation
would be hard to beat.
Lagos, the British colony to which Gov.
Macgregor, late of Papua, has been promoted, is
described in the papers as “the centre of the
palm-oil tract.’’ The palm-oil tract —the heaven
of politicians.

TESTIMONIAL .
Mr. W. ARNOTT.
Dear Sir,
I enclose a photo, of my little boy, one year and six
months old. I fed him for two months on artificial foods ;
but he suffered intensely with bowel complaint, and became
quite a shadow of his former self. None of the artificial
foods seemed to agree with him. Everyone that seen him
said he could not last much longer—he could not retain his
food. A friend said to me—“ Try Arnott’s Biscuits soaked in
boiled milk.” I did so ; and, to my great surprise, after a
few days his bowels seemed much better. So I continued
with your famous biscuits, and this is the result of your
biscuit—he is now quite a strong, healthy boy. I thought it
only fair that mothers should know the value of your
biscuits to feed their babies on. My little boy would prefer
biscuit to any food; still he was fed entirely on your
biscuits. It is with great pleasure that I send you this
testimonial with his photo. You can see he is the picture
of health. Yours respectfully,
Mrs. E O. DEA.
St. Kilda Terrace,
Marrickville Road,
Marrickville.
KED ON
ARNOTT’S Milk Arrowroot Biscuits!
The Children's Food of Health. Always ash for ArnotVs when purchasing.
Crime in the Bush.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The average city mail’s ignorance concerning the
nearer bush—to say nothing of “Out-Back ” —and
the human life therein, is greater even than the
average new-chum’s, for the new-ehum usually
takes paina to collect information concerning the
land of his exile, adoption or hope. To the city
mind the drovers, the shearers, the station-hands,
the “cockies”or farmers, the teamsters, and even
the diggers, all lielong to one and the same class,
and are accepted in the street’ under the general
term of “ bushies ” —and no questions asked. The
city mind is too much occupied by the board-and-
lodging or rent problems, &c., to have any but the
vaguest ideas concerning the unique conditions of
the life that lies beyond the cities. And, in return,
the Sydney or Melbourne man is regarded Out-Back
as a jackeroo or new-chum —little or no distinction
being made between the Australian-born “ green-
hand ” and the newly-arrived cockney; which is
just. But it is with the farmer or “ cookie ” class
that the writer is here chiefly concerned, for it is
mostly in the so-called “settled” districts that
are committed the crimes which seem so brutally
senseless or motiveless to city people.
The shearer is a social animal at his worst; he
is often a city bushman— i.e., a man who has been
through and round and between the provinces by
rail and boat. Not unfrequently he is an English
public school man and a man of the world; so
even the veriest Out-Back bushie, whose metro-
polis is Bourke, is brought in touch with out-
side civilisation. But there are hundreds of out-
of-the-way places in the nearer bush of Australia
—hidden away in unheard-of “pockets” in the
ranges ; on barren creeks (abandoned by pioneer-
ing farmers and pastoralists “moving up country”
half a century ago); up at the ends of long, dark
gullies, and away out on God-forsaken “box,”
native-apple, or stringy-bark flats —where fami-
lies live for generations in mental darkness
almost inconceivable in this enlightened age and
country. They are often in a worse condition
mentally than savages to the manner born; for
natural savages have a social law, a social inter-
course —perhaps more or less inadequate, but
infinitely better than none at all. Some of these
families live from one year’s end to another without
seeing a face except the face of somebodyof theirown
class, and that of an occasional stranger whose char-
acter or sanity must at least be doubtful, to explain
his presence in such places. Some of these
families are descended from a convict of the worst
type on one side or the other, perhaps on both;
and, if not born criminals, are trained in shady
ways from childhood. Conceived and bred under
the shadow of exile, hardship or “ trouble,”
the sullen, brooding spirit which enwraps their
lonely bush-buried homes will carry further their
moral degradation. You may sometimes see a
dray or spring-cart, of antiquated pattern, drag-
ging wearily and unnoticed into the “township,”
and containing a woman, haggard and spiritless-
looking, or hard and vicious-faced—or else a sullen,
brooding man—who sells produce for tea, flour
and sugar, and goes out again within the hour,
without, perhaps, having exchanged half-a-dozen
words with anyone. This is the only hint con-
veyed to the outer fringe of God’s country —and
wasted on apathetic neighbors—of the existence of
such a people.
These places need to be humanised. There are
things done in the bush (where large families, and
sometimes several large families, pig together in
ignorance in badly-partitioned huts) known well
to neighbors; or to school-teachers mere lads,
going through their martyrdom in such places
—and to girl-teadhers too, God forgive us!—or
even to the police ; things which would make
a strong man shudder. Clean-minded people
shrink from admitting the existence of such things,
until one, bolder than the rest, and with the cer-
tainty of having his or her good name connected
with, perhaps, one of the dirtiest cases known to
police annals, speaks up for the sake of outraged
nature and reason, and “horrifies” Australia.
But too often the informant is one of the brood-
ing, unhealthy-minded ruck who speaks up only
from motives of envy or revenge.
We want light on these places. We have the
crime of the Dederers —the two brothers who killed
their father and burnt the body as they would
have burned a log, and yet seemed quite uncon-
scious of having done anything out of the common.
To those who know the conditions under which
many families like the Dederers exist in Australia,
their crime is neither inexplicable nor particularly
astounding. The Dederers, if I remember rightly,
were reported as never having been even to the
nearest “township” in their dark lives. No
doubt they were incapable of expressing, in any
sort of language, bush or otherwise, what they
felt; if, indeed, they felt anything.
Such dark ignorance is especially dangerous
because it is ape-like in its “ emotions,” in its likes
and dislikes. There are families in the bush with
the male members of whom an intelligent and ex-
perienced bushman would never trust himself
alone —if he had reason to be satisfied with the
natural shape of the back of his head; nor yet
with the female members —if he valued his neck
and the post mortem, memory of him. You might
be mates with a man in the bush for months, and
be under the impression that you are on the best of
terms with him, or even fancy that he has a de-
cided liking for you, and yet he might brood over
some fancied slight or injury—something you
have said or done, or haven’t said or done —any-
thing, in fact, that might suggest itself to an
ignorant, morose, and vindictive nature —until his
alleged mind is in such a diseased condition that
he is capable of turning on you any moment of
the day or night and doing you to death.
So the respectable farmer—too outspoken and
careless, perhaps, but good-hearted, and never
dreaming of the existence of an enemy—turning
to slumber again after the “ cock-crow ” hitch in
his sleep, hears a furtive whistle and the clatter of
retreating hoofs on slanting slip-rails, and thinks
it is some over-late or early neighbors passing
through ; but starts wide enough awake next time
to see the glare, sniff the smoke, and hear the
roar and crackle of fire, and, rushing out, white-
faced and with heart standing still, finds a shed or
stack —the stack of unthresned wheat, perhaps—
in flames. The crime of arson used to be very,
very common in Australia —and no “land laws”
or “ wrongs of Ireland ” to explain its prevalence.
Such malice is terrifying to those who have seen
what it is capable of. You never know when you
are safe, no matter how carefully you guard your
words, looks, or actions; and the only remedy—
for the application of which the law would
promptly hang you—would b* to sit up nights
with a gun with a chalked sight, until you got a
glimpse of your ape-minded and unprovoked
enemy, and thon carefully shoot him.
There are places iu Australia where the exist-
ance of the evil-eye and of witches is believed in ;
and where national, religious, and clan hatreds,
which perhaps have died out in the old-world
countries from which they came, are preserved in
their original intensity ; where is all the ignorant
suspicion and distrust of a half-savage peasantry.
The police, whose duty it is to collect returns for
harmless agricultural statistics, can tell you of the
difficulty they experience —and not in such out-of-
the-way localities either —and of the obstacles
thrown in their way when trying to obtain the
barest reliable information. “ Experienced great
difficulty in obtaining information from land-
holders ”; “ Declines to supply necessary informa-
tion”; “Still refuses,” &c., are common on the
margins and “remark” spaces of returned and
re-returned schedule-forms. Perhaps the cruellest
of all tbe bad sights of the bush is the case of the
child born to a family with which it has nothing
in common mentally (possibly physically)—the
“ throw-back ” to original and better stock —whose
bright mind is slowly but surely warped to mad-
ness by the conditions of life under which the
individual is expected to be contented and happy.
Such warped natures are often responsible for the
worst sexual crimes. There are brutally selfish
parents in the bush who regard and work their
children as slaves —and worse. Any experienced
bush school-teacher could bear me out in this, with
heartrending stories of child slavery and ill-treat-
ment almost past belief. I remember the case of
a boy who attended night-school with me for a
few months in the bush. His parents sent him
under pressure of “public opinion.” He had to
work from daylight until after dark, and do the
work of a man —or be starved and beaten to it.
He was 19, and an idiot. But some people said
that he was only an adopted son.
Democratic Maoriland, with its natural and
geographical advantages over Australia, is yet not
free from the dark spot I refer to. I have known
three white children at a Maori (native) school
who belonged to a family of (originally) seventeen
children. Two or three of the family were alleged
to be the children of the eldest unmarried daughter.
Of the three who attended school, two girls and a
boy, the boy was over fourteen; the girls eight and
nine. The boy was ignorant even of the existence
of an alphabet. He had the face of a weazened,
vicious little old man; and a good deal of the
nature. The girls’ faces were little masks of what
their mother’s might have been were she 20 or 30
years older. Both parents looked younger and
fresher than the children. Boy and girls rose at
daylight, cooked their parents’ breakfast (bacon,
eggs, &c.), carried it into them, had a meal of
bread and fat, and, when necessary, went into the
busb to cut and get together a load of firewood.
And the girls were eight and nine. The boy’s
physical development was naturally abnormal, but
his head didn’t seem to belong to his body. Sons
can be over-worked, starved, stunted mentally,
and otherwise cruelly treated to such an extent
that they are capable of turning upon and killing
a brutish parent—just as savage slaves will, when
they get tbe chance, kill their savage masters.
Then there is the unprovoked, unpremeditated,
passionless, and almost inexplicable bush murder,
when two mates have lived together in the bush
for years, until they can pass days and weeks
without exchanging a word, or noticing anything
unusual in the circumstance—till the shadow of
the over-hanging, brooding ridge, or the awful
monotony of the horizon less plain, deadens and
darkens the mind of one so that the very present*
of his mate, perhaps, becomes a constant source
of vague but haunting irritation. Then, one
day, being behind the other with an axe or an
adze to his hand, he suddenly, but dispassionately,
smashes his skull, and is afterwards utterly unable
to account for his action except by the muttered
explanations that he “had to do it,” or “ some-
thing made me do it.” Bush loneliness has the
same sort of influence on the blackfellow alone
with whites—as instant*' 1 the latest reported crime
committed by a blackfellow, who afterwards ex-
pressed sorrow for killing the “poor old man,”
but couldn’t understood why he did it—unless it
was because the white man, having stooped to
drink, was in “ such a good position for killing.”
Such crimes as those just instanced, and worse,
might be described as the ultimate result of a crav-
ing for variety— for something better or brighter,
perhaps, but, anyway, something different —the
protest of the outraged nature of the black or white
savage against the—to him —unnatural conditions.
Respectability only intensifies the awful mono-
tony of these wretched bush townships—till the
women are forced to watch for dirt and holes in a
neighbor’s washing hung out on the line, and men
to gossip and make mischief like women. Short-
comings in a neighbor are talked about and ex-
aggerated—and invented. Even a tragedy is
secretly welcomed —notwithstanding the fact that
the whole community is supposed, in double-
oolumn head-lines, to be horrified. Careless re-
marks are caught up, disturbed and magnified.
No respectable girl can leave the township on an
innocent visit without something discreditable
being discovered to be connected with her
departure from the wretched hole. City spielers
attach themselves to local pubs, and prey
with little or no disguise on idiotic cheque-men;
bush larrikins —who are becoming more con-
temptible and cowardly than their city prototypes
—openly boast of their “ successes,” and give the
girl’s name. And both classes are accepted as
oommonplace—the community never dreams of
giving them an hour’s start to get out of reach of
men, or stand the penalty.
Then there is the miserable bush feud which
arises (perhaps started generations ago—the
original cause forgotten) over a stray bull, a party
fence, a girl, a practical joke, a misunderstanding
or a fancied slight—anything or nothing ; and is
brooded over by men who have little else to think
about in the brooding bush. There is the threat
to “ pull yer ” and have satisfaction —the miser-
able court-case and cross-action brought on the
paltriest pretences that ever merited the disgust
of a magistrate—intensifying hatreds to a mur-
derous degree. And “friends” aid and abet and
fan the hell-fire in men’s hearts, till at last birth is
given to the spirit that sneaks out after dark and
cuts a neighbor’s wire-fences, or before daylight
and stands a gate ajar, or softly lets down the
rails that a neighbor’s own cattle may get into his
crop or garden, destroying the result of months of
weary toil and taking the food out of his children’s
mouths. The spirit that shoots or hamstrings
horses grazing under the star-light; that sets a
match to stack or shed.
Mischief breeds mischief ; malice, malice ; and
the tongues of the local hags applaud and chorus,
and damn and exaggerate and lie, until the
wretched hole is ripe for a “horror.” Then the
Horror comes.

THE KIDNEYS.
WHAT THEY ARE FOR.
The duty of the Kidneys is to take the poisonous
waste from the blood. Every drop of blood passes through
the body several times a day. When healthy, the kidneys
remove about 500 grains of impure matter daily. When
unhealthy, some share of this is left to poison the blood.
The whole train of kidney diseases results from poison-
laden blood. Kidney complaints result from the over-
work of the kidneys. Kidney diseases show themselves
by backache, unusual desire to urinate, greasy froth in
water, red and white brick-dust in water, fluttering and
pains in the heart, tired fe - i ig, abundance of pale or
scanty How of dark-colored urine, waxy skin, pulled eye-
lids, dizziness, dropsical swellings, and soreness about the
small of back and over the kidneys.
The symptoms given above are the earlier ones. Most
kidney diseases will run into Bright’s disease if neglected.
All kid i‘V diseases are dangerous, and call for immediate
care. The earlier the warning is heeded, the most certain
the cure. There comes a time, if kidney disease is
neglected, when the tissues of the kidneys becotne dis-
eased. That condition is beyond human remedy. It is
not within the province of medicine to replace the diseased
tissue. All that Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills can do is
to relieve the kidneys before that condition comes, and set
them working right again.

The Widow.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A widow lived in Wcxford-street,
Of vice aud opium reeking.
Long hours she spent in doing work,
And longer still in seeking.
Yet still at dawn and dark, eon' nt,
Perused her well-worn Testament.
To woman’s lot these latter days,
Proud sir and honored madam,
Have added to the curse of Eve
The further curse of Adam.
Three girls the widow’s hearthstone knew ;
Eve’s curse took one, the other two.
The youngest fled from mastering toil,
From sickening life inhuman,
For money leads the man astray,
And want of it the woman.
Yet still less closely followed her
The luster than the procurer.
The other twain died sinless deaths.
And sinless, too, this other
Who walked the weary streets at last
For bread to feed her mother,
Yet died with hope unsatisfied.
“Streets, streets in Heaven,” she moaned aud
died.
The widow laid the Book aside
That spoke of love and glory,
Of palms and pavements formed of gold,
Long famed in Bible story.
She wept no more till break <>f day ;
The devil wiped her tears away.
”Twas day. The busy court was filled,
The dusty air was reeking ;
Outside the tread of heavy feet,
Indoors the raucous speaking.
When by the crowded railings went
A white-haired woman, old and bout—
Stole by the dusty parajiet,
Past constable and warder,
Up to the empty witness-stand.
The officer cried “ Order ! ”
“ What brings you here ? ” the justice cried. -
“ Tis breach of promise,” she replied.
A titter round the listeners ran,
Her withered figure eyeing ;
Trembling, she raised the sacred Book
Beside her elbow lying,
And laid the quivering hand of age
Upon the worn and open page.
“ I call,” she said, with straining breath,
“On God, the great Defendant,
Who vowed the widow s friend to be
When on His grace dejiendent—
A husband to the widow-, yes,
And father to the fatherless.
“H is letters—here they lie,” she shrieked,
“ Through weary years respected,
For help withheld, for faith forsworn,
For promises neglected.
I cite Him to appear before —
High God — ” Her laughing is not o’er.
Sydney.
CONSTANCE CLYDE.
The “Dull Sickening Thud.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I havk heard itl It is the only one of the ghastly
conventions of the scaffold that I found there.
The firm step, the flickering sunbeam, the
strangling gasp, the noiseless gliding of the hang-
man in his stockinged feet, and his disguise—all
were absent, and only the Dull Sickening Thud
remained.
What most of all strikes the man who first sees
an execution is the utter absence of ceremony
about the whole affair. It is just as if somebody
had said, “Take him out and hang him” ; and,
when you come to think of it, that is exactly what
does occur. Still, in the executions of history and
of fiction, there is something impressive in the very
act itself, apart altogether from the idea that a
human being is deliberately killed by other
human beings. The executions of Charles 1.,
Marie Antoinette, Sydney Carton, Robespierre,
thrilled as we read them ; not, perhaps, altogether
by reason of our sympathies with the condemned,
but because there seemed to be something almost
supernatural about the man who is standing mid-
way between life and death ; and when, as boys,
we read of Robert Emmett and Andrd swinging
in mid-air, the incidents seemed to be invested
with a glamor that ate right into us.
What of the real thing which I saw the other
day for the first time? It was little more im-
pressive than the swinging of a bag of flour from
the hoist of a warehouse. As far as dramatic
effect was concerned, it was utterly destitute of
the quality ; the pithing of a bullock at a slaugh-
ter-house was ten times as strong an incident.
There were ten of us in the lodge at the gaol-
gat*-—four pressmen (one with a record of fourteen
Jirevious hangings); two justices of the peace (one
at, bearded, and prosperous; the other pale,
nervous, and silent—eloquent of the fact that he
was sorry he had come). A suburban hairdresser,
and a coffee-palace manager, the only two absolute
outsiders, were in the same condition. While in
th** yard a detective and a sub- nspector of jioliee
talked of the case beneath their breath. Every-
body, even the warder who collected our admis-
sion -tickets, Rixike underbreath—why, they did not
know themselves. . It could not have been for fear
of disturbing the condemned in his cell, for he
was far away, with three thick stone »alls
between.
Suddenly the warder asked one of the pressmen
the time. “ Five minutes to ten.” The warder
left his seat at the door and said to the scribe,
“You’d Vtetter come along.” The rest of the
waiting crowd rose to follow. The eleven piced
along a court into a building, up a few steps,
through a door of iron bars, and saw before them
an octagonal balcony, »ith a dozen warders, silent
and at attention, seal tered around it. The press-
men and the bearded J.P. knew that above them,
in that clear space where no warders stood, Death
was waiting confident and satisfied, but the rest
hesitated, stopped, and were about to walk further
%vhen the senior warder motioned upwards with
his hand. Our eves rose with it, and, cutting
down to the high balcony in front, running at an
angle from a low beam, we saw a thin straight line
—the rope. We had expected something so differ-
’cut—something like the pictures we had seen of
Tyburn tree perhaps; possibly a single tall post
with an arm and an angle-strut. Gallows 4t)ft.
high, Carlyle wrote about, and here was a beam
running scarcely two feetover the head of the man
it was to hang. No wonder the rope escaped our
notice. It was so short, so straight, so still ;
it did not dangle at all. The two visitors
seemed to think they had been cheated
in some way and that 4'tiis violation of
the canons of ’hanging was disgraceful. They
could see hardly anything of the man who
was to die and to see him at all they needed to
crane their necks and craning their necks made
matters feel too realistic We began to wonder
which cell it was There was a door on each side—
both equally guarded by warders-and there was
nothing to differentiate the sides except the legend
“Cook-house lift” to the right, and that was
foolishly irrelevant
The iron gate rkttled behind, and we turned
together to sfe three men in black suits with tail-
coats-a sort of uniform that was not a uniform,
They trooped in in singlefile, and while we wondered
who they were and why the senior warder didn’t
stop them, they walked through us, turned and
strode up the iron stairs. In a moment we could
see them again. They walked slowly and silently
around three sides of the octagon, and then at the
door on the left the leader stopped and said lie
“ demanded the body.” That was another sur-
prise. Had we not heard his words we would have
thought he was remarking that there was a nice,
cool change in the weather. We had almost
thought he would be preceded by a brace of heralds,
and wo certainly expected him to make the demand
in strong, impressive tones, such as the Judge’s
Associate uses when he asks a prisoner, “ Are you
guilty or not guilty ? ” But we had no time to
marvel. No sooner was the demand voiced than
there was a clatter of feet. A portly, red-faced
man dashed across the open space ; at his heels, a
younger man, pale, lean, gaunt, and spectacled,
Both wore heavy boots that clattered loudly on
the bare boards. Then arose another voice—a
strange voice that no one recognised but all knew
when it bettan “ May Jesus bless—.” The clergy-
man came, from the door and the hangman and his
spectacled assistant drew out a small white figure
between them, almost in their arms.
The figure seemed to have less to do with
the affair than anybody else. It seemed
so utterly insignificant and left the impres-
sion that - we could have got on with the exe-
cution just as well without it. Still we looked
at it with its head on a level with the chests of the
big warders on the platform, and we marvelled
how such an insignificant object could have all the
fuss made about it that there was outside. Here
before ns there was no fuss at all. It was just as
if we had come to see several men walk across a
fl or. There was nothing more thrilling or more
impressive up to the present. The grandeur of
the Robert Emmett and Sydney Carton business
we could still understand, but that anybody's
heart should beat the quicker because of the
trumpery affair being enacted before our eyes
appeared absurd. The figure wore a pair of white
trousers, a light shirt, and a face of the same color,
Its neck was scrawny, thin and long—ominously
long—and its arms were fastened behind its back
by an ordinary leather strap. The wonderful
pinion we had read about was nothing but
a strap, and the legs were left unfastened,
He could not have walked over that space
unassisted—that was certain—and the hang-
man and his assistant hustled him across the
few feet and stood him in the centre, while the
parson took up a position in front. The parson
was the only bit of romance about; his robes
almoxt made the function impressive. But the
parson started to improve on the Bible, and the
impressiveness went out of him. That was the
finishing touch, and the execution, which might
have been redeemed a little from the common-
place, relapsed further than ever. The figure,
still supported by the hangman and his mate,
was asked if it had anything to say. It had,
and, to the surprise of all, said it—short though it
was—in a voice so strong and clear that the
prisoners in the cells along the corridors must have
heard. “That Heavenly Kingdom,” he con-
cluded, slowly and deliberately, his voice rising in
a powerful diapason, “in which lam now pre-
pared to meet nay Hod.” There was another and
a quicker clatter as the hangman let go his arm
and dashed across the floor to the right, pitching
against the lever with his full force and sending it
back in a semi-circle. The yellow oblong of the
trap pushed out from below the floor-—then the
figure dropped. It was just like the dropping of
any other object. That it was a human Vicing
did not make the difference we had expected. The
figure shot down and stopped suddenly—so sud-
denly that we were almost surprised, and for the
first time I heard “the dull, sickening thud.”
Dull, it certainly was, but not sickening. Just
one soft concussion, and then silence. The leg of
the figure shook itself at us for a moment, and
forced upon our notice the broad arrow and a big
black “ 25 ” on the thigh, and after that there was
stillness. The man didn't seem to swing on the
end of the string like the man that Lord Tom-
noddy didn't see hanged. He fell straight, and
neither turned nor oscillated the fraction of an
inch that could be observed. “ Rest in peace ! ”
said the parson, but nobody answered him.
After a second or two, the senior-warder stepped
out and unbooked a cord, and a canvas blind, old,
ill-fitting and dirty, rolled down and hid the
motionless figure from our view. We stood look-
ing at it without knowing why, and, beneath the
roller at the bottom of the blind, we saw on the
stone pavement a dark patch that slowly swa ved
right and left. The shadow of the dead. The
figure must be moving, though it had appeared
still as the walls around before the curtain fell.
We watched that dark patch waver and dance, as
the crowd slowly melted from the balcony and
stood among us —all but he who remained beside
the rope that was his tool of trade.
Then the sheriff tapped us on the shoulder, and
we awoke and walked out. The pressman who
had told the warder the time looked at his watch
again. Only eight minutes over the whole trans-
action ! Outside the gaol, the kerbstones were
lined with men and women who had gathered
around to stare at the gaol-walls and allow their
own imaginations to thrill them as the actual
sight of the tragedy just enacted would have
utterly failed to do.

“Go and Tell tlie Olliers.”
Thkuk were eleven of us in all, four women aiul
seven men. We were passengers on board tlk:
sailing ship Black Squall, 909 tons, bound from
Loudon toCaleuUi. lhecnw had mutinied and
marooned us on Amsterdam island - a great,
bleak rock in tin* Indian Ocean. Ten days passed.
We had con-mined most of our provisions and all
our water. Thus far wo bad vainly sought water
on the island. No rain from the heavens, no sail
on the sea! Our tongues were cracking like up-
lands in a drought. You have read of the tor-
tures of thirst. May fortune confine your know-
ledge to that.
One more attempt; the last. Two of the men—
the least feeble - started. Hours went by, hours
thatsmote like li runners and burned like clots of
molten lead. Exhausted and despairing the men
lighted upon a spring half hidden under a remote
cliff. Each took a few swallows. Then one of
them fell fainting on the ground, just able to say
to his companion, “ Go—yo tell the others /”
People say that suffering is the mother of selfish-
ness. Often it is so; not always, thank God.
Anyway, the sense of relief ever glows with grati-
tude and pity. Head the following—a woman’s
story—and you will see why the two are paired in
my mind.
“From my childhood I have been subject to
headache and dizziness. At the ago of seventeen
I was very weak and ill. My tongue was furred,
my appetite poor. My food seemed to give me no
strength ; I was always tired. My hands and
feet were cold and clammy, and my skin of a
creamy hue. One day, whilst in service at Mrs.
Firth’s, Park Farm, Thornhill, 1 visited my home.
On arriving there I had great pain and fluttering
at the heart; I could scarcely stand. It so
frightened iny mother that she got some neigh-
bours to assist me to the doctor’s. He said, * Your
heart is in a shocking state.’
“ He gave me medicines, but I was not relieved
by them. Getting worse, I gave up my situation,
being too ill to leave home. Soon I was seized
with a nasty cough and irritation of the throat
and chest; I could got no sleep on account of it.
I would sit up in bed until daybreak coughing and
spitting, and was, of course, worse tired than
when I went to bed. My legs trembled so that I
could not stand or walk much ; I was not able to
wash or dress myself. In this condition I re-
mained month after month, growing weaker
daily. On one occasion I was so bad that mother
thought 1 was dying, and fetched Mrs. Senior, a
neighbor, saying, ‘ l)o come and help me.’
“In January, 1890, 1 went to the Dewsbury
Infirmary, where I was under treatment for six
weeks. The doctors gave me iron mixture and
other tonics, but they bad no effect. Other young
girls were there, being doctored for ailments like
mine, to as little purpose as far as I could see.
“I next tried change of air, and went to Hali-
fax, also to Jlatley, without benefit. I continued
to waste away, and people said I would never re-
cover. In May, 1891, I first read of Mother
Seigel’s Syrup, in a book that was left at our
house.
“Mother got me a bottle from Mr. J. Day, the
chemist, Thornhill Lees, and 1 began taking it.
After taking only a few doses I found a little
relief, the heaviness of my chest and the pain
at tny heart were easier, and my food digested
and agreed with me. 1 now grew stronger
every day, and was soon able to go back
to my work. But I kept on taking the
Syrup stiU, and, to the surprise of every
one, the color came to my cheeks, and I have since
enjoyed the best of health. All my family were
delighted, as you may well believe. (Signed)
Miss Hannah Milnes, 18 Walker’s Buildings,
Brewery Lane, Thornhill Lees, Dewsbury, Oc-
tober 12th, 1892.
“P.S. — Jf ice could have afforded it, we should
have published all the particulars of my case in the
newspapers, that others miyht know where to look
for a remedy. —ll.M.”
There! that is it. She wanted to tell the
others. And by printing her story we help her to
tell them where to find a cure for indigestion and
dyspepsia and its dangerous consequences when
all hope seems to have faded away.
